Post-mortem Fiction
When in August, IDI4, old Europe sud-
denly started smashing up the furniture of
civilisation, one of the minor articles that
came in the way of the old gentleman’s axe
was literature. And the makers of novels
found themselves left wondering in a world
that no longer required their goods. Even
in the midst of the universal frenzy Europe
wanted novels, but not the sort of novels
that their novelists had been accustomed
annually to turn out. The end of one world
had come, and out of the ruins of that epoch
a new world was beginning to arise. And
all that the novelists could write about was
a world that had been destroyed by shrapnel
and high explosives. What the new world
needed was information, in the guise of
fiction, of what that new world was like,
or what it would be like, what changes it
was making-in- the mankind that had to in-
habit that new world. And this is just
what the professional novelists, with the
possible exception of H. G. Wells, were con-
genitally incapable of doing. (And even
Wells failed his readers. Instead of an
analysis of our present state, for which
Wells’ frequent incursions into futurity had
specially qualified him, he gave his eager
readers Bealhy, a week-end novel; Boon, a
merely delightful satire on his literary con-
temporaries—dead men, too, amid the ruins
of their shattered conventions; and The
Research Magnificent, in which he took his
hero a trip through the Balkans at peace—
the Balkans now utterly smashed into un-
recognisable fragments !)
But, luckily for The professional novelists,
who have to make a living, war or no war,
their public was just as slow in discovering
that a civilisation had been counted out.
So the dazed novelists, after a momentary
effort to swing their thoughts from the past
to the present, went back to their old ante-
bellum job; and the bewildered public
turned from the horror of the present to
pictures of a peaceful, static civilisation
wherein no thought of war intruded—and
the novelists found their circulations once
more improving. But such fiction is post-
mortem fiction, an autopsy on the dead body
of an obliterated epoch in history. Into
our veins the war has been injected, and
we shall never be the Same again, never
recapture the placid content and the futile
revqlts of that civilisation that died so
dramatically in August. 1014.
So here are two excellent examples of
post-mortem fiction. Alice and a Family, by
St. John G. Irvine (Bell) ; and The Free-
lands, by John Galsworthy (Heinemann,
both per Dymock’s Library).
Alice and a Family, by St. John G.
Irvine, belongs to Wells’s type of weary
giant fiction. This delightful study of the
cockney is designed solely to amuse the tired
business man and his weary wife. It is
merely the story of a London kid of 15 who
takes in hand a shiftless family named
Nudds. and knocks some sense into them.
Alice has a sharp tongue, managing ways,
and an extraordinary amount of tact. The
whole Nudds family, regarded socially, and
not with what Alice’s mother calls
“yoomer,” is a horrible indictment of the
alleged civilisation of prosperous England—
or what was prosperous England, for there
is no hint of the coming war. If this Mr.
Nudds is the British workman it was quite
time that Wilhelm shook him out of his
shiftlessness. There must be quite a lot of
Nuddses in England, and Wilhelm knew it.
But the author has no other intention than
to amuse; he does it honestly, and his
characters are real people. That is the
trouble in these troublous times: there are
too many Nuddses, and they are too real.
One fancies, and one hopes, that so capable
and fine a craftsman as St. John G. Irvine
will not write any more Nuddsian romances,
because there won’t be any more Nuddses
to write about. ’Erb is sure to enlist, and
he won’t be a Nudd when he returns.
John Galsworthy, in The Freelands , has
not even heard of the war. His novel belongs
to the family of The Man of Property and
The Country House: it might have been writ-
ten at the same time as those masterpieces
in irony. He takes a family of four brothers
—the author, the manufacturer, the bureau-
crat and the small land-owner —in order to
have four divergent view-points in his elab-
orate analysis of the land question in Eng-
land. Yes, that is what John Galsworthy,
in this year of disgrace, is worrying his punc-
tilious soul over. The land question in an
England that exists no longer, that strange,
quiet, beautiful, green English countryside
that has been so swiftly submerged in khaki.
And, with the whole of civilisation in the
melting-pot, Galsworthy is analysing a case
of arson upon a land-owner’s estate.
His characters are as out-of-date as the
Nuddses. “Of course,” one character is
allowed to interject, “we might have a Euro-
pean war or something that would shake
everything up.” But Galsworthy sheers
quickly off that possibility—it would destroy
his neatly-constructed story. So he regales
his accustomed readers with a picture of
what was, never suggesting that it will never
be so again. And he takes the trouble to
show, us, with the most delightful irony, all
the sides of the now-forgotten question of the
depopulation of the land of England and the
hapless plight of the English ploughman.
But. the English ploughman has become a
soldier, and when he comes back from the
war he will settle the land question. There
is little likelihood that the enlisted plough-
man will come back to follow the plough; he
will not be the sort of peasant to put up
docilely with w r hat the land-owners think best
for him. When peace comes he will not beat
his sword into a ploughshare: he is more
likely to beat it into a share of the English
soil that he has helped to save from destruc-
tion.
So this page follows with impatience a
tllat is full of quiet English beauty,
that is exquisitely written, that is firm and
beautiful in texture, and through which runs
a delicate thread of delicious irony. These
loving descriptions of the beauty of the coun-
tryside, these charming pictures of skv and
tree and blossom, these evocations of the
scents and the songs of idyllic England
sound to us like a faint memory that escapes
recapture. All that has gone; all that may
never return.
However, there will still be prisons after
the war ; and Galsworthy makes his reader
feel what a prison is like with the power
that lie showed in the “solitary cell” scene in
Justice. Nedda goes to see a laborer in
prison
“Then she became conscious of a shaven-
headed. noiseless being in drab-grey clothes,
on hands and knees, scrubbing the end of a
corridor. Her tremor at the stealthy ugli-
ness of this crouching figure yielded at once
to a spasm of. pity. The man gave her a
look., furtive, yet so charged with intense
penetrating curiosity that it seemed to let her
suddenly into innumerable secrets. She felt
as if the whole life of people shut away in
silence and solitude were disclosed to her in
the swift, unutterably alive look of this
noiseless, kneeling creature, riving out of
her something to feed his soul and body on.
That look seemed to lick its lips. It made
her angry, made her miserable, with a feel-
ing of pity she could hardly bear Poor
man ! How he must hate her, who was free,
and all fresh from the open world and the
sun, and people to love and talk to! The
poor _ man’ scrubbed on steadily, his ears
standing out from his shaven head; then,
dragging his knee-mat skew-ways, he took
the chance to look at her again. Perhaps
because his dress and cap • and stubble of
hair were so drab grey, those little, dark
eyes seemed to her the most terribly living
things she had ever seen. She felt that tliev
had taken her in from top to toe, clothed and
unclothed, taken in the resentment she had
felt, and the pity she was feeling; they
seemed nt once to appeal, to attack, and to
possess her _ ravenously, as though all the
starved instincts in a whole prisoned world
had rushed up and for a second stood out-
side their bars. Then came the clank of
keys, the eyes left her as swiftly as they had
seized her, and he became again just that
stealthy, noiseless creature scrubbing a stone
floor.”
The end of the story is the defeat of the
young rebels that had tried to stir up the
English peasant to make the land-owner
ameliorate his pitiable lot. And though this
page. commends Galsworthy on his sense in
sending his hero and heroine out to Maori-
land, it is pained to learn that these two
fine people found in Maoriland a “ranche.”
The Red Page
The War
Dr. A. A. Martin, senior surgeon of
Palmerston North Hospital (M.L.), volun-
teered as a surgeon with the British Expedi-
tionary Army in France ; and his experiences,
as narrated in A Surgeon in Khaki (Arnold,
10s. 6d.), make a highly interesting contri-
bution to genuine war literature. lie saw
much active service in the early excit-
ing days of the German thrust at Paris and
Calais; his style is fluent and vivid; his
vision is keen, and his comments and eom-
.plaints acute and accurate. Everywhere in
those tense days he noted muddle and in-
competence. His description of the'-sudden
shifting of the British base at Le Havre
to safer quarters further south, owing to
the threat from the armies of the Hun,
shows once again the capacity of the British
for muddling through.,
“The French charge magnificently with
the bayonet, but they charge in a state of
tremendous excitement. When rushing
across an open space they shout and scream.
... .On they go, an impetuous and irre-
sistible avalanche of steel, yelling, stab-
bing, slaying, overwhelming The British
charge stoically and more grimly. They do
not shout. I have heard them cursing. The
British line advances as a sinister cold line
of steel, in a sort of jog-trot. It is a line of
cool-brained gladiators, alert of eye and
thoroughly bent on slaughter. Our Briton
sees his foe. and smites savagely with the
calculating judgment of a good Rugby for-
ward and with the bound of a wild cat....
The German, in spite of what is often, said
to the contrary, is a brave and determined
man with the bayonet They charge well,
advancing with a stooping, jerky trot, utter-
ing hoarse guttural cries and ‘Hurrahs.’ On
they come, in solid masses shoulder to
shoulder.... When in a solid compact pha-
lanx this German charge is very dangerous
and formidable, and has been able, although
at a frightful cost, to brush aside and over-
whelm veteran British and French troops.
But if this solid column is broken.... the
sense of cohesion or ‘shoulder to shoulder’
is lost, and the heavy column is then no
match for the lightning bayonet onfall of
the French infantry or the weighty heave
forward of a British regiment.”
Among the many mistakes made by the
British authorities was the failure at first
to provide motor ambulances. It was due
to Surgeon Martin’s efforts that this neglect
was remedied. His criticism of British Army
Medical methods, chiefly due to a tenacious
adherence to out-of-date traditions, is at
times necessarily sharp; but he makes some
very sensible suggestions for efficiency.
All through his experiences at the Aisne
and the Marne, the author refers to the
unclean conditions in which he had to
operate. “The whole British Arm'y at the
Front was lousy.” And he classes lice with
shrapnel bombs and high explosives. He is
interesting when discussing gas gangrene,
“one of the terrors of the doctors at this
tune. Gas gangrene has nothing to do
with gas-poisoning. "It was a new and
totally, unexpected complication of the
wounds, and at first we did not know what
to do in the face of this pressing danger....
Me had many deaths, and performed many
amputations to save lives.” Gas gangrene
is caused by a group of bacilli, amongst
which may be many organisms, sometimes
as many as 10, all belonging to the same
family. They are all spore-bearing, and
grow in the absence of air. These bacilli
are found in the soil of France and Bel-
gium. and are always to be found in soil
that has been closely cultivated for centuries
past. When a man is wounded in the
trenches instinctively he puts his hand to
the wound, and thus introduces the organ-
isms with which his dirty hand is infected.
As the organism spreads tip the limb it pro-
duces a gas of its own. If the case reaches
the doctor early’ it can be cured; if late,
when the limbs are dead, amputation is
necessary. Tetanus was another scourge;
but now anti-tetanic serum is given to every
wounded man, and tetanus cannot develop.
He tells a delightful story of a truculent
I russian officer. When captured, he still
had his revolver in his belt. The English
had forgotten to take it,, and he had"for-
gotten that it was there. The Prussian said
that he was disgraced -by being made a
prisoner, and lie wished he had been shot.
An English officer went up to the snarling
prisoner, and pointing out that he still had
his loaded revolver in his belt politely sug-
gested to him that even now lie was at
perfect liberty to go and “blow his damned
head off.” The Prussian's eyes blazed with
fury, but he made no movement, and handed
over his revolver to the English officer, who
refused to touch it, and called on a soldier
to take it.
The author found many first-hand evi-
dences of Hun atrocities, and lie does not
hesitate to discuss the worst of these. “It
is a subject which the women of France will
discuss freely and openly, and with a con-
centrated bitterness that one can readily
understand. The subject is that of the
women who have heeu ravished and outraged
by the German soldiery. Many of these vic-
tims, married women and young girls, are. to-
day pregnant to German fathers, and the
burning question with the women of France
is how best to help their unfortunate
sisters, and what is to be done for the off-
spring. In the French Chamber of Deputies
the subject has been debated with equal
freedom and bitterness. Leading French
newspapers, too, have envisaged the position
in powerful and appealing articles.” The
Academy of Medicine, as well as the
Catholic Church, condemned abortion, the
former pointing out that the mother’s life
might be endangered. “Arrangements have
now been completed for the reception of
these pitiful expectant mothers into mater-
nity homes, where they will be attended at
the State expense. After birth the child is
to lie brought up by the State at some place
undeclared. The mother will not see the
child at any time, and will know nothing of
its future....No wonder that the French
woman speaks of the German soldier as a
loathly thing.”
By the way, it is to kill these beasts that
Australia is calling for another 50.000
recruits.
A*
The Achievement of France (Methuen;
Is.) is welcome. These reprinted articles
from London Times do the fair 'thing to
our closest ally, and stress the sometimes
undervalued fact that upon France, has
fallen the main burden of the war; and they
help us to understand how bravely and how
efficiently France has done her duty. The
story of the war up to the end of June is
precise and lucid, an ordered narrative that
puts the salient features in their true per-
spective. And, with an equal fairness, the
author indicates representative French
opinion of England’s co-operation in the
great campaign. Such books as these are
not necessary to counteract the Hun’s efforts
to breed dissension between the Allies; but
they are as cheering as a hearty handshake.
What is the Best Australasian
Short-story?
This competition has, so far, not attracted
much interest. A guinea is offered to the
competitor who sends in a list of six, or
less, titles of short-stories written and pub-
lished in Australasia, that most nearly cor-
responds to the total number of votes re-
ceived. Judging by the lists already
received, there is no story sufficiently well
known to be named by more than two com-
petitors ; and without a representative num-
ber of competitors the value of the competi-
tion will be nil. Under these circumstances,
the date of the closing of the competition is
extended to December 17.

novels include A. G. Hales’ McGlusky the
Reformer, and Ethel M. Dell’s The Way of
an Eagle.
The Forgotten Territory
It is a significant fact that when Elsie It.
Masson set out to describe An Untamed Ter-
ritory (Macmillan, 65.), she should appear,
to Australian readers, an explorer in un-
known country. That wedge that strikes
down to the heart of the continent from the
north is to most of us as remote as Asia.
“The Australian who visits the Territory is
surprised and strangely entranced with this
portion of his continent.” And the Austra-
lian reader will be as surprised and as
strangely entranced by this delightful book.
This poor relation of ours dwells right at
the continent’s front door; but it is only
occasionally that any of us sets out to visit
her. Elsie R. Masson paid her wonderful
visit, and brings back good news.
The history of this lost region is one of
settlement after settlement bravely begun
and abandoned, until at length the Territory
was tethered to the rest of the continent by
a strand of wire. But the rail's that should
follow the straight, faint track of that tele-
graph wire have been lost sight of in the
much more important quarrels of our poli-
ticians. Half a million square miles have
been lost or lqislaid in the map of Australia
—“moist tropical landscape watered by rivers
flowing between banks of jungle; rich pas-
toral country ; ridged land and sandy desert.”
It is separated from the other States by a
fortnight’s journey by sea. It is nearer
to Hong Kong than it is to Sydney. It is
rich in minerals, yet the mining has petered
out; it is suitable for sugar-growing, yet the
one attempt to grow sugar has failed. “Twice
a week ever since the Overland Railway laid
its last rail a train has run from Port Dar-
win to Fine Creek; once a fortnight ever
since a train has run from Adelaide to Ood-
nadatta. There they have faced each other
over the thousand intervening miles, and
then turned tail and gone back again.” So,
to the lasting disgrace of jealous States-
frighters, those who go to the forgotten Ter-
ritory “journey 60 years into the past, into
an Australia which has long passed away in
the south, but which still lingers in the wild,
intractable Northern Territory.”
The author’s second chanter disarms the
reader. It is entitled “A Woman’s Life in
Darwin.” The end of her first year found
the author undaunted. During that year
she had experienced some discomforts and
many small worries; and she had had mo-
ments of home-sickness; but that was all.
And she is not discouraged over the servant
question. But it is in her “out-bush” chap-
ters that Elsie It. Masson has her chief dis-
coveries to record. Again and again she
speaks of the almost “unbearable cold” of
the nights when camping out. And she leads
the reader into strange places—a buffalo-
hunter’s lonely but beautiful home on the
Alligator River, the ruins of Port Essington,
where a settlement was made by a detach-
ment of marines in 183,8 —and abandoned ;
an interesting trip upon the Roper River.
These journeys she describes with a delight-
ful spirit and a vivid style that enables the
reader to visualise the exotic charm of this
neglected portion of our national heritage.
And there is a faithful account of a murder-
trial of Australian blacks that enables us to
guess the strange world in which the mind
of the aboriginal still lives; but the White
Man’s justice seems somewhat of a misfit for
the dark criminal. When the murderer of
another black is convicted: “Then follows a
term at Fanny Bay gaol. Here he enjoys
unheard-of luxuries—good tucker, tobacco, a
stretcher, and a mosquito-net. During the
day he works at the Government Gardens.
After release, the prisoner, no longer a myall
but well-trained, disciplined and in the pink
of physical condition, may choose to work in
town, or he may return as a hero to his own
country, where he assumes great . airs of
superiority and is much respected by those
of his tribe who have not yet been ‘ longa
Fanny Bay.’ ”
The note of enthusiasm is this book’s
best recommendation. Elsie R. Masson
looks forward to a populous Territory, with
the last of the Asiatics dead or returned to
their homes, and the flavor of the East de-
parted. The settler in the Territory, man
or woman, does not need heroism on a large
scale, but bravery in small things, courage
to take reverses cheerfully. The man must
be the sort that will tacile each task as it
comes along without stopping to ask himself
if this is what he came to do; the woman
must be the sort who will adapt herself to
new conditions, endure hardship without
grumbling, and without looking back con-
stantly on the comforts she has left behind.
“Those who are not prepared for a struggle
need not come to the Northern Territory...
Theirs will be the task of breaking in the
bush and forcing the country to yield them
wchat it can—a home, a livelihood, a pic-
turesque. adventurous life, and the prospect
of a rich future.”

“Barcoo” on Flapperature :
, As a man of the world always on the roam, and
living continually in hotels, I advise “Jack Spur”
(.13. 11/11/T5) to interview the Sydney or Mel-
bourne library, when lie will be informed that the
latest Garbage is besieged on arrival; and if he
attends himself he will And that 65 per cent, are
composed of “Miss Shortskirts,” the balance being
mature maidens of uncertain age, sprinkled with a
few dames of 40 with peculiar sparkling eyes. Nat
Gould and his editions in Australian circulating
libraries are rare. In England he is the author,
judging by his sales on railway stations. Here he
is never asked for. Anyhow, what does it matter
if the flapper reads Garbage at 16? If she has
brains she won’t be reading milk-and-water fiction
when she puts up ber hair.

in terrible danger of deadly kidney disease.
This insidious disease creeps on, gradu-

when it is too late the poor sufferer discovers
he has Bright’s Disease. Have you kidney
trouble? Do you know how to recognise
whether you are suffering from this or not?
Here are some of the symptoms of kidney
disease: Aches and pains in various parts of
the body, nasty taste in the mouth first thing
in the morning, constipation, cloudy and bad-
colored urine, general feeling of weakness
and irritability, pufliness under the eyes—
these are all symptoms of kidney trouble.
And then people who suffer from backache,
rheumatism, gout, sciatica, gravel, stone or
pains in the bladder, muscles and joints are
all suffering these pains through weak or
diseased kidneys, for these are the sure and
certain symptoms of kidney trouble.
All these things mean that the kidneys are
weak and diseased. They need strengthening
and making healthy.

VARIOUS VERSE
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
A Vignette.
A red and gibbous moon slinks down the sky,
The searchlight’s lances probe the mottled
green,
A lean destroyer, throbbing, hisses by,
Laying long trails of foam across the sheen.
A burning village smoulders, and a tower
Of smoke blots out the battle-weary stars ;
Far off a ship’s bell clanks the passing hour.
The weary horses stamp and chafe their bars.
- NPS-'
Now trembling Dawn creeps pale above the
peak,
A Maxim chatters, and the great ships near
From iron throats with tongues of flame
outspeak,
Filling the morn with thunder and with fear.
TIIE DARDANELLES.
JACK SOMMERS.
The Little Lute.
You have unslung your little lute,
The little lute you kept for me ;
Past reedy trail and knotted root
Your feet went swinging gallantly;
All that you left of love or lute
Is one unchanging melody.
Its plaintive ghost cries everywhere
Where lies the beanfield in the sun.
The shy leaves curling from the glare,
The flowers fading everyone;
And where the summer dust falls soft
upon the ditch edge from the trail,
Or amber hay piles in the loft,
Or brown stalk flattens to the flail,
I hear it —-in the groaning wheel
Or axle crying on the turn,
And in the mill among the meal,
Or where the furnace fires burn ;
1 hear it in the rushes’ song
Or in a meadow’s daisied dream ;
But, most of all, the whole day long.
It calls me in the shallow stream.
The yellow waters soaked with sun,
Banks buttoned green in cresses cool —
On by the ruffled race they run,
And out into the Silent Pool.
I think at midnight, if I went
When all the pool is blind with mist.
Down where the jealous leaves are bent
Above a petal’s amethyst—-
If, fearless-hearted, I should dare
A stairway of the lily’s root.
A blest white sleen might find me there
Unbroken by the little lute !
QUEENSLAND.
M. FORREST.
“From (censored).”
I don’t suppose yer’ll git this letter, .Tim,
But, if yer does, tell Sis I ain’t forgot.
I might be down an’ out before yer does
(Lor’! Listen to them shrapnel, ’ow they
buzz!).
It’s sundown an’ the light’s a-growin’ dim.
It’s ’ard to see —so please excuse the blot.
It’s rummy-like up in the trenches here.
Yer thinks of things yer’d never think of
’ome.
I used to reckon girls was made for play—
Sis knoo w’at I was thinkin’, anyway.
It didn’t suit ’er bettin’-book, no fear!
Well., once ’t I’m back with ’er —no more
to roam!
So tell ’er as I’ve tried to do me bit.
An’, if the blighted Turks don’t do me in,
I reckon w’en I’m back in old Prahran
She’ll find as I’m a better kind o’ man
Than ’im she ’anded out the frozen mit.
So long—old chap, yours truly,
SOUTH AUSTRALIA.
MICKO FLYNN.
On Behalf of Youth.
Make your parting now,
Youth will have its way.
Youth will laugh and ride away
Though the untilled earth be waiting for the
plough.
Spare your pleadings old,
What if harvests rot!
Corn will thrive when youth is not,
And there’s time for labor when the blood
is cold ;
When the dream is done,
When the world grows small—
Known, and plumbed, and charted all —
And there’s no more wonders left beneath
the sun.
QUEENSLAND.
VANCE PALMER.
Bingaloo.
When I went down to Bingaloo
My heart was blithe and gay.
I hadn’t anything to do
Or anything to pay.
The lambs were frisking on the green,
The breeze was honey ’neath the blue;
Life was a lilting song, I ween,
In quiet Bingaloo.
There was a girl in Bingaloo—
Gold eyes and hot bronze hair.
Her mood was kind, her heart was true— —
No truer anywhere.
She kissed me as the dawn grew bright
(She never has kissed you.) ....
She kissed me, kissed me, every night,
That girl in Bingaloo.
If you’ve a lady you would woo
In happy style, dear chap.
You’ll seek in vain for Bingaloo
On any proper map.
You know where poets’ dreamings lead,
Where poets’ damsels coo.
By this you’ve guessed the truth, indeed. ..
There is no Bingaloo.
N.S.W.
FRANK MORTON.

TERMS TO SUBSCRIBERS.
(In Advance, Only.)
For £1 6s. 6d. per annum you can have The
Bulletin sent regularly to you every iveek for a
year, including Special Christmas Number, post paid.

Special Notice.
Insufficiently stamped letters are liable to be re-
fused.
The contents of this paper are copyright by the
■ publishers. The right to reproduce or translate any
part of the contents of this number is hereby ex-
pressly reserved by the publishers.
The Editor will carefully read, and, where prac-
ticable, acknowledge in the “Correspondence” column
all contributions submitted, whether in the form of
Political, Social or other Articles, Verse, Short Tales
or Sketches (those dealing with Australian subjects,
and not exceeding two columns in length, or, say,
3000 words, are specially acceptable), Paragraphs,
Letters or Newspaper Clippings. All communications
will be regarded as strictly confidential.
The Bulletin will return all unsuitable MS. when
the address is specified and stamps are enclosed. But
no MS. will be returned, or preserved, unless these
conditions are observed. When MS. is forwarded
ivithout stamps for return it will be understood that
the copy is to be destroyed if not acceptable. Per-
sonal applications for return of MS. cannot receive
attention. The Bulletin desires, further, those
sending MS. to distinctly understand that it is not
responsible for the preservation or transmission of MS.
To Amateurs in Black and White.
Anyone supplying to The Bulletin original
humorous or other topical drawings in line or wash,
of sufficient merit and interest and adapted for
direct reproduction, or any interesting photograph
illustrative of a current event, will be paid for same
on acceptance. Any picture containing a good idea
but unfitted for direct reproduction — i.e., any picture
which has to be re-drawn —will be paid for as a
suggestion only. The conditions under winch such
artistic contributions ivill be received are those
applying to MS.

THURSDAY, DECEMBER 9, 1915.
WHITE AUSTRALIA AND DISCOL-
ORED GERMANY.
German shells may blow up historic build-
ings and destroy the sandbag architecture of
trench defences, but it is not within the
power of cordite to remove national convic-
•.* , ,
o s and aspirations save by a tedious pro-
cess of extermination. Ireland, for all her
distressful history, is Ireland, dreaming still
of union and self-government, and near the
realisation of her dream; Italy, trampled by
~ ~ . T . , . . , .
Goth and Vandal and Lombard and Austrian,
torn by dissension and broken small by
petty rivalries of men and cities, won to her
place as a nation, and to-day, in the strength
of unity, batters at the last barrier that holds
, f •£n j. • i
liti lorn the possession of all that is her
own; Rome only failed in her imperial mis-
sion because her people had ceased to think
imperially. It is necessary to reiterate
this plain fact, sealed and attested by his-
tory, for the benefit of those timid souls
who cannot focus their spectacles properly
beyond the smoke of the present con-
vulsion. Because one nation, formerly
regarded as white, has broken up the
peace _ of the world and aimed a large
but indifferently-directed blow at civili-
sation, they cry that the White Aus-
tralia doctrine is no longer tenable, and that
we must perforce alter our views of things
and fling wide the doors that we had declared
closed for all time. To follow such a sug-
gestion would be to admit ourselves beaten,
to confess that our efforts to aid Britain had
been utterly useless, and to take a lower
stand as a people, who had been walloped
from the mountain-top of ambition and
forced to seek shelter in the hotch-potch of
mixed nations. It is safe to say that no
Australian worthy of the name feels like
that; it is equally safe to say that our
White Australia policy goes on.
* * * *
Two years ago we accepted Germany in
good faith as one of the white nations. Had
we looked at matters more closely we might
have had doubts about her color. “Made in
Germany” might well have had a significance
greater than its mere reference to shoddy
cloth, bad cutlery and smelly chemicals. Out
of the heart of Germany came the wild burst
of savagery which destroyed Roman civilisa-
tion and put back the world’s clock of pro-
gress for a thousand years, and the Teutonic
race long continued to supply barbarian
forces to frustrate every effort for the
restoration of the peace that the Goth
and Vandal had broken. On good scientific
authority it seems to be established that the
underlying element in the mixed German
race was a savage as implacable as the
Apache Indian, and that the peculiar skull
formation of the true Teuton lent itself to
the singing of hymns of hate as far back as
the days when the lake-dwellers of Europe
ate pony.and fashioned weapons of bronze.
All this ought to have kept the nations sus-
picious of certain storm centres beyond the
Rhine ; but so much that was called German
was not true Teutonic, and the German band
played so well, that we forgot to look for
renewed trouble and were prepared to accept
Hans for the simple, industrious white per-
son he appeared to be. Now that he has run
amok we know better. From the Australian
point of view the German can no longer
rank as a white man. As a settler he
seemed to have his points; but we can do
quite well without him, and if we decide
that llans is black and to be regarded per-
manently as an undesirable alien, that
decision, if it errs at all, will at least err
on the side of safety. Australia can develop
without anything made in Germany, and the
Commonwealth will in future be all the
whiter for the absence of any person re-
motely suspected of association with those
who reddened Belgium and discolored their
souls in her blood.
* * * *
The question as between White Australia
and the Asiatic is not in the least affected
by the fact that the Kaiser’s subjects may
henceforth go on the black-list; but the real
trouble with those who have failed to grasp
the true inwardness of the national policy is
that the war has revealed certain Asiatic
peoples as good Allies to whom Australia
owes gratitude. Those races of India which
represent the higher standards of Asiatic
civilisation and culture have proved capable
of srreat self-sacrifice in the common cause ;
and the Indian States have paid willing toll
in blood and treasure to assist their white
friends to a victory in which they them-
selves are only indirectly concerned. Japan,,
armed and ready, adhered loyally to her
treaty obligations with Britain, and has gone
far beyond them in the tremendous indus-
trial assistance she has rendered to Russia
in the matter of munitions and army sup-
plies. These facts cannot be ignored : and
Australia has not the least wish to ignore
them or under-estimate, the value of Indian
loyalty or Japanese faith. But they do not
in any way affect the ideal of a White Aus-
tralia. Long before the war started, un-
prejudiced minds were prepared to see the
fine qualities of many of the Asiatic peoples.
The art work of Japan, springing from the
inherently fine taste of the people, is some-
thing that we more than admire—we envy.
Even old China, ill-organised, battered by
ages of misrule, and patient beyond all
imagining, has qualities which appeal to us.
While Persia weaves a carpet or India ham-
mers her eccentric fancy on leaves of brass,
none but a fool can be blind to the fact that
Asiatic civilisation has that in it which
lifts it far above the squalid savagery that
lurks in the black corners of Europe’s great-
est cities. Admitting all this, and acknow-
ledging that the nobler peoples of Asia can
be both honorable friends and faithful allies,
we are still under no compulsion to throw
open our doors to them or invite them to
permanent residence on the soil of this con-
tinent. The making of every nation is in
the nature of a great experiment, and races
in their final form are the result of reactions
similar to those of the chemist’s laboratory.
If we, the citizens of the Australian Com-
monwealth, who are the experimenters, de-
cide that from the racial elements which go
to make the future nation the Asiatic shall
be entirely excluded, that is our own affair
alone, as long as we can keep the retort and
crucible in our own hands. There is nothing
whatever in the nature of insult implied in
our choice of material. We can honestly
admire what is best in many peoples and yet
take the firm, scientific stand that an ad-
mixture of those peoples must be fatal to the
resultant race.
The White Australia policy is not a wild-
cat imagining of eugenic dreamers. It is
based on the solid logic of history and the
accumulated knowledge with regard to other
experiments that failed. A whole continent,
bounded on all sides by the sea, and as yet
not seriously affected by race mixture, is in
our hands. It is a continent of infinite
variety of climate and soil, and under pro-
per development capable of producing all
that man can require for a highly civilised
life. The White Australia policy is based
upon the idea that within this sphere the
white race can develop to the full all within
it that is highest and sweetest and
noblest. The narrow economic ideal
of a bed and three square meals a
day has been left far behind. We look for-
ward to a time when a completely educated
white community, strong in thew and
muscle, clean in thought and high in pur-
pose, shall lift this Commonwealth to the
first place among the nations. We look
forward to a time when our looms shall turn
out the finest fabrics; when our hammers
shall beat out the finest wares of metal ;
when the potter’s art and the painter’s craft
shall fill every home with things of beauty
and delight. We look forward to a time
when the teeming wealth of a highly culti-
vated soil shall put wealth in every hand
beyond the dreams of ancient despots or
modern millionaires ; when poverty shall be
a thing unknown and disease beaten back to
its last ditch ; when to every man born in
tiie land shall come as a natural heritage
the right to live his own rich life to the
fullest advantage to himself and those
who follow him. And none of these things
cart be unless we have freedom and scope to
work out the- experiment in our own way.
Perplexing as some of the problems that lie
in the immediate future are, the ideal re-
mains ; and though there may be turmoil of
strikes and labor awards, and division of
opinion on minor questions, and bitter con-
flict as to the choice of paths towards the
goal,, there are already steps taken which are
full of promise. Already the right of the
toiler to live a better life than he lives in
other lands is practically established, and
there is more and more to follow till the
mists clear and the road lies straight before
us. The White Australia policy has an
economic as well as a racial basis, and the
mere fact that Asia’s economic ideals are
below our own is enough in itself to justify
the drawing of the firm line which we are
bound to defend at all costs and every
hazard.
* * * *
Friends of Asia declare that we shall
understand her better when the war blows
past and the last bugle calls the world to
peace. It is the duty of the friends of Aus-
tralia to point out that the time may be
near when Asia will understand us better
and discover that there is solid logic and
sound sense behind our policy. Perhaps the
history of the future may show that Asia
understands us better than might be
expected. Our own chief need is the proper
understanding of ourselves.
CERTAIN PARTY POLITICIANS
REJOICE.
The other day, it being the twenty-fifth
anniversary of the N. S. Wales Labor
party’s birth, there was a “reception” and
a sports meeting; various persons made
speeches and poured the oil of gladness over
themselves and their friends; other indivi-
duals gave the newspapers reminiscent
stories about the early struggles, and the
meeting addressed from the cart-tail, and
the pilgrimage with a soap-box in search of
opportunities to get the democracy’s ear.
Also figures were produced to show how
sweeping had been the victories achieved
by the party at the polls, and how firmly it
had secured control of the political machine.
But nobody got excited. In fact, there was
nothing to get excited about. Yet it was
all very interesting in a statistical sort of
way, and those members of the party who
had been longest in Parliament were given
a chance of testifying to their complete
approval of the manner in which things in
general had worked themselves out. It was
a party celebration, held for party purposes
by solid party men, and the big drum had,
of course, only one note —Party—Party—
Party! Is not the Labor party on top?
Then what more appropriate than that the
partisan who has a job, or wants one,
should fling up his hat in public and talk
wild words about “the triumph of the
cause”? Nothing, obviously nothing. It
had lifted him from the ruck, anyhow, and
that was triumph enough to go on with.
* * * *
But to a paper like The Bulletin, which
cares less than tuppence about parties and
refuses to be interested in anything but
principles and the realisation of policies,
it looks as if all this machine-made rejoicing
over half-a-jubilee might with great advan-
tage have been postponed for five and
twenty years. Then the ram’s horn trumpet
on which the Hebrews performed the Jubilee
fantasia of emancipation could be produced
- —if there was then anything to blow about
—and the whole community could be in-
vited to turn handsprings and otherwise
show its great approval of the steadfastness
and courage with which the political Labor
party of N. S. Wales had kept the vows
taken at its christening. As it turned out
the invitation of last week was mainly to
give three cheers because Labor had a
majority in the Assembly and was in office;
because Holman and Dick Meagher and
the rest are still holding their end up
against the other lot of battlers —all of
which looks a mighty poor excuse for
bursts of applause, except from Holman
and Dick Meagher. Of the position the
Labor party occupies to-day in relation to
its ideals of five and twenty years ago; of
the strides made towards accomplishing
Labor’s economic reforms; of the purifica-
tion of the political atmosphere—about
these subjects, which alone can form just
grounds for party adulation, there was (he
silence of the Illimitable West. According
to Leviticus xxv., the Jubilee was the year
of remission from the penal consequences of
sin. From the persistent dumbness of the
Ma State’s coterie of triumphant party
barrackers, it seems as if the Silver Jubilee
is a fixture at which the unrepentant sinner
should rejoice and take a pledge to keep on
. sinning for another five and twenty years.
* * * *
Yet there is to the credit of Labor during
these 25 years, first of honest effort, and
finally of ignoble time-serving, a list of fine
accomplishments. In the industrial arena
it has initiated many salutary reforms and
prevented the passage of reactionary
measures. It has done much for education.
It has struggled to help the under dog. It
lias tackled many gross abuses in the social
field. Most of these things were well worth
fighting for. But while it has done well
with one hand it has done evil with the
other, and on the balance the direct wrongs
done to the people so far outweigh the good
things as to make them almost negligible.
The awful record of borrowing and bloat-
ing ; the criminally reckless expenditure of
the public funds; the painful evasion of
duty while pandering for the support of
special interests; the fumbling about in
search of palliatives to meet the circum-
stances of the moment; the revelation of
an administrative ineptitude so profound as
to be almost unbelievable —these have placed
on the shoulders of the people a weight
scarcely less than might have been put
there by the most ignorant of Tory parties.
After 25 years a Labor Government in office
is compelled to make the shocking admis-
sion that the whole economic and industrial
superstructure of the State is based upon
the capitalist money-lender—that the people
could not get employment unless the Minis-
try went on raising millions a year from
Cohen. And in its frenzied struggle to
avoid being found out it is going on. cadging
even at the cannon’s mouth, and handing
out doles here and largesse there. For
what purpose? To ultimately benefit the
men and women who toil, or to buy votes
and thus buttress in their jobs the renegades
who are fattening on the people? For these
individuals to make merry at a “jubilee” is
a mockery of that Labor which has been
sold like a bullock at Homebush. The
healthy ambitions of the earlier years have
come to nothing. Fat has gone on getting
bigger in circumference from year to year ;
landowners have flourished like pests on the
expenditure of public money, and used their
gains to extort larger and larger tribute
from the people; booms have have been en-
gineered which benefit nobody but specula-
tors and other parasites. All Labor has got
out of it is a job, paid for by a money-lender
who will have to be paid back presently.
No —there wasn’t much to rejoice about on
the 25th birthday; and unless the Parlia-
mentary Labor party in N. S. Wales purges
itself pretty quickly there will not be much
excuse for blowing on the ram’s horn when
the marker calls 50.
THE RIGHT WAY TO SMASH THE
GERMAN PIANO.
Apart from the story of the dame who in-
vited her friends to come and help her smash
the piano because it was made in Leipsic, a
lot of nonsense is being talked by persons,
Fat and otherwise, about things originally'
made on the hungry side of the Rhine, and
public attention is directed to side issues
instead of being focussed on essentials. The
things that we already have of German make
represent the disastrous policy of the past;
and the extent of our purchases from what
are now enemy countries was so great that
if we were to smash everything the German
ever handled we should make a pretty mess
of our possessions. To use and enjoy the
things we have while we provide that our
possessions come from a cleaner source in
the future is the sane policy for to-day'. But
just here occurs the point that we steadily
go on evading, in spite of all the curses
poured on the Kaiser and all the assurances
that are given of a vague and general mend-
ing of our ways. Hymns of Hate notwith-
standing, the history of wars and their con-
clusion shows that bitterest foes become
fastest friends. This war is certainly not
like others that went before it; but for all
Fat’s present throbs of ardent patriotism,
it is certain that, when the cannons cool, his
dislike for the German won’t cancel his
eagerness to make money out of his wares
if there is a way left in which he can do it.
Patriotic zeal doesn’t burn well under an
outsize in waistcoats ; and if the gate is left
open and the German can supply the goods
the German will get the trade just as surely
as the Devil would if Hell were a country
of cheap production.
* * * «
We must cease worrying about the pencils
with the Bavarian brand, cut out all the
piano-smashing hysteria, and get down to
business. What the German sold us in the
past is cause for regret, but it cannot now
matter the whisk of a feather-duster. What
we ought to be concerned about is that we
may never be tricked into assisting him to
re-build that trade supremacy which he vol-
untarily threw aside when he pushed his
blood-stained boot through Belgium. To do
this everybody knows that the first step is
the establishment of a towering tariff on
everything made, grown or handled in Ger-
many. On the face of it this ought to he
sufficient; but it is not. We have had our
lesson of the thoroughness of the German in
preparing for war, and we have no reason to
doubt that what is left of him after the last
battle will be just as thorough and just as
unscrupulous in scheming to pick up again
the loose ends of his lost trade. Half the
countries of the world are likely to be open
as depots for the distribution of his goods,
and even a totally prohibitive tariff against
the manufactures of the one country we
desire to hit and stall off would not prevent
us from buying and using its wares.- Nothing
could be easier than to make Nicaragua a
clearing-house for German articles of com-
merce, and by the time we had discovered
the fact and amended the tariff law, therehy
banging the gate against Nicaragua, the
goods might be pouring in from new agencies
in San Domingo. To be effective in shutting
out the work of German looms and foun-
dries it is necessary that the tariff wall
should be uniform and of equal force against
all countries. The only possible way in
which Australia can be sure of shutting out
the manufactures and products of our pre-
sent despised and detested foe is by making
everything we want to use. from a bottle
of disinfectant to a battleship, within our
own borders.
* * * *
To sink the tariff issue is to play into
the hands of Germany in the future. If we
want to be victorious in industrial war as
well as in arms, the time to act is now.
Delay is as disastrous in the more compli-
cated field of commerce as it has already
proved in the game that is played with 12-
inch guns and monster armies. All tariffs
that Australia has yet known have been
revenue-producing. and the protection
afforded to our industries has never been
what it pretended to be. The public mind
has been like an ass halting between two
burdens, and the importing crowd has never
failed to get a point or two ahead of the
straight-out advocates of making things in
Australia. The general desire to forever
shut out goods of enemy origin appears for
the moment to be quite genuine; and on the
wave of that desire we must go forward and
do things. The Federal Government has its
chance to take a definite stand, establish
the real Protection which would produce
practically no revenue and enable Australia
to get down solidly to work as a self-depend-
ent manufacturing country. Once Australia
took on the job of doing her own work the
“Made-in-Germany” spectre could never
trouble us again.
* * * *
It is in a very special sense the duty of
people who are now very patriotic, but who
two years ago were hand-in-glove with Ger-
man traders, to throw themselves into this
movement. It was to their efforts in the
past, and to the political stand they took,
that we owed the German pianos and pen-
cils and lamps and knives that are now
worrying them. True repentance is not
shown by taking an axe to smash the pencils
or inviting neighbors to come and play with
their boots on the piano, but by making an
honest effort to ensure that such things will
not again stand in. the road of patriotism
and progress. The cash that went out of the
country to pay for the piano and other
articles helped to buy the very guns that
have been killing our brothers at the Dar-

clanelles. Those of us whose cash interests,
translated into political terms and votes, pre-
vented the establishment of effective Protec-
tion, were the allies of Turks and Germans
who did the killing. To maintain the old
position, backed by the old fallacies and out-
worn arguments, is to continue in that un-
holy alliance. While the patriotism of the
men whose guilt was greatest is at white-
heat, that feeling should be nailed down and
made permanently productive of patriotic

larger Protection now when the consciences

are troubling them a little over the shameful

PLAIN ENGLISH.
Assisting' the Empire by Walking and
Getting Hot, Sore Feet.
How much longer are these circus tricks
going on? It seems about time an explana-
tion arrived, because, while the vaudeville
touch was tolerable and perhaps necessary
in the earlier stages of the performance, the
hour is getting quite late enough to hurry
up with the programme. These young men
who are tramping through the country, pick-
ing up a mate here and there —the “Dun-
garees” and the “Wallabies” and the
“Waratahs” and the “Coo-ees” and the
“Kangaroos,” and the “Midnight Birds” and
the “Let ’er go Gallaghers,” and all the rest
of them —are wearing out good shoe leather
and exhausting a fine reserve of enthusiasm
to mighty little purpose. One lot tramped
about 300 miles lately and pulled up
260 strong; another crowd set out
from Warwick (Q.), and after a
fortnight’s speechifying and cadging along
the track, 125 delivered themselves
at Brisbane. All of which looks like
a mighty lot of tramp for very little
soldier—an absurd waste of effort and wind,
and, what is of much more consequence, time.
The days these men spent trudging through
the heat and dust could have been filled much
more profitably in camp learning their job.
In addition to raising 9000 men a month
Australia is committed to the raking in.of
an additional 50,000. It is a big job
under the present Micawber system, but
in whatever way it is going to be done this
vague trudging over the landscape appears
about the most futile that could be attempted.
And viewing the matter from the strictly
military point of view, these extended plods
are not doing the men any good. They
would be far better for a ride in the train.
Anyhow, if Billjim’s arrival at the place
where he is most wanted has to be delayed
for several weeks while he gets corns on his
feet helping to wear out Australian roads,
the system is one for which the warmest
approval can certainly be looked in Berlin.
The Partly Unified Cadge.
The Commonwealth Government’s “scheme
of borrowing” for the States is very much
on the lines of offering a toper unlimited
swigs at meals if only he will leave off his
habit of swallowing a bottle of brandy , be-
fore breakfast. There were long discussions
between the Federal Treasurer and the State
representatives, the former hoping that a
scheme might be devised under which the
cadge from John Bull would be left to the
Commonwealth, the proceeds to be cut up
among the conspirators. Asked to put for-
ward a statement of their requirements the
States weighed in with offers to be satisfied
during 1916 with bites like this: —
N. S. Wales .. ..£4,450,000
Victoria .. . . 2,500,000
Queensland . . . . 3,000,000
S. Australia .. .. 3,000,000
Westralia . . . . 2,300,000
Tasmania .. •• 750,000
This meant £16,000,000; and as Hughes
and lliggs had come to the conclusion that
£12,000,000 would be enough for developing
our great natural resources and keeping
votes safely in the bag, they cut down the
bites to be allowed, thus: —
N. S. Wales . . . . £3,060,000
Victoria .. . . 1,720,000
Queensland . . . . 2,562,000
S. Australia . . ... 2,062,000
Westralia . . . . 2,050,000
Tasmania .. .. 516,000
After prolonged reflection the agonised
States agreed to this £12.000,000 —all ex-
cept N. S. Wales. They merely wanted, in
addition, the right to raise within the Com-,
monwealth another £16,000.000. Appar-
ently the horrified lltggs. with the vision of
the multitudinous millions he has to get for
war purposes, said “No” with great em-
phasis. At any rate, he would only consent
to be the States’ mendicant in London on con-
dition that his principals agreed to be con-
tented with raising £5,000,000 on their own
account locally. Again there was agree-
ment —except by N. S. Wales. The net re-
sult of it all, therefore, is that the Ma State
takes its own way to the pawnshop, and the
other States will put up with £8,940,000 be-
tween them from London and £3,610,000
secured by flying kites on the Australian
breeze. This with the £8 900.000 which is
the irreducible minimum required by Hol-
man ought to keep the wolf from the door
for a year: and with the plaster of the war
debt it should give the Kid something sub-
stantial to remember the Old Man for.
. .
Service and Its Rewards.
There is nothing like the frank and full
recognition of outstanding merit. Colonel
Wallack has been appointed temporary
Brigadier-General; and this paper lays at
his-, feet the vegetable offerings suitable to
the occasion. At the same time it is a little
remarkable that, on the very day when the
promotion was announced, with Ministerial
comments, Colonel Ramaciotti, the new
commandant at Sydney, should abolish at a
single sweep one of the chief instruments of
government employed by the late comman-
dant. He cleared out the military police and
arranged that the men in training should
police themselves. There is no doubt what-
ever that most of the recent very serious
trouble could be traced to the fact that the
military police have been a body separate
from the forces which go out to
reason with Turk and Teuton. Nothing
is more irritating to a recruit than
to feel that he is in any way under
the authority of those who, from cold
feet or other causes, are unwilling to go to
the Front. The civil police are tolerated
by the ordinary citizen because, even if they
don’t always show brilliant intelligence in
the discharge of their duties, they at least
are prepared to take something more than
ordinary citizen risks for the public good.
But the military police, as lately constituted,
failed to earn the respect of the soldiers for
the simple reason that they were not called
upon to take the ordinary risks of military
life. Such an arrangement was naturally
fraught with danger; for, failing the support
of those among whom they are set to keep
order, and being in a minority, they were
bound sooner or later to strike trouble and
strike it hard. That they did strike it and
strike it frequently is common knowledge.
Now the whole system is changed. Colonel
Ramaciotti, bringing plain common-sense
to bear on the problem, has got right at the
root of an irritation that has been apt to
crystallise into brick-bats, and henceforth the
police of the camps are to be supplied by
the military units themselves. What his
reward will be we don’t know. But. taking
a line by the past, we should not be at all
surprised if he is made a corporal.
Arms and the Tendency to Use Blue
Metal.
The minor disturbances in connection with
the forces now in training, while they must
be deplored, are nevertheless quite in accord
with the traditions of the British voluntary
army. T. Atkins seldom if ever mutinies
and is rarely found wanting when his work
lies plainly before him, but the tendency to
conduct a little war on his own account is
always present. Kipling’s “row in Silver
Street” and his account of how Mulvaney
tackled the big drunk draft are photographic ;
and every reader of the tale of Havelock s
tremendous march remembers how the heroes
of the strenuous business began to paint
Cawnpore red when they forced their way
through to that place of horror. The sur-
prising thing is that voluntary armies do
not much more frequently start to burst up
the civil section of the community. The
voluntary system sets the soldiery apart as
a class, and gives them interests distinct
from those of the general mass of the
people. Nothing is more natural than that
there should be trouble when any kind of
friction arises. The remedy is plain. Once
the voluntary system goes the whole nation
becomes part and parcel of the army. It is
no longer necessary then to remind citizens
that the boys in uniform are going to fight
for them. The nation as a whole is fighting
for itself, and the soldiers have ceased to be
a class apart. Military and civil are terms
that have no longer any meaning. Further,
the tendency of the voluntary system is to
put the more reckless spirits in the fore-
front, while an honest conscription would
sort men out, and their merits as citizens
would not be lost the moment they put on a
uniform. This must make for stability in
the ranks and help to hold the wilder crowd
down. When Australia, as Australia, fights
for her own, instead of deputing her job to
a small section of her citizens, it is pretty
certain that she won’t start to smash up her
own property before starting out to smash
up the foe.
Drink as a Preventive of Crime.
“Quidam’s” sermon on Strong Drink: —
Careful study shows irresistibly that alcohol, for
any direct physical effect it produces, is a fraud.
Modern science seems to regard it as a poison with
bad effects (if any) on the organs, and of very poor
value as a stimulant. Even if one is dying of snake-
bite, any physical good conferred by draughts of
spirits is so flimsy that it is not worth while gallop-
ing miles to the nearest pub for a bottle. But the
point both the anti-alcohol scientist and the wowser
of the virulent type overlook is the indirect or
mental effect of alcohol on the human being. And
this is a very large feature of the question, too
important to be omitted in any consideration of the
matter. It is probably the one feature which has
enabled alcohol to defeat scientific teaching and logi-
cal reasoning, and bob up serenely at every street
corner, because there always seems a gulf between
the teachings of teetotallers and what the average
man instinctively feels to be the truth.
When everything is going wrong and you are
worried and in the right condition for lying awake
all night brooding over things, decide to go to the
nearest pub and have a few drinks. If your wife
has sense she will suggest it. Many sensible wives
have ere this pulled their husbands up on the first
steps towards suicide by a rational prescribing of the
pub remedy. Anyway, go and have a few drinks, and
get into conversation with anyone who will have a
drink with you. Ten to one he has done many
things and been in many places you haven’t, so he
will prove interesting. And when you return home,
carrying just enough to make the lights a bit
dazzling, isn’t the world a different place? All sorts
of magnificent schemes enthral you, probably all
quite impracticable and illusory, but at the moment
you don’t mind that because you don’t know it. Now
the world seems a poor, helpless sort of a football
for you to kick around, and not at all the dreadful,
enclosing, menacing, insurmountable wall it was in
the afternoon. So you go to sleep with splendid
thoughts, and next day you are refreshed and san-
guine again, and no man is really beaten while he is
sanguine. Physically the effect of that strong
drink was probably bad, but the mental effect caused
by its drugging of sensibility has far more than out-
weighed that. It has, in fact, acted like the drug
that it is ; but when things are out of cog drugs are
often beneficial.
Alcohol does for you just what the medical man
with the good bedside manner does. He instils con-
fidence in the patient, and his own auto-suggestion
builds him up to fight the ailment. Later on, the
M.D. collects the fee.
Yards, rods, miles have been written about the
immoral effects of alcohol, and it is about time for
some gifted person to arise and publish a soul-
stirring work on its moral effects, not only through
the infrequent valuable idea evolved in the brain of
the partially boozed, but also through the suicides,
murders and other crimes timely drink has pre-
vented. A large percentage of the crimes due to
drink show up in the police-court records, but it is
impossible to get any reliable statistics as to those
that would have occurred but didn’t because King
Alcohol blew them away with his beery breath.
The case for something really solid in the
way of land-values taxation was ably if un-
intentionally put by new Lands-Minister
Hutchinson when in the Victorian Assem-
bly he gave the State immigration figures as
from July, 1914. The number of immigrants
to Victoria was 4623. Of these the total
settled on Victorian farms was 40, or less
than 1 per cent.! Then, hopefully, the Min-
ister remarked that “after the war there will
probably be a great influx of people from the
older lands.” No matter how long, it may
remain in office the Peacock Government
will never have the futility of its present
expensive land-settlement schemes presented
to its notice in a plainer manner. If the
elderly, unenthusiastic Hutchinson really
has any desire to do anything but collect his
Ministerial salary as it falls due, the sight
of these awful figures should impel him to go
deeply into the matter of land-freeing expedi-
ents, with an eye on the “great influx” pre-
sently to arrive; and if he has any sort of a
mind at all to call his own, a Hutchinson
agitation for adequate land-taxation will be
the early outcome of his meditations. But,
unfortunately, Hutchinson’s past career
promises nothing large and sweeping and
nobly planned; and his present lack-lustre
appearance promises less. For him the poli-
tical barometer is “set laissez-faire” —which
means a continuance of pleasant weather for
advertising-agents and private land-sharks.
South Aus. furnishes the latest example of
a very old swindle. Every year, as soon as
the grain is sown, somebody—as often as not
a Minister —forecasts a harvest of some
dizzy millions. In N. S. Wales this season
official figures were talked actually before
the crop was sown. On the smallest provo-
cation thereafter more weird figures are
published; and then the “official prelimi-
nary estimate” drops in. Usually this is
something very wild. Just about that time
the wheat-buyer begins to get in his fine
work. The vastness of the crop has duly
impressed itself upon cocky, who mostly
thinks that the little Australian handful of
wheat governs the market, and the buyer
makes bargains at pence lower than the
true price. In S. Aus. 35 million bushels
were talked of two months ago (mostly, it
is true, by cockies themselves, who three
times out of four give absurd estimates of
what they will reap) ; then came the
Treasurer’s Budget Speech quoting 29
million bushels; now the Government
Statist lobs in with 27-1 million bushels.
When it is all over 25 millions are
likely to be the outside figures. Every
year in N. S. Wales the Railways
make their own estimates, and they
are invariably millions —anything up to
eight millions —less than the official esti-
mate ; and the official estimate is never
right. But the bloated figure is a handy
thing to bash cocky over the head with,
while the agent squeezes out of him another
ha’penny for the impoverishment of the
country and the glorification of the city—
for the impoverishment of the producer and
the glorification of the parasite.
♦
The same report comes in from every State
—the pretentious private homes lent or taken
over so that the wounded Kangaroos might
rest or recuperate are nearly all more than
half empty. Some of them are about nine-
tenths empty. If Jimbile has a mate who
has a home, no matter how poor a sort of
home it is, Jimbieu would rather smoke
there than in a marble hall. It is a charac-
teristic thing about the man to whom mate-
ship means more than to any other person in
the world. The pretentiousness of these
official rest-houses, moreover, appals him.
And that, by the way, also explains why so
many men, especially from Outback, get so
hopelessly tangled when they come to the
city. Bill of Booligal passes the decent
pub, which to him seems too swell, and turns
into the den, which outwardly anyway is the
nearest approach to the bush pub he has
been used to. After that there is soon
trouble, and there will be trouble right along
until the authorities get sense enough to
provide the camps with wet canteens.
♦
Over 100 doctors will be needed for the now
Commonwealth army of 50,000 men. The
fear has been expressed that the matter will
provide a fresh problem for the Defence
authorities, the supply in the past having
been hardly equal to the demand. The view
of the profession in Melbourne is that, in the
making of appointments, there is too little
consideration of actual qualifications and too
much wire-pulling. When the charge of the
Melbourne Base Hospital was given to that
faithful family-practitioner, Carty Salmon,
M.H.R. for the Grampians, who had many
years previously given up medicine in
Lower Toorak to practise dialectics in the
House, the medical profession went about
muttering. It is no reflection on Salmon’s
capacity to agree that there was something
to mutter about, considering that there were
plenty of men in constant practice with
unarguably better claims than the M.H.R. to
rule at the Base Hospital. The fact that
Salmon has since resigned his hospital-job,
being unable to keep his health and both
salaries, does not affect the position as far
as the principle goes. A suggestion has been
made for a selection Board, to consist -of
three non-official members of the medical
profession, who should deal with all applica-
tions for military service—“the personnel to
be of the highest order if confidence is to be
restored throughout the profession and a
death-blow given to influence and favoritism.”
Favoritism or no, the appointment of such
a Board is quite within the province of the
Defence authorities; and there is now surely
enough in the history of the A.A.M.C. to war-
rant some such action.
The Pipes of War.
Splutters the flame and the grease runs
down,
The wick is fogged in a winding sheet,
And a woman waits with her long black hair,
Her small pale face and her great despair;
For she hears the pipes in the street,
In the street and over the hill,
Wherever she goes she can hear them still.
She rocks the cradle with one bare foot,
And the babe in its bed is whimpering ;
And over there where her good man sleeps
Is a grisly phantom that writhes and weeps,
The wraith of a once glad thing;
And it plucks the pillow from ’neath his
head
And he clutches sleep, but he wakes instead.
Wakes to the sound of the calling pipes
That toss and shudder through pine-boughs
wild,
And he looks for a feathered hat to don
And belt and bayonet are girded on.
Then he stoops to kiss the child.
And her small wet face in its long black
hair,
While the red flame dies in the candle there.
For the wind from the pipes came bearing
down,
Over the cobbles bearing down,
And it passed like a storm on the grey wall’s
face,
And it blew like hail on the market place,
And harried the dreaming town ;
It was cold....and yet ’twas hot, that
breath,
Like a fever glow that will chill to Death.
Out; on the moon-white road they heard,
Sleeping swagman and teamster brown;
One flung his bluey behind the track
And slung his rifle across his back,
And one cast the long whip down ;
A quiver ran through the tall fern’s stipes ;
The green bush woke, for it heard the pipes.
And the Piper? Some say his face is fair,
With a laurel wreath on a Titian head ;
But there are women who crouch and pray,
And in their weeping I hear them say
That his locks with blood are red,
That the magic music that swells and moans
Is played. through hollows of dead men’s
bones.
From end to end of our continent
The Piper skirls on his pipes to-day;,
I can see the swing of his ghostly kilt
And his plaid is bright, as is blood fresh
spilt,
And I hear him loudly play,
And the crowded troopers have left the shore
But the Piper marches and calls for more.
More from the plains of the wide Bareoo,
More from the mulga, more from the town !
They stand to attention all the way
With a quick salute when they hear him
play,
And they take the bridle down
And ride, as their grandsires rode before,
To that haunting rhythm that cries “More!
More!”
More! Will the list be never full?
More! Will the hunger never cease?
Level columns from west to east,
Food for the far-off vulture feast....
And never the white bird Peace,
Whose eyes were blinded by battle smoke
When the hills to the cry of the pipes awoke.
I had two sons, they were fair and fine,
Such as a mother loves to own ;
But there came a night of a golden moon,
And of mirrored stars in a blue lagoon,
When I heard the war-pipes moan.
They rose like an echo among the reeds,
Like a hint in Heaven of violent deeds.
I had two sons. . . .but the Piper called
And Robin guessed him a Viking bpld
And Rupert named him a noble Chief.
And I —l knew him alone as Thief.
I am grey and sick and old. . . .
Two sons in my prime were born to me.
Now I have two graves at Gallipoli.
QUEENSLAND.
M. FORREST.
An Appeal to Jeremiah.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Leave the fretting to the women! Leave
the squealing to the cowed !
Brother, what’s the good of growling in a
manner loose and loud !
Bottle up your Jeremiads! Still the sour
protesting yelp—
Things might look a lot more cheerful if
you’d hurry round and help!
Don’t make songs of ev’ry failure, or predict
our final doom
More than twice a week. O Brother! Don’t
fill other’s hearts with gloom ;
For it doesn’t help the nation while it’s busy
with the Hun
To be told in anguished whispers, “He has
got us ! We are done !”
Screw your courage up, dear Brother! Heap
no ashes on your head,
And refrain from wearing sackcloth until
someone’s really dead.
Armageddon’s far from finished, and we’ve
got no time for woe —
But if you must keep on moaning, do a
little for the foe!
N.S.W.

Armageddon and Stuffed Doves from
Yankeeland.
Defence Minister Pearce lias been fore-
casting the conclusion of the war “in the
Spring.” What grounds he has for this
excursion into the region of prophecy are
as deeply shrouded in gloom as everything
else in connection with the struggle.
Pearce may be right; but there is nothing
in the military position to-day, so far as it
can be grasped through our driblets of
alleged information, giving any encourage-
ment to the idea that the enemy is on the
point of being beaten. Even if he be
weakening—and the evidence on that point
can easily be exaggerated—it is as true now
as ever that the job ahead of the allied
arms is one calling for enormous effort if
the _ disaster of a jerry-built peace is to be
avoided. The call is all the time for men
—for every man that can be given a gun—-
and so far as this paper can see it is
mighty poor business to try to get the
necessary man by assuring him that, unless
he hurries up, the fight will be over before
he arrives. What looks nearer the truth
is that, unless he moves with speed, the
fight will go on indefinitely, and meantime
there will be dreadful sacrifice of life.
Germany’s wastage is frightful, too. Nowhere
have her victories been decisive. That she
can now win in the sense she origi-
nally expected is not believed even
at Berlin. But between holding up
the associated Huns and actually wallop-
ing them when the weather gets fine
again there is a tremendous difference. The
Hun, even if thrown right back to his own
frontiers, would still remain a hard nut to
crack. And if the fight ended without the
kernel being pulled out, millions of men
would have died in vain. Meanwhile the
position in the Balkans remains so obscure
that no one at a distance can understand
anything about it except that no reason can
be found for giving way to jubilation. The
Servians have been cruelly smashed, the
allied forces are in a situation of indifferent
tactical strength, the Greek insists on play-
ing poker, and something or other is happen-
ing up Rouihania way, but what it may be
is wholly indefinite. Our own lads are keep-
ing the Turk busy in Gallipoli; the British
force operating in Mesopotamia has been
smitten with considerable violence, which
the usual misleading reports did not lead us
to expect; and the safety of the Suez Canal
is again being talked about in a tone which
suggests that something lively may soon be
doing thereabouts. Meanwhile the U.S. crank
Ford has sailed from N’Yark with a cargo
of reporters and vegetarians to “call the
men out of the trenches for Christmas.” To
Ford the world should give the unanimous
vote of thanks that has long been waiting for
the man who would introduce the element of
pure comedy into this murderous business.
The demented car-maker with his ship cov-
ered with stuffed doves, his squirrel and his
poets and nut-eaters and Holy Rollers and
typewriters, is the most hilarious circum-
stance of modern days.
A fine specimen of N.S.YV. Attorney-
General D. R. Hall’s acumen in the wheat-
buying business is the case of the French
barque Noemi, which arrived at Sydney
lately with 3000 tons of wheat. The cargo
was purchased at Oregon (U.S.A.), and
wheat is worth less in Sydney to-day than
the Government actually gave for the cereal
in America. That is to say, the State is
“out” more than the big amount of the
Yeight. Instead of taking delivery the Gov-
ernment has decided to send the job lot to
Kurope for sale, in hopes of making itself
look less conspicuous as a business mug.
The Bulletin says good-bye to an old
colleague : —-
Nerves frayed to the breaking point by heart and
liver troubles account for the suicide of Alt' Vincent
at Manly last Monday night. He had been des-
perately ill for over a year, and in the hope that a
change might do what medicine refused to do he
was transferred from Melbourne to Sydney a few
months back. The hope was not realised; the
disease steadily became worse instead of better.
Vincent was born at Launceston on the 14th February,
1874. As a boy he copied Phil May diligently, and
was showing great promise as a draughtsman when
his drawings were accepted by The Bulletin in the
early ’nineties. In 1894 he went to Melbourne
under engagement to Punch, and not long afterwards
was appointed to the Melbourne staff of The
Bulletin. For nearly 20 years he remained there,
his regular work being broken only by two trips
to China and Japan and one to England. His style
never lost entirely the Phil May influence; but
in the delicacy of his work he often surpassed his
early model. His humor was pleasant, his line
always graceful, his pen so diligent that he has left
a hundred or more unpublished drawings behind him.
Alf Vincent’s well-groomed, handsome figure was
well known on the Melbourne block and on many
golf links near the city. Apart from his work and
his golf he had very few interests; the life of this
shy, reserved man was singularly placid and un-
eventful. He did not marry until between two and
three years ago, and besides his widow he leaves
one child.
