“Nobody’s Widow,” produced at Sydney
Criterion last Saturday, does not promise
to be any more popular than the long suc-
cession of comedies imported by the Firm
from the nootral republic. As usual, it is
practically a one-act play that lingers
through three acts. The characterisation is
its weakest point. No attempt is made to
give either the hero or the heroine any per-
sonality ; all they have to do is to “sling off”
at each other in the presence of a bewildered
host and her guests, to whom no apology is
made for their rudeness. The hostess is the
least satisfactory party. She is depicted
during the first act as a pleasant, easy-
going woman, but in the second she is
not only unable to see from the heroine’s
excited happiness and obvious allusions that
that girl has just accepted a proposal—a
piece of match-making that she has been
scheming to bring: about —but she immedi-
ately discloses herself as a violently flirta-
tious woman, almost unpleasantly insisting
upon being kissed by the man she
hoped would marry the heroine. The only
parts that are at all characterised are those
of a couple of guests who are introduced for
no reason and fade away after the first act.
The scene is chiefly set in Roxana’s parlor
in a private house; and though the other
guests seem to make this room their own,
this place of public assembly is selected,
with an apparent air of wickedness, by the
hostess for her supper with the hero. The
humor is purely verbal—a succession of three
acts of repartee of the obvious kind. When
tin; hero objects to go down on his knees
to propose, because it will spoil the creases
in his trousers, the heroine suggests that he
should wear kilts; and when he threatens
to sleep outside her bedroom her retort is
that he will get rheumatism!
* *• * *
Roxana (Muriel Starr) has secretly
married a duke (Charles A. Millward) in
Europe, but on her wedding day she finds the
bridegroom kissing another woman. She
runs away, and some months later comes
back in deep mourning to her friends with
the tale that her husband is dead. The
duke has contrived to be invited to the same
house, in the hope of winning back her
love. The duel runs through the three acts,
and is complicated by the fact that Roxana
had gA a divorce —without the husband
having even been notified of the proceedings.
Apparently these things happen in American
comedy. At last the duke, ignorant of the
divorce, states his intention to insist upon
his marriage rights by force. He even goes
to the length of taking off his tie. How-
ever, the complication that lie is now di-
vorced and has to be re-married leads to a
third act, when Roxana forgives him —
though for no apparent reason except that
it was high time that all good married
people were in bed. In real life there
would have been no third act —or, if there
had been, it would have been hardly pos-
AT SYDNEY TIVOLI FOLLIES.
Cupid Welch and Black Venus Weems.
sible to stage it. There is one delightful
and natural scene, early in the second act,
where Roxana and her duke, under the in-
fluence of the really beautiful stage moon-
light, exchange their genuine loving vows.
There are also: (1) a sedan chair telephone
box; (2) the latest style in cabin trunks;
(3) Grace Ppiotta, smothered in cold cream,
applying anti-wrinkle treatment to a face
that doesn’t need it; (4) the comedy of the
situation of a husband who is officially dead,
but really very much alive; and (5) the
final picture of Roxana going to bed (off
stage), and the exasperating fall of the
curtain before her husband follows.
* * * *
Muriel Starr fortunately had her moments
of emotional tension, and throughout acted
with intelligence and aplomb. It is a pity
that her part is not conversation but repartee.
Charles Millward’s mannerisms grow. There
is one, repeated through the comedy, in which
he attempts to stroke an imaginary mou-
stache. Couldn’t George Barnum give him a
moustache or side whiskers for the rest of
his ducal season? And, though his cold,
stolid, unemotional manner is permissible in
a duke, the comedy would have gained in
human warmth had he shown how much he
really is in love with his duchess. At times
he suggested that the report of his decease
is true. The dramatist, Avery Hopwood,
must be blamed for the inconsistencies in
Grace Palotta’s part of Betty Jackson ; but
her wooing of the indifferent duke is scarcely
in the true vein of comedy. Gertrude Bos-
well, Sydney Stirling and Clarence Blakiston
are thrown away in minor parts. The
staging is magnificent.

Jack Cannot's well-contrived burlesque of
a fumbling, fussy illusionist didn’t appeal
to some of Sydney Tivoli Follies’ press
critics; yet it is one of the best scraps of a
bright show. Cannot, with two greasy curls
lying flat on his forehead, hurries through
his interminable preparations for his grand
trick whereby two nervous lads, a dripping
block of ice, two tin dishes, a bundle of steel
hoops, a cane chair and a dirty sheet are to
be turned into a Polar bear. When at last
he fires his magician’s revolver, the whole
laboriously-arranged pile of boy, ice, sheet,
hoops, etc., collapses in a hopeless and dis-
gusting medley on the stage. Nevertheless
the fatuous juggler insists on the ingredi-
ents bowing correctly to the audience and
accompanying him off the stage as though
a triumph had been scored.
Branscombe’s Dandies are jollying along
at the Garden Theatre (Sydney), and with
each succeeding programme are widening
their flow of customers. Amongst an open-
air loving community the show has a busy
summer in front of it.

At Sydney picture-theatres this week :
Crystal Palace : “The Fighting Chance” —a five-
act sermon by Robert W. Chambers on the text that
the right woman can dp more to discourage constant
shicker than all the Early Closing and No-Liquor
Acts. The trouble is that woman is a mere side-line
when the shicker calls; but that point isn’t
discussed.
Empress: “The Double Trap” and “The Hidden
Voice” —a couple more exciting chapters in “The
Clutching Hand” series.
Lyric: “A Woman with a Past”—a first-class Nor-
disk film recounting the thrilling adventures of a
young woman who fell into the clutches of a gang
of crooks and became their cat’s-paw. The gang
was finally scratched for all engagements.
IXI “The Girl in the Taxi” at Melbourne
Majesty’s W. H. Rawlins, the Baron Dauv-
ray of the first Melbourne production, is
again demonstrating how he delights to lark
and skite under the influence of frail ladies
and fizzy refreshment; and Fred Maguire
and Paul Plunket, as his son and nephew
respectively, continue to emulate the
ancient’s expensive giddiness. On Saturday
Maguire shared a fancy supper with a new
Suzanne, Miss ' Dorothy Brunton, whose
manner, however, rather dolefully suggested
that more than sufficient unto the evening
was the Maguire thereof. Two a.m. frivo-
lity is not Miss Brunton’s forte; nor is
Miss Vie particularly fitted for the part of a
baroness whose hair has turned white from
respectability. Field Fisher drones in the
old part of the elderly waiter who tends
young sinners till all is blue ; and Workman
makes up like an imminent bilious attack
as the wealthy scent-manufacturer from
Tours. Alfred Frith and Miss Gwen Hughes
appear as Monsieur and Madame Charcot,
and the only moral difference between the
two is that, while hubby is never found out,
his frisky missus is never found in. The
dancing of Miss Daisy Yates and her part-
ner, Sydney, is a blight feature of a revival
which is only for seven nights and concludes
on Saturday. Next week Her Majesty’s
will be closed to provide a free stage for the
numerous people who are preparing for the
“Mother Goose” opening on the 18th inst.
Arnold Bennett’s “What the Public
Wants” was honored with another revival
by the Repertory amateurs at Melbourne
Athenaeum on Saturday. Bennett’s attitude
to the Worgan sort of journalism, revealed
ia a satire that is ever laughing and urbane,
makes the comedy a nopular entertainment
for the ordinary bloke, whilst affording the
superiah person the satisfaction of hearing
his prejudices formulated in piercing mots.
The dialogue throughout is natural and
clever, and a good company headed by the
hard-working McMahon gave full play to
its axioms and arguments on Saturday night.
McMahon repeated his study of Sir Charles
Worgan, and showed all the aspects of a
Philistine who has the ironical job of sav-
ing real artists from starvation. Fx*ancis
and John Worgan (Helton Daniell and
Hugh Rose) suggested xesthetes somewhat
chastexied in their mental arrogance by the
magnificent commercialism of their brother,
while Norman Penair and Reg Newson gave
due weight to the differing vulgarities of
Saul Kendrick and James Brindley. The
financial helplessness of a mostiy-unpaid
actress was sincerely pictured by Miss Rob-
son ; and Mrs. Fowler and Miss Edith
Fraser satisfactorily indicated the main
features of Mrs. and Annie Worgan l’espec-
tively. In the first act artist David Low
made an emphatic debut in the role of the
dramatic critic, Simon Macquoid; and poet

The doings of a crook and his accomplice
at a wedding party, complicated by the in-
sanities of two kleptomaniacs, a few appal-
ling police, and various uncontrollable human
sundries, are the features of the short-lived
tumult which arrived at Melbourne Royal
on Saturday. “Stop Thief” enjoys a career
of less than two hours, but in that short
time it affords abundant evidence for the
complaint that the Amurkan sense of the
ridiculous is more stirred by mechanical
comedy-business than by honest humor.
There is nothing reasonably laughable in the
notion of a rogue trying to dodge detection
by assuming a bogus identity and sec etly
dropping misappropriated valuables in the
pockets of innocent bystanders. The joke
goes too far, with the result that the bulk
of the characters lose all their small pre-
tensions to reality and become the mereiy
indifferent victims of a sleight-of-hand per-
former. There is one really funny happening
in the piece, however, and that occurs when
the generally idle and useless larcenist pauses
in the act of thieving to assure his donah
solemnly that he has noble ideals and a grow-
ing feeling for the Better Life. This little
passage shows that even Yankee farce-makers
are never so absorbed in the imbecilities of
their puppets as to forget to remind the
populace that there are sermons in drones,
books in the cunning crooks, and good in
everything.
* * *
John Webster, as tlie boss embezzler,
bustles through the part on a pair off. legs
that seem to be more sensitive than Ins
visage to the preposterous situations he is
endeavoring to create. Miss Beatrice Nichoils
easily convinces the audience that a mask
of innocent humility and a tailor-made are
all the visible requirements needed for the
part of his lady help, Nell. J. I’lumpton
Wilson appears as the foolish old gentleman
whose absence of mind isn’t compensated
for by the presence of any other rational
quality. Gerald Harcourt, as the ancient s
prospective son-i n-law, talks so hurriedly
that his words seem to collide and splinter in
the process. Tom McLarnie is chief of the
police gang, and enforces law-n’-order with a
face like a rock. Common feminine sounds
ranging from hysterics to school-girl giggles
are contributed' to the general disorder by
Miss Marion Marcus Clarke, Miss Elma
Royton. Miss Mattee Brown, and Miss
Fanchon Campbell; and Harry _ Sweeney
plays the minor part of a doctor in an ap-
propriately inhuman way. “Stop Thief _is
.only booked for a six-niglits’ riot. “Ivick-in
is the bill for Saturday.

powers of persuasion go for naught until
he brings his faithful warble to Blarney’s
aid. A song from the Sojer invariably suc-
ceeds in having the desired effect. He sings
her hostility to sleep, as it Jvere. and the

Mary Law, violinist, and Peter Dawson,
baritone, still head the bill at Melbourne
Tivoli. Mary is now giving some of the
choicest items in her repertoire, while Peter
is singing with all his old meticulous atten-
tion to details, In the rest of the pro-
gramme the Nolans, Hazel Moran, Coy De
Trickey, Mile. Nadje, Morris Golden, and
Jack La Vier continue to give every satis-
faction.
* * * *
The art of tumbling from high altitudes
without visible damage continues to be the
trade secret of Montambo and Wells, the
clever pair of balancers at Melbourne Bijou.
Of the new artists Kipp and Kippy have
come in with a comedy juggling turn ; a lady
named Orphea is responsible for an interest-
ing musical act; and Miss Carne has the
job of imitating Daisy Jerome and other
celebrities.
* * * #
Before returning to Sydney for a final
fortnight in Australia, Muriel Starr pur-
sued Millward through the four acts of
“Under Cover” in Adelaide. The machine-
made American play gave her a good chance
to run through ail the emotions she ex-
presses naturally; but her dazzling smile had
fewer chances. Adelaide said good-bye with
regret. The Royal is closed for the moment.
At the Tivoli is what would have been
called in old days a “stock” company, very
capable, supporting various visitors in all
kinds of plays. Conway and Fitzherbert
are, as a matter of fact, quite well able to
divide the “lead” without any visiting help,
but just now the forceful Harry Roberts
is along with the forceful American-Civil
military play, “Held by the Enemy.” Pos-
sibly everybody concerned is a shade too
deliberate; at any rate, the old play did not
the time the writer saw it thrill the audi-
ence in the old way. At the King’s, Har-
rington Reynolds introduces a playlet,
Bailey’s dogs seem really to feel the poses
they adopt, Alice Pierce brilliantly shows
how Miss Starr and other stage favorites
get their effects, and Alsace makes excellent
music with Lorraine. Pictures are “intense”
just now, “The Pib” at Wondergraph, “The
Sbulamite” at West’s.
So far Tasmania has the distinction of
being the one State in Australia which has
not sent a single member of Parliament to
the Front or anywhere near it.
Died last week, Tom Boulton (83), of
Nambucca, who blazed the first track
through the then giant forests between the
Macleay and the Nambucca, and thereafter
spent a useful half-century in the rich N. S.
Wales North Coast district.
AT POVERTY POINT.
The Australian National Miniature
Theatre, whose home is out at Niagaroon,
Oakleigli (Vic.), is devoting the evenings of the
17th and 18th inst. to the production of
three one-acters “introducing hitherto un-
approached subjects.” The pieces are “Ex-
periment,” “The Moon Child” and “The Re-
turn,” and they are alleged to deal respec-
tively with a “study in Christian Science,”
“a fantasy woven around an aboriginal
legend,” and a certain damsel who “is will-
ing to go into Hell with her eyes open rather
than any longer endure the loneliness of
the bush and the whims of a truculent
father.” No charge will be made for admis-
sion. All that the intending spectator re-
quires is an enthusiasm strong enough to
survive the train service between Mel! ne
and Oakleigli.
A memory of the late ARTHUR RUSSELL,
of Hoyt's (Melbourne).
“S.S.”: The luck of Arthur Russell.
Arthur was a dentist by acquired profession,
a showman by born inclination. How the
Bohemian got into his blood I don’t know,
but even while making plenty of money in
the public’s mouth he was always planning
shows to take its eye and ear. His weak-
ness was for the tent and a nomadic life,
carrying marvels to the Backblocks, the
Pacific Islands and waste places. He ran
many shows that failed to take on till he
got into the picture business, established
Hoyt’s, made a fortune, and, that being
assured, died a comparatively young man at
51. I’ve known nine men go out like that,
and they still say nothing succeeds like
success.
Lance-corporal Charles Murfitt, who re-
ceived the D.C.M. for restoring telephone
communication under heavy fire, earned men-
tion for distinguished conduct when Maeter-
linck’s “Blue Bird” was first staged in this
part of the world. He was in charge of the
wonderful electrical effects of that produc-
tion; and after the “Blue Bird” . company
disbanded was connected with various Aus-
tralian theatres.
“J.F.11.” : G. Tt. Sims provided the late
George Edwardos with several of his early
experiments. ()ne of them was “Blue-Eyed
Susan,” in which Nellie Stewart played the
title role. Sims and his collaborator, Henry
Pettitt, had diligently rehearsed it, and
having seen it, as they thought, safely and
successfully launched, went away for a holi-
day. In a few days they received this mes-
sage from Edwardes: “ ‘Blue-Eyed Susan’
is not going well. It wants a lot of work
on it to make it right. If you don’t come
back within a week and see to it I shall put
up the notice.” They hurried back, but too
late to put Susan straight. That was
characteristic of Nellie Stewart’s luck in
London. She never really got a chance to
display her abilities. She was the innocent
victim of a whole chapter of cruelly-provok-
ing accidents and disappointments. Her
one striking success was as principal boy in
a pantomime at Drury Lane, in which she
had Dan Leno as coadjutor. But ill-luck
pursued her even there, as she broke down
under the strain of two performances a day
in the depth of a severe London winter.
Lily Langtry is not quite so beautiful as
she was in the long ago; still there are
plenty of people who will flock to see an
elderly actress who was once wheeled in a
barrow at a fashionable continental resort
by a gay heir to the throne, of the British
Empire. So Mrs. Langtry has been telling
the London interviewers that she has had
offers of two autumn tours. The first would
take her to India, Burmah, China, Japan,
the Malay States and the Philippines. The
second would be another visit to the United
States. On thinking them over she had
come to the conclusion that, while the
former was the more attractive on the score
of novelty, it was rather too big a proposi-
tion in tliese troublous times; so she has
plumped for six months’ touring under the
Stars and Stripes.
“Gin Fiz”: Fred Graham, who lingered
about Australian theatres until be was prac-
tically told to get out, has found his feet in
TJ.S.A. He got the chance of playing prin-
cipal part in a mix-up labelled “Search Me,”
and the first-night audience marked him up
as a discovery. Also doing well in and
around New York is Leo Stark, who, though
advised not to leave Australia, took a chance
and, strange to say, dropped into a part in
“The Spring Maid” that suited his peculiar
wooden style. Since then his mechanical
walk is spoken of as “The Stark skip,” and
is actually imitated !
“Melb”: The Williamson picture-theatre
in Bourke-street, opposite the Royal, will
be ready in a few weeks. The job is only
one of alterations. The firm lias just built
a second and much larger wooden cinema
studio, on (he site for the new Comedy
Theatre, opposite Her Majesty’s. This in-
dicates that the Comedy Theatre is hung up.
'.Meanwhile the new Empire Picture Theatre,
Bourke-street East, proceeds more slowly.
It springs from a deep foundation. Nine
Bourke-street picture-theatres to be open
every night!
“Nibbidard”: Jo Smith, the writer of
several well-known Australian plays,'who is
a successful furniture-warehouse manager in
private life, has set a shining example to the
sharks who, masquerading as patriots, pro-
mote entertainments which yield £3O for
wounded soldiers and a crop of “expenses” of
twice that amount. On September 29 Smith
staged at the St. Ivilda Town Hall his
comedy. “Before the Dawn,” proceeds to go
to the Wounded Soldiers’ Fund. Here is the
balance-sheet, duly attested : Receipts—Sale
of tickets and admissions, £ll7 95.; sale of
sweets, etc., £ll 9s. lid.; total, £l2B 18s.
lid.; expenditure, Nil; profit, £l2B 18s. lid.
“Before the Dawn” will be repeated at St.
Kilda Town Hall on December 15 for the
Red Cross, and at the Auditorium (Melb.)
on December IS, to provide winter comforts
for Private Jim of Gallipoli.

The special Christmas number of The
Bulletin will be on the bookstalls and in
the streets on Saturday. As far as this
paper is a disinterested judge, the number is
all right. There isn’t anything about the
war in it; in fact, Souter is the only writer
who even has a story in it built on the
war. Souter —the black-and-white artist
who invented the cat —has a son who was
very early in the trouble; and that may
account for the two or three excellent war-
stories he has already done in The
Bulletin, and the best of the lot is in the
Christmas number. There are other yarns
to make up 20, and an exceptionally fine lot
of verse, including “The, Fable of a Cell,”
one of the strongest pieces of grim humor
written in these parts for many a day.
“SHOP!”
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Ike Israels is a business man,
Pawnbroking is his trade ;
And, like most members of his clan,
He takes his pleasure when he can
In seeing dramas played ;
A patron of the gods is he,
A gallery-critic frank and free.
“Carried away” quite oft is Ike,
To interject lie’s fain ;
Particularly does he like
Commercial scenes, or those that strike
Haply a bithneth vein ;
Show him a bargain or a deal —
Never in vain “shop” made appeal!
One night he watched the starving star
Come on the stage alone—
His relative avuncular
Lived near (these rich old uncles are
In stage-plays not unknown) ;
The hero carried, please to note,
Over his arm an overcoat.
“Uncle!” he moaned, the while were played
Some dismal minor chords —
“Uncle!” Upon his knees he prayed;
The overcoat meanwhile he- laid
Beside him on the boards.
Ike couldn’t sit still, though he tried—
“Veil, how much do y’ vant?” he cried.

Australia’s Cinderella, the Northern Ter-
ritory, is still scarcely regarded as one of
the family by her bie sisters down south.
Blank ignorance, reinforced by utter indif-
ference, is the attitude of the average Vic.
or N. S. Wales citizen towards the Terri-
tory and all that therein is. One “merchant
prince” in Sydney doesn’t even know the
difference between a N.T. telegraph station
and a nice girl. During bush-parson Wil-
kinson’s two years’ journey through the
Territory baekblocks he sent from Powell’s
Creek an order for goods to a Sydney firm,
mentioning that the package was to be
addressed to Alice Springs. When he
reached the Springs there was no sign of
the parcel, though ample time had elapsed.
A wire to Sydney brought the reply, “Goods
sent as directed.” Months afterwards, on
his return to Powell’s Creek, he found the
missing package waiting there, addressed
“Miss Alice Springs, c/o Rev. W. M. Wil-
kinson, Powell’s Creek, N.T.”
* * * *
“Moana”: Lunacy emerged from hiding
in Maoriland the other day when several
allegedly Scawtch Societies gave vent to a
demand for a “Special Regiment,” garbed
in kilts, to be sent to argue with the Turk.
The howl for the sporran is still rumbling
round Volcanoland, one of the main argu-
ments used in its favor being that it would
greatly increase recruiting, as “thousands
of Scots of picked physique” would rush to
assist the Empire if they knew they could
do the assisting in a kilt. If this tale be
true, and if there really are thousands of
Scotties in Loan Land who haven’t enlisted
simply because they can’t wear a certain
weird brand of millinery, the sooner Massey
goes after them with the axe of conscription
and teaches them the meaning of patriotism
the better.

“Andy ’O.”: “Sniggs” (B. 11/11/T5)
divorces poor lluon from Kermadec, who
were one and the same party, coeval and
indivisible. lluon Kermadec was second in
command on the Esperance to Admiral
D’Entrecasteaux on the Recherche. The
expedition, which set out in 1791 to locate
La Perouse, discovered the channel which
separates Bruni Island from the Tasmanian
mainland, and it was named after the
Admiral. The ves-
sels got into the
channel in error,
owing to a mistaken
bearing from the
Mewstone Roc k.
D’Entretcetera was
ill in bunk at the
time, so hardly de-
served any fame in
this connection. The
channel was explored
and charted, and the
broad lluon River
discovered and given
the name of the sec-
ond-in-command. A
rivulet, tributary to
the Muon near Gee-
veston, was named
Kermadec, now in-
explicably corrupted
to Kermande e.
Apropos, two fine
ports just south of
the lluon were
named Recherche
and Esperance, after
the two ships. The
former is now better
known as Research,
and people think
you are a new kind
of Hun if you use
the correct Proven-
cal. It is true
that D’Entre-and-
the-rest called the
Derwent “Riviere de
Nord,” but he only
sailed the estuary.
It was left to Hayes
(afterwards Admiral
Sir John) to explore
the river. He charted
it in 1794 to a point
three or four miles
above Bridgewater,
say 14 miles from Hobart, and named it
after the Derwentwater of his own loved
Westmoreland.

The man who makes the translation
swears that the extract from the Bltjd-
tiiuh.st und Splutter goes this way :
Let us not be deceived. Australia is organising
for war with remarkable thoroughness. Three months
ago the call for men was heard at Rockhampton,
and seven citizens of military age at once sprang to
arms. They could have reached a training camp in
a few hours by train, but on the advice of the local
newspaper decided to use their influence in securing
further drafts of soldiery. They therefore at once
set out by road for Cairns, where their numbers were
swollen by two, and after being banqueted the
regiment set its face to the west, and with songs
and cheers started on a march to Cloncurry, Camoo-
weal, Darling Downs, Cooktown and Gympie. On
leaving Gympie they were 14 strong, and crossed
the border into N. S. Wales after losing only six,
who had worn their feet off. The survivors traversed
the Diamantina, visited the basin of the Darling,
KNEW THE SYMPTOMS.
“ George, dear, yon really get handsomer
every day.”
“Yes, I know. It’s a habit I have when
you want a new hat.”
passed safely through Canberra, Sydney, Tamworth
and Cootamundra, where the whole population turned
out with sandwiches, and forded the Murray after
being joined by 12 comrades. Thence they marched
by way of Bendigo and Albury to Melbourne, which
was entered with bands playing and flags waving
on the public buildings, the total on the roll being
28. The men were at once hurried into camp, and
after treatment in hospital it was expected that they
would be ready to start learning drill in about seven
weeks. The compulsory service leagues have been
completely routed by this exposure of the hollow
character of their demands, and the principle of
encouraging the voluntary taking of prolonged walks
is regarded as thoroughly justified. These men will
reach the Front some day. We must recognise this,
and be prepared to hear of more following.
Meanwhile if the Minister for Defence is
permitted by the Censor to open his mouth
except for the purpose of commending an
officer, it would be just as well if he made an
announcement regarding the usefulness of
dissipating a vast amount of energy for the
mere purpose of showing the enemy that we
are handling things as though a circus agent
was in supreme command.
* * * *
“Ex-Gent”; The Maoriland Minister of
Education has lately been waited on by a
deputation worried by the increasing scarcity
of school-teachers. The position is really
acute, and some Outback districts are con-
sidering the advisability of enlarging the
school-house doors, so that the local John
Hop can get his feet in and give the neces-
sary insthruction. Married as well as single
teachers are going, and who can blame them ?
Existence on the frostiest chunk of Bul-
garian rock is gay compared with the school-
master’s life at Maori Flat or Rimu Gully.
On the other hand, there are the young un-
married farmers who won’t go, and the
bachelor solicitors who are too fully occu-
pied counting the deeds in their strong-rooms
to think of shifting, and the wifeless archi-
tects praying for the firebell to ring, and
any number of people whom the Loan Land
could readily spare. Yet the muddle of
voluntaryism goes cheerfully on.
“Billy Blue”: Dr.
Watson, the Com-
monwealth Historian,
in the fourth volume
of the Records, re-
vives an ancient tale ;
and this historian
herewith carries it on
a few years. Gov-
ernor 'William Bligh
arrived in N. S. Wales
on August 8, 1806,
to succeed Philip Gid-
ley King. Two days
later King issued to
Bligh a grant of 1000
acres, near where
Riverstone is now (it
was called Copen-
hagen), another grant
of 240 acres called
Camperdown (now the
Sydney suburb of
that name) and 105
acres elsewhere.
Bligh’s daughter, Mrs.
Mary Rutland, was
also provided with a
grant of 600 acres.
Bligh assumed the job
of Governor on the
13th, and at once pro-
ceeded to hand out
land to the Kings.
One grant of 700 acres
to Mrs. Anna Josepha
King she very ap-
propriately called
“Thanks Farm.” The
magnitude of this
little transaction can
be judged by the re-
sult. In May, 1841,
the trustees of Gover-
nor Bligh’s estate
held two sales of the
Camperdown land, and
234 acres were sold in
allotments for £36,001,
an average of £154
per acre. The King
grants, including
“Thanks Farm,” re-
main in the King
family. Mrs. Mary
Rutland’s grant was
subdivided and sold,
and the town of St.
Mary’s is built on the
site. “A mere inter-
change of courtesies”
between the two Gov-
ernors, one of the
Kings commented air-
ily on the original
transaction. Justice
Pring, the most prom-
inent modern connec-
tion of the family,
would probably call it
something else if a similar courtesy came
under his keen judicial nose.
* * * *
Adelaide’s first two woman “patrols”—
they don’t wish to be called police—started
their job on December 1. They propose to
do most of their work between 5 and 11 p.m.,
and give special attention to the precocious
under-16 girl who stays out late. All women
offenders in the police-court they will give
advice and help to —with one exception. “In
any instance where a woman attempts to
snare or trap a young girl we shall show no
leniency.” Chief Secretary Styles has got
Lis new Joan Hops from the State Chil-
dren’s Dept., and intends to add to their
strength almost at once.
* * * »
Melbourne has a Peace Alliance. Affili-
ated therewith are the Sisterhood of Inter-
national Peace, the Peace Army, the Social-
ist Party, the Women Teachers’ Associa-
tion, the Federated Clerks’ Union, and the
Typists’ Association. It looks quite a for-
midable aggregation of societies. But it is
really only painted dough. The affiliation
of the Clerks’ Union was the result of a
catch vote at a meeting attended by five mem-
bers or so and the caretaker. The same
Clerks’ Union, under similar conditions,
resolved not long ago to knock off eat-
ing butter as a protest against the
drought. The Women Teachers’ tail is
wagged by the Women’s Political Ass. The
typists’ non-union is a feeble little clattering
thing. The Charles Strongs, of the Aus-
tralian Church, are the main works of the
Sisterhood of International Peace; the
Peace Army is Vida Goldstein; and most
people are aware of the immense influence
which isn’t wielded by the Socialist Party.
It all recalls J. C. Kirby, a well-known
“social-purity” parson of S. Aus., who once
confessed that at that moment lie was, as
near as he could remember, seven societies.
What the Melbourne Peace Alliance really
should do is to meet regularly once a week
and knit a pair of socks. These would
keep at least one soldier warm in the com-
ing winter and help him to be thus fit to
fight on the side which stands for peace in
our time. That extra soldier might make
all the difference between an early settle-
ment and an extended conflict.
THE RIDDLE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
To be really smart we must wear our hats at an
angle which obscures most of our profile and a collar
that nearly hides our mouth. —Fashion note.
Is her face so fair to see ?
Who shall answer? AVho can tell?
Hear her laughter ripple free!
Is that mouth of hers a sell ?
Look ! A wisp of saucy hair
Beats the straw, eludes the veil —
Is it dark or is it fair?
Is she pink or sweetly pale?
Light her walk, erect and strong
Are her -movements as site goes—•
Has she lashes curved and long
Leaning towards her slender nose?
Girl, this fashion’s modest rule
Is a swindle and a cheat;
Such a hat may keep you cool,
Such a collar may be neat,
But I hold it shame and sin
That a girl should tease my taste,
Letting eyes and lips and chin
Simply go to heedless waste.
It is really past a joke.
Girl, that hat-tilt irks me so;
Laugh once more, and you’ll provoke
Me to lift your hat and know!
x.s.w.

FOR the past quarter of a century in New Zealand most readers of’ the Triad
have been readers of Tiie Bulletin, and pretty soon now (as things go) most
readers of The Bulletin in Australia will be readers of the Triad. The
Triad has no prejudices. It is the helot of no party or clique. It is not a slave of
reputations. It says what it thinks and explains why it thinks it.
The Triad for December is full of good things. There are many pages of out-
spoken criticism. There is a seasoning of frank fun. The literary contributions
are by writers well known in these pages—such as Randolph Bedford, Hugh
McCrae, Will Lawson, Frank Morton and M. Forrest. The Triad offends lots of
people, and is heartily glad of it. It never offends tolerant people of good will and
intelligence. You can buy it for sixpence at any newsagent’s in Australasia. Or you
can send six-and-sixpence to the Manager, Box 1558, Sydney, and have it posted
to you for a year. A specimen copy post free for Id. stamp. This is an offer that
should be taken now. The Triad stands to all present bargains, even if the most
patriotic and democratic Commonwealth Government puts up the postage on news-
papers to a guinea an ounce.

“T. 0.1.”: The Burdekin mansion in Mac-
quarie street, Sydney, has changed hands at
£40,000, Alexander Hay being the buyer. It
was erected in the first ’so’s by Thomas Bur-
dekin, a Sydney ironmonger who arrived in
1826 and established a business in George-
street, which may be considered the pilot-fish
of that conducted afterwards by Joseph Bur-
dekin Holdsworth, and now by Holdsworth,
Macpherson and Co. There was a legend
that Tom Burdekin built partly on someone
rise’s land, and had to give compensation.
He died in the mid-forties, leaving three sons.
One was Marshall Burdekin, a barrister, who
was “called” in 1859. lie was appointed
< 'olonial Treasurer in the Cowper Ministry,
hut was defeated on going up for re-election.
A second son, Jon. Bossley Burdekin, did not
take any part in politics ; but the third, Syd-
ney, was in Parliament 14 years, and in
the drawing-room of tl)e Burdekin mansion in
those days old Henry Parkes presided over
many a Liberal caucus.
Australia’s importing mania is becoming
better known in Woodrow Wilson’s country.
A Yank has just written to the Melbourne
City Council offering, for cash in advance,
“to place in its parks” one or any number of
buffaloes at from £BO to £6O each; black
bears at 35 to 50 dollars; coyotes, lynx,
beavers, badgers and Eskimo dogs at from 10
to 100 dollars. He will even let Melbourne
have a few rabbits at two dollars a
pair, white rats at 25 a hundred, and
skunks at a reduced figure for taking a
quantity. No business has been done so
far; but if he could offer one of his country’s
dirt-eaters, cheap, he might book an order
any time this summer.
The sworn testimony of an ex-curate, now
in vaudeville :—•
These faked Archbishops of Canterbury now
crowning false-alarm queens all over the suburbs
remind me that, of all the actors I ever knew Julius
Stormer had the most exalted idea of his profession.
Stormer looked upon life as a vast tragedy put on
by the Creator, and he held that very few of the
characters in history had made the best dramatic
use of their opportunities. Stormer regarded most
kings and bishops merely as indifferent actors who
were blind to the possibilities of their parts. He
told me one day he couldn’t understand why the
Fathers of the Church wasted their time preaching
dull, stumbling sermons to indifferent congregations
when they might be receiving the homage of their
curates or making picturesque pilgrimages to shrines
within easy walking distance of their cathedrals.
When someone objected that Australia, being' a new
country, had no shrines, Stormer retorted that they,
could he put up quite cheaply. One day a heavy-
weight bishop who wanted to learn how to elocute
without artificial teeth, because he considered arti-
ficial teeth an infringement of Providence’s patent
rights, was directed by evil chance to Stormer’s
studio, where he got on quite nicely until Stormer
ventured the opinion that Moses was a fine actor.
The bishop stiffened in his gaiters and begged
Stormer to explain himself. Stormer then said he
regarded Moses as a man who wouldn’t have been
out of place in a good stock company alongside
Pilate and Herod. While the bishop fought for
breath, Stormer congratulated- his visitor on having
at last realised his opportunities. He was- going
gaily ahead and begging the dignitary to allow him
to take a service for him when he became conscious
of a swollen umbrella pointing at the door. Behind
it was the bishop, looking very ripe and ready to
explode. Stormer hastily flung the door wide and
the Right Reverend gentleman, without any sounds
or motions of farewell, floated slowly and majesti-
cally down the stairs like a large ecclesiastical
balloon.

There are still some perfect gentlemen in
the world —or were till the other day. One
of them, because he hated to give trouble,
decided that it was really necessary to shoot
himself; and he walked to the Mortuary to
do it. A while ago another man bought a lot
at Rooltwood and had a grave digged. The
morning after the hole was completed he
was found lying in it, dead. But the Law
would waste money; instead of taking the
obvious hint and just shovelling in the earth,
it dragged him out and removed him to wait
for an inquest.

“Hamer”: The weary old argument about
what a public place is broke out again
this time at Wentworth (N.S.W.).
Two rival pastpralists attended a land sale
at Endfield, near Pooncarie, and there one
of them announced his objections to the
shape of the other’s nose, and was subse-
quently fined for using threatening lan-
a public place, and bound over to
keep the peace. His appeal was heard at
the Wentworth Quarter Sessions, the con-
tention being that, as the sale was over, the
scene of the language had ceased to be a
public place. Judge Bevan, however, held
that the invitation to the public to attend
made the spot “a place within the meaning”
until the invitation lapsed through the with-
drawal of the people. It cost the appellant
£lO 10s. to find this out. Possibly he has
money left to dispute it in a yet higher
court. There are any number of legal
gentlemen who will help him to get the
point settled for a couple of days before
sending it on its rounds again.
* * * *
“O’Phimerty”: When German cruisers
were loose on the seas some persons on the
Tasmanian West Coast noticed mysterious
lights flashing across the peaks of Zeehan
and Heemskirk. In Hobart it was thought
that one or more of the piratical cruisers
might be hiding round about Port Davey,
where there are several deep-water inlets
screened from the sea by headlands, and lying
against miles of wild, uninhabited country.
As no Australian warships were available
for purposes of reconnoitre it was decided
to make a hunt from the land side, and Gib-
lin, M.H.A., undertook the job. With two
other bushmen he tramped westward from
Tyenna over about 80 miles of the roughest
country in the Commonwealth, and by the
aid of carrier pigeons reported that not a
war punt or battle garbage-tin could be dis-
covered anywhere. But that didn’t affect
the merit of the strenuous job carried
through by Giblin and his two bushmen
mates.
From the Wireless Operator:—•
The ordinary range of a ship’s apparatus is about
300 miles in daylight, and well over 1000 at night
time. Why it is so much better after dark is one
of the marvels which cannot yet be fully explained.
One night when east of Rarotonga I heard San
E’rancisco and Sydney stations within an hour of
each other. This could be done only with exception-
ally suitable conditions.
On a ship I was with between Wellington and
Sydney a passenger was suddenly taken ill. We
“THE ANSWER."
“My poor man, you must he cold. If I give
yon five shillings will you get some
clothes ?”
“ Madame , male it seven, and we’ll buy a
sheepskin vest and save a soldier .”
had no doctor aboard and were three days from
Sydney. We called up a steamer, and our captain
diagnosed the case by air, and their doctor pre-
scribed. The patient was treated accordingly, and
we kept in touch for a few hours, reporting different
symptoms. The shore doctor told us we had saved
the man’s life.
Just after the outbreak of war, when German
battleships and other boats were at large, wireless
work was of great importance, and business was
brisk. Owing to atmospherics the telegraphists at
then-lately-captured Rabaul were having trouble get-
ting their traffic to Port Moresby'. (Atmospherics
are disturbances caused by stray electricity in the
atmosphere.) The operator on the German boat
Ivomet, which was taken later, was making condi-
tions worse by working at the same time, which
causes jambing. He would say, in a good Austra-
lian off-handed way, “Come, now, old man, let me
help you”; or, “You are having trouble. I am
closer than you, let me relay it for you.” He actu-
ally did send all cipher messages he picked up to
Tsingtau.
Wo were trooping, and in the Indian Ocean I
heard a ship’s station asking for anyone within
range to answer, as her chronometers appeared to
them to be going wrong. The matter was urgent.
A chronometer four minutes out means the ship is
a degree (or about 60 miles) out of her course. I
agreed to give this ship certain signals every fif-
teenth second, taking the time from our chrono-
meters. She was one minute nine seconds slow in
her time.,- We saved the people on that ship great
anxiety, perhaps the boat itself.

There is always the other way of looking
at things. A couple of Red Cross canvas-
sers after “gifts in kind” called on Mrs.
Cocky the other day, and as she seemed a
bit hard in the mouth, they casually men-
tioned that Mrs. Hayseed, further up the
creek, had given some vegetables. “And so
she should,” said the lady. “Hasn’t she got
two boys at the Front?”
* *■ * *
Ford’s peace mission seems likely to meet
many blizzards. The Kaiser is supposed to
have said that he doesn’t want the motor-
man on the premises, as Alphonso of Spain
- —the tall potentate with the large mouth —
is near at hand to carry any olive branches
or shoo on any doves that may be needed.
Woodrow Wilson, who has gone out of the
peace business lately, is not smiling on the
scheme; and Britain, knitting socks and
hammering cannon like the deuce, merely
remarks that she will hammer peace into
Germany in the old way. Before long Ford
will begin to realise that it is easier to
reason with a refractory motor-car than talk
Christian brotherhood to a swarm of angry
nations. But for the present the picnic
hampers are well supplied, and the man
who supplies the cash has plentv of friends
t > tell him that he is the greatest man in
the world.

“Brandy has killed more men than
shrapnel,” says a prominent Melbourne
Rechabite.
Though shrapnel kill a thousand score,
Old brandy will kill more and more.
The hint, belligerents, is plain—
Get back to grapeshot once again.
VENUS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Venus has not been noticed for many months.
The beautiful evening star has once more arrived,
and can now be glimpsed by sharp eyes in the even-
ing glow. —Astronomical note.
1 how my head that I had been
So careless, so indifferent, .
As not to note the heavens’ queen
Was absent from the firmament;
But I do know, to my delight,
The star of Venus still is bright!
It may be other things have held
My thoughts; the ruddy glow of Mars
Has drawn me, or my mind was spelled
By Saturn, or by other stars ;
But that which gladdens every night,
The Star of Venus, still is bright!
It may be seen in evening’s glow ;
More radianQ. in night’s dearer hours.
When love’s sweet dreams are all we know,
Its splendor in the heavens- towers.
Life, let us love! The earth’s all right:
The star of Venus still is bright!
In spite of wars and many things,
. (O full red lips for kisses, fit!)
Man’s fancy to one fealty clings,
With one dear star his sky is lit.
Though kingdoms fall, and nations fight,
The star of Venus still is bright!
N.S.W.

PERSONAL
ITEMS
Died in harness at the age of 72, Joseph
Win lei', proprietor of Melbourne Advocate,
and brother of the late S. V. Winter, fondly
known as “Stormy,” one time managing
director of Melbourne Herald. Joseph,
who was born at Goulburn (N.S.W.), was
an ardent Irish Home Ruler, and in 35
years collected £40,000 for the cause. He
took over the conduct of the Advocate, a
I loly Roman organ very well patronised,
after it had been founded by S.Y. It was
in the editor’s chair that he collapsed, dying
before the doctors could arrive. He Was
largely interested in the Herald, and at
shareholders’ meetings his voice was gener-
ally heard on behalf of “old and trusted
servants of the company” and the conser-
vative point of view.
William Alexander Long, who died in
Sydney last week after a weary illness, was
son of the late “Bill” Long, and was born
in Sydney in the ’3o’s. In the ’so’s he was
sent to England and returned a barrister,
having been called at the Inner Temple in
1802; but he never sought briefs. He ber
longed to a family too rich to be bothered
with other people's troubles for a fee.
“Young Bill” entered the Assembly in 1575
for Central Cumberland, and in the next
Parliament sat for Parramatta. He was
Treasurer in the Robertson Government,
A HEAVY DRINKER.
THE DISTRACTED FATHER: “I wish he'd
follow the King.”
1877 ; his brother-in-law, W. B. Dailey, who
was Attorney-General, dragged him in. Go-
ing to tlie Leg. Council in IS>5. he remained
there till 1909. In his prime he had large
interests in the A.J.S. Bank, and was chair-
man for a time. The bank was unluckv :
and so was \V. A. Long. He lost a fortune
on the turf, and in his old age was no doubt
thankful for an annuity of £5OO left him
by his cousin, AY. E. Sparke.
That remarkable man, Thomas Cherry,
who occupies the Chair of Agriculture at
Melbourne University, is a versatile don.
He has an interest in the Cherry Churns,
well known to most Victorian farmers ; is the
author of the standard work on Australian
agriculture; owns an M.D. degree; keeps an
extensive and profitable dairy-herd on his
estate at Glen Iris, and owns or leases odd
lots of grazing land all over that district;
and is one of the world’s authorities on
artificial manures and their production. Of
the junior Cherries, the riper son is in khaki,
while the less mature lad last year put up
a record as the youngest student to become
dux of Scotch College in the history of that
elderly institution.
Adelaide’s R. Barr Smith left a comfort-
able £40,000 to charities. Practically every
ur.sectarian benevolence in Adelaide gets from
£lOOO to £3OOO. There is a thoughtful gift
Jo the Destitute Asylum, the income to be
“applied in providing entertainment or
amusement for the inmates”; and the S.A.
Agricultural Society gets £lOOO to supply
annual prizes “for the best dogs or horses
whose tails or ears have not been mutilated.”

Andrew Fisher dropped in at Kalgoorlie
last week, after doing the 1000 miles from
Fort Augusta. The east-west railway has
still a gap of nearly 350 miles in its middle
and most awkward section. The first 100
miles from the railhead at the South Aus.
end were covered by camel buggy, and a
motor-car came out and took him the other
250.
“Lee Oh’s” hail:—
I tow in Captain Matthew T. Clayton, of Auck-
land (M.L.), for the Most Ancient Mariner compe-
tition. He is 84, and it is 70 years since he first
took a trick at the wheel. He served his time in
one of the old East Indiamen, and sailed the Pacific
when all the adventurers of the world were fighting
their way to California, and ’Frisco was a collec-
tion of shanties on a sand-hill. In ’4B he was
apprentice on one of Bobby Towns’s barques out of
Sydney, the old Statesman, and went sandalwooding
in her through the New Hebrides, and along the
coast of New Caledonia, long before the tricolor flew
there. That was the glorious day for the trader.
Beads and knives bought hundreds of tons of sandal-
wood for tile China market. Clayton afterwards
ran one of the old Blackwall liners, the thousand-
ton frigate-built ship Kent, between London and
Melbourne, and once carried to London River nearly
half a million in gold from the Victorian diggings,
besides 250 passengers. That was in the very early
60 s when ships were ships, when 50 men were an
ordinary crew for a thousand-ton Blackwall liner,
and captains as well as owners made money hand
over fist.
Prof. Bryceson Treharne, the founder of
the Adelaide Repertory Theatre, and afore-
time director of the Adelaide Conserva-
torium, left Australia for a holiday in Europe
some time before the war, seeking a place
of seclusion where he might develop his gift
for musical composition. Luckily the chance
was provided for him by that celebrated
patron of the arts, Wilhelm the Mad. Tre-
harne was in Yerminy at the moment of the
outbreak of the war, and with many other
British civilians was interned at Ruhleben
camp. Here he found the leisure for which
his soul craved, undisturbed by any necessity
to worry over making a living. The Musi-
cal Standard prints a letter from the late
director of Music in the School for the Art
of the Theatre at' Florence, another internee,
who proclaims Treharne “one of the most
imaginative and talented of contemporary
British composers,” and adds that he feels
that “this remarkable vitality and originality
should have much to say in the musical crea-
tion of the future.” The Ruhleben camp is
allowed to amuse itself; and Treharne has
been responsible for most of the original
work performed there. The whole of the
incidental music in the camp’s production
of “As You Like It” came from his pen,
and his song compositions are many and
remarkable. It is probable that he will, in
gratitude, dedicate his Ruhleben works to
the Ivaiser.
On December IS Bigjohn Forrest will
celebrate the “golden jubilee” of his entry
into the civil service of Westralia. A native
of Bunbury, at 18 he joined the Survey
office and rose to be Surveyor-General—a
job he had from 1883 until IS9O. when
responsible government arrived, and he be-
came Premier. John is entitled to £5OO a
year as ex-Surveyor-General, and few men
have better earned their pension.
Called up from Coogee (N.S.W.), December
2, George Raffan, 63, contractor and station-
owner, and known throughout Australasia
and the Cold Country as an ardent follower
oi the game of bowls. A burly Scot from
the bleak north, with a physique and
appearance inherited from Norseman ances-
tors, he hurled generosity and kindliness
wherever he appeared. Starting life in N.
S. Wales as a carpenter, liis experience in
the ship-building trade, learnt in a hard
school, stood him in good stead when he
undertook a job at Pemell’s old mills, Dar-
ling Harbor, where the hardwood piles had
ruined all the imported chisels procurable
in Sydney. lie never looked back and
retired from contracting after completing
many big works. As a buyer and seller of
station properties he made no mistakes,
and with everything running well he gener-
ally mixed bowls and sheep, getting enjoy-
ment from the one and the wherewithal
from the other. He once stood*for the State
Assembly, and there were only two votes
hi tween him and David Storey, who
has held the seat ever since. The
yarn goes _ that, at the only place
he did not visit, because there were only four
men, three women and two horses, the' whole
lot voted for the bald hat-importer.

Against each letter of the word “Kaiser” put the

K equals 11 plus 6.. 116

1 equals 9 plus 6. . 96

E equals 5 plus 6. . r,6
It equals 18 plus 6.. 186
Total .. 666

A FREE AGENT.
VOLUNTEER ( reflecting ): “Well, a bloke
might as well go of his own accord.”
Boyd Cable (“Vagabond” of so much good
stuff from his artillery observing station in
Flanders) has been filling in his spell-o in
England finishing up a book. Its title is to
be “Between the Lines,” and it ought to be
here within a mail or two.
The gold-hunters who woke up the West-
raliau Cinderella are gradually passing on.
The latest to go is J. A. Roberts. The
Roberts brothers —the other one was buried
in 1913 —made their first big punch out of
the Revenue claim at Yaloginda, in the
vicinity of Meekatharra. From this show
they obtained about £20,000 in three years.
GEORGE ELLERY, the new Town Cleric
of Melbourne.
They then turned to Paddy’s Flat and -sold
one of their leases to the Golconda Co.,
which passed it on to the Ingliston Ex-
tended. A neighboring lease, the Ingliston
Consols, they kept for themselves and a few
friends, and to date this has produced nearly
£”00,000 worth of gold and paid over £50,000
in dividends. Both the brothers helped
many a lame dog over a stile. And there
arc a lot of lame dogs on the prospectors’
hard track.
A Maoriland girl. Eva Ashbolt, well known
in Wellington and Christchurch, lias got into
the limelight in England through being in-
strumental in capturing a couple of notori-
ous German spies. She was in a ’bus when
her attention was collared by the manner
of two “ladies” who sat near her, and when
one of them stooped to pick up a dropped coin
Eva felt the whole conspiracy blow up like
a burst Zeppelin. So she wrote on a card,
with her name and address, the words: “Two
suspicious characters inside —call a police-
man,” and handed it to the conductor. At
the next stop the policeman arrived, and two
spies that the authorities had been raking
London for were bagged. A few days later the
Maoriland girl received a cheque for £5O
from John Bull with kind regards and many
thanks.
Among the men reported
wounded at Gallipoli and
sufficiently repaired to have
a fresh go at the Turk is
Leo Murphy, one-time law-
yer of Bendigo. A mixed
genius this. He wrote the
gay verselet, “The Itoseleaf
in the Beer,” and a great
deal too little besides, and
is recognised as a first-class
tennis player, footballer,
swimmer, golfer, chess
player and maker of long
breaks at billiards. The onlv
game Murphy didn’t shine at
was the game of collecting
money.
When the call sounded there were no deaf
mutes on a little selection at Weston, Saw-
yer s Gully (N.S.W.). The selector, named
Metcalfe, promptly gathered up four sons and
flocked to the colors. A fifth son would
have flocked also, but the doctors barred the
way.
THE KAISER'S CONTENT.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I have abolished the Russian winter —The Kaiser.
The Kaiser sat within his tent,
A bear-skin rug - about his shins.
A pleasant warmth and light were lent
By artifice, and silver bins
Of costly liquors rich and old
Were there to keep the Kaiser bold,
And help in warding off the cold.
The Kaiser was a jovial man —
Full well he’d fed on steaming roast.
Aloft he raised the royal can,
And gave his officers a toast:
“Drink deep to me (we’ll rest anon),
A beaker drain, for I have gone
One better than Napoleon !
“He fled before the wintry blast,
And was a beaten man. Behold,
Your Kaiser rules the northern blast,
Subdues the majesty of cold!”
Without the keen wind, edged with sleet,
Cut down the Huns like tender wheat;
And Jack Frost spread their winding-sheet!
VICTORIA.

But the most interesting announcement for
the half-year was made, not in the accounts,
but in the chairman’s speech. There is to
be another issue of shares at par, one new
for every seven old. This will carry the
paid capital to £4,000,000 —double the
amount it was 10 years ago. The directors
admit that the bank doesn’t at present need
this money. It will, in fact, be invested in
the war loan. But, put plainly, the bank
feels that it can afford to distribute more
money in dividend ; yet it very wisely doesn’t
want to pay more than 10 p.c. There is
only one way in which it can divide more
without putting up the rate, and that is by
increasing the capital. Some cos. do that
by the simple process of transferring reserves
to capital—in other words, by issuing bonus
shares. But a bank is an institution which
depends upon credit, and any serious weak-
ening of a bank’s credit not only damages
the bank itself but is liable to seriously
injure people in no way connected with it.
For this reason it is very undesirable that
a bank should pay dividend upon any cap-
ital which its shareholders have not pro-
vided in hard cash. The 8.N.5.W., recog-
nising this, takes the middle course and
issues more capital at par. This, while
having the same practical effect as increas-
ing the rate of dividend, strengthens the
position of the depositors and other creditors.
And whatever encourages the depositors
to leave their money undisturbed is good
also for borrowers. What a borrower
wants is not to be able to get money when
loans are being thrown at you, but to be left
at least undisturbed when accommodation is
hard to get. In 1900 capital was
fAOOO.OOO and the reserves £1,276,748;
total £3,276,748. The debts to the public
amounted to £24,735,490. Liquid and
semi-liquid assets were then £11,941,272.
So there was £12,794,218 of debt then not
represented by liquid and semi-liquid assets,
and there was £3,276,743 of shareholders’
funds protecting the investment. Now debts
t J the public are £50,301.583; and liquid
and semi-liquid assets £30,040,334. So the
present debt not represented by liquid and
semi-liquid assets is £20,261,199. And
there is £6.211,065 of shareholders’ funds.
Thus there is now £1 of the shareholders’
money protecting every £3 ss. of the public’s
money used in making advances and in pre-
whereas 15 years ago £1 had to pro-
tect £3 ISs. Assuming that the present non-
liquid assets are of the same value, £ for £,
as those of 15 years ago, it follows that,
even before the coming increase of capital,
both creditors and borrowers are better
placed than they were 15 years ago. And-
they were very well placed then.
The movements in the accounts during
the past 12 months have been very extensive
on paper : —•

But most of the big changes are largely
accidental. “Bills, etc.,” include the
Commonwealth’s balance, chiefly war loan.
That accounts for about £3,000,000 on each
side. Ordinary deposits have also increased.
As remarked six months ago, people with
spare money are in these uncertain times
letting somebody else take the risk of in-
vesting it. Hence a lot of this money is
left for little better than safe custody. Ad-
vances have shrunk, largely because mer-
chandise stocks are running down, and there
is no inclination to launch out. The move-
ments, though large, are thus explainable
by the conditions we are passing through;
and they are of much less consequence than
they seem to be on the surface.

The co. writes off £2700 to provide for
depreciation; and then, in case any share-
holder should be worrying over the war tax.
strips some of its own special silver lining
from the war-cloud, coins it into a 5 p.c.
bonus, and throws it on top of the usual
12 p.c. dividend. This doesn’t leave a very
substantial lay-by: of the year’s profit the
co. divides over two-thirds and hangs on to
less than one-third to reinforce its own posi-
tion. The one thing to be said in excuse is
that the co.’s position was already fairly
strong. The funds are equal to ISO p.c. of
the year’s premium income; and that is sev-
eral points better than the average of the
strongest English cos.
The net premium income was rather less
this year than last; but a very line growth
is shown in practically any other period
that you care to name:—

It has been a juicy thing for shareholders.
Thirteen years ago the capital was only
£62.500; and the 8 p.c. dividend of that
period gave shareholders £5OOO. Since then
they have not put up a penny, and to-day
they draw £34,001, which is equal to 55 p.c.
on the capital as at 1902. And it is quite
likely that, if no legislative accidents hap-
pen, they will make just as much progress
in the next 12 years as they have in the
past dozen. Unquestionably there is the
business to be done; for in spite of the
enormous strides they have made during the
past dozen years, Australian offices still do
but a very small part of Australia’s under-
writing. There are no trustworthy figures
on the point; but a recent estimate made
the policies held in the Commonwealth and
Maoriland only 30 p.c. of the aggregate.
There is no valid reason why our own cos.
shouldn’t gradually get the other 70 p.c.
The figures prove that it is a wonderfully
profitable business, once a co. lias become
well established ; and the more profitable it
is the more desirable it is that it should
belong to ourselves.
And the better-class Australian cos. are,
in proportion to their risks, stronger on the
average even than the better class English
cos. On that point there is positive proof.
This Colonial Mutual Co. has not been par-
ticularly energetic in building up reserves:
it has been a bit too greedy for dividends
and bonuses. Yet it has £379,310 of funds,
and by the end of this current year should
have received £127,000 of premiums after
paying exes. That is, £506,000 will be
available by September 30 next to pay
claims arising this year; and the most
claims that have actually had to be paid
in any one vvrar were £98,951. Contrast
£98,951 and £506,000, and you get some
idea of the strength of this Australian
society.
Besides, a policy-holder can turn into the
office of an Australian co. and, so to speak,
look into the till. The investments are set
out, aud a man can pretty well tell what
they are worth. As to those of a foreign
concern he can make a wild guess or depend
upon the statements of very interested
parties. Not that this paper is going to
say that the Australian insurer will do the
right thing in taking any Australian policy
in preference to any foreign policy. There
are two or three Australian offices which
are partly wild gambles and partly menaces
to the public. Governments won’t realise
what a danger the reckless insurance office
can be. A man carrying on business in a
big city building occupied by perhaps 50
firms gets into difficulties. He is a bad
risk; yet one of these reckless offices will
issue him a policy—they will issue almost
anybody a policy. A fire occurs presently,
and perhaps a score of honest people are
burned out —honest people who would not
have suffered that loss and inconvenience if
there was a law to shut out the wild-cat
.insurance co.

When the accounts were issued a year ago
it wasn’t certain that Britain was quite as
much mistress of the seas as she thought
she was. Germany had done such extra-
ordinary things with its army that it seemed
likely it had some trick up its sleeve for its
navy also. Hence 12 months ago this co.
no doubt made fairly liberal provision for
marine-insurance contingencies; and that
probably explains why the 1914 profits were
nearly £7OOO less than those for the previous
year. Since then the German navy has
merely grown seaweed and occasionally
knocked out a fishing smack; so the con-
tingencies for which the co. provided haven’t
arisen, and the withheld profits have
doubtless been shaken out into the present
accounts. That may partly explain the vel'y
magnificent profit of £56,928. For the rest
the co. must certainly have benefited by the
new Australian disposition to give Austra-
lian products first chance.

business: —-

A little change has been made in the form
of the accounts this year; and it /will have
to be noted by those who try to check the
foregoing figures by the official ones. Pre-
miums this year are shown in the co.’s
revenue account “less reinsurances, returns
and unearned."’ Theoretically this may be
the best way of exhibiting the business.
Hitherto, however, the Queensland, follow-
ing the usual practice, has shown the pre-
miums less only reinsurances and returns;
the unearned premiums have been shown on
the other side as a transfer to the unearned
premium reserve. The amount added for
the past year to that reserve is £12,844.
which sum is in the foregoing table included
with the year’s premiums for the sake of
uniformity.
An intending policy-holder must apply
three tests to an insurance office: —
(1) What are its funds in proportion to its risks?
(2) Are those funds available, and what would
they fetch if they had to be realised?
(3) What is the co.’s reputation? In other words,
what is the “moral hazard,” as insurance cos. call it
in relation to policy-holders?
(1) A co. ought to have funds at least 14
times greater than its premium income.
This co.’s capital and reserves amount to
£391,907, and its premium income (which
is the handiest measure of its risks) was
£240,849 last year. So the funds were more
than 14 times the premium income, though
not very much more. The position is thus
reasonably satisfactory. (2) The funds are
made up this way:—
Government securities .. £93,748
Fixed deposits and cash . . 212,344
Freeholds .. .. .. 110,918
Municipal bonds and sundries 69,367
£486,377
Less liabilities .. .. 94,470
Co.’s funds .. ..£391,907
There are no mortgages ; furniture has been
written right off; and no such items as
“business extension expenses,” stationery,
etc., disfigure the accounts. No co. could
come better out of this test. (3) A “moral
hazard” can’t be measured by figures. But
when he sees that a co. divides no more
than one-third of its profits and [Hits two-
thirds of them by, an intending policy-holder
can at least he satisfied that that co. has a
reasonable regard for the policy-holder’s
interests. So much from the policy-holder’s
standpoint. From the shareholder’s the
most important thing to consider is pro-
bably: Will the State let it last?

The £lOOO written off the past year’s profit
goes to reduce the co.’s freehold and safe
deposit. Down to 1911 the co. had a lease-
hold, which was gradually depreciated until
in 1910 it stood at £9OO. Then a freehold
was bought, and the two properties, lumped,
appeared in 1911 as valued at £20,434. It
is pretty clear, therefore, that there isn’t
much leasehold now left in the composite
item. For a long while the co. had no com-
petition except from the family lawyer and
the good old friend of the family. Now a
couple of other cos. are in the business;
and if the family lawyer and the friend of
the family are pushed out of it, there will
be plenty of room for the three.
Sooner or later it will be the law that
no one shall engage in this business unless
he puts up a bond. But even then it will
be much more satisfactory to employ an
entity that isn’t a mortal person. It
doesn’t matter who dies —directors, man-
ager, anybody—a trustee co. lives on. The
testator must see how important that is.
He makes a will and appoints Jones. .Jones
dies, and he has to alter his will. He then
appoints Smith. But Smith is a colonel,
who goes away to the war. Just then the
testator dies, and the business is hung up
till the Court can say what shall be done
in Smith’s absence. Then word comes that
Smith has been killed, leaving Brown as his
executor. Now, Brown can, if he likes,
proceed to act as though he had been ap-
pointed trustee by the original testator,
though the original testator may not have
known him, or may actually have mistrusted
him.
Looking at the thing with the eye of the
bc-neficiary, this co. has guaranteed its good
behavior by (1) putting up a paid capital
of £23,225; (2) making itself liable for
another £53,025 of capital; (3) accumulat-
ing £41,479 of reserves. Of these funds
£22.529 is liquid, £19.430 is in property
loans, £23,088 is in property. A further
guarantee exists in the fact that this co.
absolutely loses its living if it ceases to be
known as a satisfactory trustee. From the
shareholders’ point of view the most cheer-
ful feature is this steady growth in the

* * * #
On December 1 the Tas. Government took
possession of the electrical plant and under-
takings of the Hobart Gas Co. The price
was £lOB,OOO. The value put on the plant
by the co. itself was £67,000, which was not
an under-estimate. The rest of the price was
for goodwill and for concessions which Par-
liament handed over to the Gas Co. a few
years back. The vendors get £BOOO cash
and 5 per cent, interest on the balance. The
Hydro-Electric Department reckons to boom
the use of electricity for domestic uses now
that it has the distribution in its own hands,
and so help to tide over the lean years just
ahead. Other schemes which 'will use power
are in the air, but are likely to stay there till
things settle down. The power from the
Great Lake is expected to be on tap in
Hobart at the end of January.
* * * *
“D.F.” : There seems to be too much water
in the Tasmanian Government’s hydro-elec-
tric scheme, and its immediate prospects
are not glittering. The manager stated in
evidence before a select committee that the
capital cost would be at least £350,000.
He gave a nasty jolt, by the way,
to the pictures painted in the past
of Hobart being made the great manu-
facturing centre of Australia by means
of hydro-electric power at nominal prices
when he remarked in his evidence: “It is
no use quoting ridiculously cheap rates
because somebody in the past has said they
were going to get cheap power.” And he
finished up by saying: “We have completed
the works and we have to get as near paying
the interest bill as we possibly can”—which
doesn’t sound a bit enthusiastic.
It is reported that the Lucknow (N.S.W.)
mines are to be reopened by a “strong co. of
Sydney and Melbourne capitalists.” The
co. will need to be extra strong to do any-
thing with the old mines, there being 32
miles of drives, etc., to unwater before it
can reach the points at which Warnford
Lock left off after spending over £20,000 in
fruitless prospecting. When all is said
and done it will probably be found that the
“strong company” will confine itself to pot-
tering about the shallow levels.

Dir. B. R. Gelling has retired from the
service of the Mutual Life and Citizens’
Assurance Co., but retains his seat on the
Board as a director of the co. Mr. Gelling
is well known as the secretary of the old
“M.L.A.” and manager of investments of
the “M.L.C.,” and after nearly 28 years’
service has decided to engage in pastoral
pursuits in the Singleton district.

Old Farm diamond property at Copeton

With W. G. Jira as managing director, the
co. embarked on an entirely new scheme
of draining the mine, the efforts of former
cos. having been defeated by water. A
shaft 40ft. deep was sunk on the northern
boundary in granite, a few feet only out-
side the water area. From the bottom
drives were made to cut the drift, and draw
the water off to the new shaft. Here a
steam pump has been installed. The ex-
periment is proving successful, and the pro-
perty should soon be dry. Wash containing
diamonds and tin has been struck in every
drive. Diamonds from two to 28 carats per
load have been obtained from the prospects,
the average being six carats per load, be-
sides 101 b. of tin. Already a nice parcel of
diamonds has been won, and the sinking of
a new development shaft is being proceeded

ALUMINOFERRIC is used by the Sydney Water
Board, New South Wales Public Water Supply Depart-
ment, and many country Water Boards and graziers
for precipitating impurities and clarifying water in
reservoirs, cattle dams, etc. It is simply thrown
into the reservoir in the form of a solid cake, or
dissolved and added as a liquid. In three hours’
time the most discolored water will be as clear as
crystal. One pound will clear over 2000 gallons of
water. It is exceedingly cheap and quite harmless.
Sole agents: William Adams & Company Limited,
Sydney, Melbourne, Brisbane and Perth. — Advt.
