* * * *
Occidental, Cobar (N.S.W.), ought soon
to reappear in the dividend-list. Manager
Mackenzie has been pursuing a vigorous
policy of development, and excellent pro-
spects are showing at various points in the
deeper ground. The mine is being prepared
for an increased output both on and below
the surface. Published figures indicate a
recovery of about 80 p.c. of the gold con-
tents of the ore. This is low for slime-
cyanidation, but the management may have
determined that an increase would not lead
to any more profitable result. Also, the
company persists in using a plant on which
a tonnage royalty has to be paid. It is a
matter of £ s. d. as to whether it would
not be advisable to substitute a non-royalty
plant.
* * * *
C.S.A., Cobar (N.S.W.), is developing
into a mine of great possibilities, particu-
larly in the Tinto section. For some time
past there has been persistent buying of
the stock, although the Exchange list has
not revealed the fact. A smelting plant is
about to be erected. This may be sound
business from the co.’s point of view, but
as a matter of public economy is wrong. At
an expenditure of less than £15,000 on a
light line of railway the mine could be con-
nected with the smelting plant at Great
Cobar. The C.B.A. has abundance of the
basic ore which the Great Cobar needs, and
on practical grounds everything favors an
amalgamation. Such a scheme was on the
tapis, but Great Cobar could not finance it
or submit sufficiently attractive proposals.
As things are going, it is quite on the cards
that ere long the lesser will absorb the
greater.
* * * *
There was a scuttle from the north-east
branch-drive in the Langi South last week,
when a burst of slime threatened the men
in the No. 1 rise. The muck poured out of
the gutter in such violence as to fill 370 ft.
of the level and block up 50ft. of the big
main level. Then the floodgates penned it
back. The first mishap of the kind in this
Ararat (Vic.) mine was at No. 2 rise, higher
up the gutter, and it has blotted out that
working. The recent breakaway won’t
affect the main wash-dirt drives, which are
1000 ft, distant up the lead, but it is a nasty
reminder of the treachery of deep alluvial
work.
The final clean-up at Cock’s Pioneer, El-
dorado (Vic.), was better even than was
expected. The last four tons of concen-
trates gave 4070 z„ raising the total gold
won to 27820 z. 12dwt. of the value of
£10,928. With the cash from the 38 tons
of tin oxide also recovered, the lot would
bring the average yield above Is. 2d. per
cub. yard, and probably shove the profit on
the clean-up to £BOOO. There was a bit
left on the right side from the previous
clean-up, so market talk is of a shilling divi-
dend as a Christmas box. That would cost
£5OOO, leaving a decent sum in hand to
start the fund for the second dredge to work
the big stretch of bottom ground lying idle.
* * * *
Mount Lyell pours into the air
every year from its smelters at Queens-
town (Tas.) many hundreds of tons
of sulphur in fumes. While this is
going to waste large quantities of
pyritic ores are mined and sent to
the mainland to provide the acid used in
making superphosphates. The co. now wants
to recover the fume sulphur and use it for
making sulphuric acid, and a metallurgical
chemist with much Yankee experience is
experimenting to see whether the thing can
be done at a profit.
Mount Lyell has its third smelter blown
in again, but the fortnight’s breakdown cut
the blister copper yield for November from
600 tons to 511 tons. That means the loss
of a bit in these days of war prices. The
compensation is that the contouring of the
shoot in the crosscut at 1200 ft. is proving a
choice hank of the very richest ore to
sweeten up the poor stuff which may be
found on the other levels. The Lyell Blocks
crowd, who claim that they will be early
inheritors of this shoot, were scorched by
Sticlit in his half-yearly report. All they
can say in reply is: “Why didn’t he pro-
duce the mine plans?”
* X- * X-
There’s no drought at Mount Lyell any-
way. So far Queenstown has had 116.75
inches of rain this year, which is two points
over the record for 12 months. With six
weeks to go and still raining they expect to
get about 11 feet before the year is out.
* * * *
Mount Bischoff Extended (Tas.), after
working for some time under a co-operative
arrangement with miners and mill hands,
will continue operations on a wages basis.
During the half-year to September 30 the
output of tin oxide was 105 tons, and the
price averaged £99 15s. lOd. Revenue
totalled £10,446, out of which £7BOl was
divided by co-operative parties, leaving the
co. £2645. Reserves actually developed are
22,095 tons and “probable” 13,139 tons. The
average is only 1 p.c. of tin, but even 1 p.c.
ore is worth 355. with tin at its present
magnificent £175, or more.
Westralia wants to boil the speculative
pegger in fat. He is the motor-car prospec-
tor who scorches out to every new find and
pegs all the country for miles before the
genuine gold-hunter can get a look in. If
the find proves to be a good one lie makes
money out of position blocks, and if it isn’t
he clears out without putting a pick in the
ground. The last rush to Kurnalpi is a
striking instance. Very little work was
done outside the few areas around which
some big nuggets were unearthed, and those
who were willing and anxious to have a go
could not get within miles of where the gold
was found. Gibraltar is another case. The
country was pegged a long way back to the
railway line and half-way to Coolgardie, but
very little of it was ever tested. The fram-
ing of a regulation making a certain amount
of immediate development compulsory is
suggested as a way of scotching this fiend.
*** ' *
Westralians who recollect the North
White Feather, Kanowna, rub their eyes on
finding it once again in the front of a
market flutter. The story is that Alan
Bowler incubated the present movement
with a syndicate in 20 shares of £5O each.
The preliminary exam, was checked up by
D. Morgan, once of Glen Wills. What he
saw isn’t yet the subject of an advt. in the
dailies, but the button-holing yarn is that
he found thousands of tons of 2oz. dirt left
in the mine. The Bulletin hasn’t met
many people who have been given such a
start by the original miner. Anyway, a
miraculous float is reported; 35,000 10s.
shares floated off with the speed of a. Mel-
bourne Cup winner in a co. having a 60,000
share register. The vendors got 25,000 paids
with a modest £lO,OOO cash, leaving a work-
ing capital of £7500. No wonder some men
don’t want to go to the Front!
If this sort of thing goes on what will
happen? The Edna Central (W.A.) Novem-
ber clean-up from 2155 tons was gold worth
£1913. Costs were 17s. 10d., so a profit of
Id. per ton was earned. They are right—
much does depend on what is to be proved
at depth.
* * * *
Great Boulder’s monthly profit for the
half-year ended June averaged £26,000, and
the surpluses for the succeeding four months
were £27,408, £29,005, £28,278 and £30,962,
the last being a record. Profits like these
should be maintained for another few years,
and then the drop will be sharp and sudden.
The exercise of the co.’s Stawell (Vic.)
option is very doubtful.
* * * *
A Bulletin man at Kalgoorlie:—
At the present rate of depletion Great Boulder
may last four years. Then the end is likely to
come suddenly. Something is said locally about its
low-grade possibilities, but the official reply to this is
“Nothing continuous; nothing payable.” Boulder
Perseverance has stopped all development and has
started to stope out what is left. Two years should
suffice to do this, and after that, barring miracles,
the co, will either have to carry on in greatly
reduced circumstances or pull out altogether. Kal-
gurli’s shoot finally terminates between 1750 ft. and
1850 ft., and attention is now being directed to the
upper levels. Sufficient ore is blocked out to keep
the mill going for at least a year and perhaps a bit
longer. The co. is determined to shut down and
clear out directly the ore in sight becomes unpayable.
Oroya Links has nearly worked out its Oroya blocks,
and as developments in the Eclipse or northern sec-
tion have not come up to expectations the outlook is
not rosy. Operations will probably drag on for
years, but shareholders are not likely to get much.
Ivanhoe still has a profit of over half a million
sterling in sight, and may get more at depth. But
explorations in the porphyry which has impoverished
values so much in the last few levels shows that
geological predictions" are not being fully borne out;
and though it is extremely probable that values
will mgke again in deeper ground, there is at present
no certainty about it. Associated hasn’t done any
sinking for five years and is not likely to start.
Three levels driven below 1800 it. were practically
barren. Nowadays mill supplies are obtained from
all parts of the workings above 1800 ft., and the
discovery of an occasional bunch of rich telluride ore
keeps the show from going under. Its life is
uncertain. Associated Northern is being kept alive
by parties of tributors. It has long since passed
the stage when a co. could profitably work it. South
Kalgurli and Hainault has improved in the last 12
months, and now has at least two years’ profitable
ore in sight. Golden Horseshoe has good reserves
visible, and 100 ft. of driving on the Boulder lode at
3020 ft. level has exposed a good body of payable
ore. The co. is and always has been very reticent
about its affairs ; consequently about this mine I have
a less clear impression than of any other on the field. It
appeals, however, to have improved its position a
good deal in the last year or so. Lake View and
Star has a good length of payable ore opened up at
2100 ft. in the old Lake View lease, and as the
Hannans Star-Boulder Deep Levels sections are also
fairly well stocked with reserves, the co. looks like
maintaining present profits for some time to come.
This and the South Kalgurli seem to be the best
specs, on the field for small investors to gamble
in, if one must gamble.
All the Kalgoorlie mining cos. are growling about
the cumulative effect of Federal and State income
and profit taxation on the industry. They claim that
in the West gold is produced at greater cost and
under more adverse conditions than anywhere else
in Australia—which is all “guff,” of course—-and
that it is unfair for those engaged in the work
of getting it to be hit up in the manner complained
of. In 10 years the average yield of the State has
fallen from 665. Bd. to 38s. 2d., and now only leaves
a small margin of profit. Some mines, it is admitted,
can stand the extra taxes, but there are others that
can't. The co-called profits of mining cos. are it is
argued—and this is true—not always real profits.
Often they represent only a return of capital follow-
ing on heavy outlays on plant, development, etc.
About a dozen reasons are given to dis-
satisfied creditors by official-liquidator Will
Davies for privately disposing of Chaffers
mine and plant instead of calling in the auc-
tioneer. Davies says that he sounded every
mining co. interested in the neighborhood as
to the possibility of their bidding, and the
only answer he got was that Chaffers was
iv)t worth the price of a cable to London,
lie then asked the agent of the debenture
holders what he thought of it, and as this
person was pessimistic about the sale a
meeting of unsecured creditors was convened.
The majority was dead against putting
another penny into the property ; so, coming
to the conclusion that if the property were
put up for auction it would go for a song, he
sold it to one of the principal unsecured
creditors —by far the most enterprising of
them—for £lOO and an undertaking to pay
off the debenture holders and preferential
claims, amounting in all to between £BOOO
and £9OOO. Under the circumstances Davies
considered this to be a good deal, particu-
larly as he had retained at least £2500 worth
of stores and other assets for distribution
among the unsecured people. Upsetting the
sale is still talked of, but in all probability
it will not come to anything.
* *■ * *
Melbourne and Sydney have now two
more irons in the fire on the East Mount
Margaret field (W.A.). One, the Olympic,
for which £4OOO is asked, recently had a 3oz.
crushing placed to its credit, and is stated
to have more than the purchase price in
sight. The other, the Golden Bell, is said
to contain bunchy ore shoots aggregating
over 1300 ft. in length. To date it has yielded
£30,000, equal to about £l2 per ton ; but most
likely this was the product of picked parcels.
The mines are not in districts that have been
proved to any great depth.
* * * *
Westralia Mount Morgans, which paid
shareholders £226.000 and went smash,
is again in public notice. This time
a new make of ore 300 ft. in length
and of payable value is stated to
have been opened up “out west.” And
already the possibility of dividends is being-
discussed —by those who hold the scrip.
Collins House would feel better if the
Annans River tin show (Q.) could do
better. The past six months brought
drought and the fear that away from the
contact area values shade sharply, thus in-
volving a re-casting of the estimate of pay-
able yardage. The treatment of drift out-
side the pay ground is shouldered on to the
preparatory works account, but it means a
loss for the term of £2007. The bucket
dredge now going up will have to swill out
very solid profits before leeway is made
up, for against tin in (£1950) the
co. has an overdraft of £6915 and sundry
creditors £11,988. A great deal may be
read into this naive sentence in Wharton’s
report: “The requirements of the co. (as to
expenditure) have been met by overdraft,
which has been guaranteed on satisfactory
terms to this company.” The mine, plant,
tunnel, race-pipe line, and dam at cost appear
at £41,879, and the new dredge at £10,547,
so a fair bit of capital has to be recovered.
The long-dragged-out Snow case in
Adelaide has reached another stage. Pro-
bably Snow wishes now that, when first
accused of breaking the new Enemy Trading-
law, he had just said “Dashed if I know!
Nobody really understands the new Act, and
I may have done something technically un-
lawful. What do you think, Judge?” On
which the Judge might reasonably have said
that a £250 fine would settle things, and
teach people to be more careful. As matters
have gone, Snow won the first round, the
S.A. Criminal Court deciding that there
was no case to go to the jury at all. The
second round, fought in the High Court,
was indecisive. In the third —the one just
finished —there were eight “counts” against
accused. Six of them were dropped during
the proceedings. The seventh, entering into
a pyrites contract for the benefit of Ilirsch,
an enemy co., the jury called Not Guilty;
but they said Guilty on an all-round charge
of having commercial intercourse by letters
and telegrams with firms with odd names in
London, Holland and America, which at law
constituted trading with the enemy. Now a
lot -of fine points go up to the High Court
again.
* * * »
Commonwealth Treasurer Higgs noti-
fies holders of scrip certificates issued in
connection with the recent War Loan that
they should post the certificate, with pro-
visional interest coupon attached, to a
branch of the Commonwealth Bank, after
completing the form of request on the
back of the certificate. The Bank will then
arrange for stock to be inscribed or for
Treasury bonds to be issued when ready.
It is expected that Treasury bonds will be
available for issue by the end of December.
On the 15th of December the Bank will
remit the interest to the owner, or otherwise
dispose of it as directed by him. Applicants,
particularly those in the country, will pro-
bably find it more convenient to have stock
inscribed in their names than to have
Treasury bonds issued to them. In the
former case interest warrants will be posted
half-yearly to the owner or, if desired, paid
into his banking account, while holders of
Treasury bonds will have the trouble of cut-
ting off the interest coupons and collecting
their value. The Commonwealth Bank will,
however, if desired, take charge of the bonfis
and collect coupons free of charge.
* * * *
At Charters Towers there is a growing
inclination to divide the cash reserves saved
in more prosperous times. Brilliant Ex-
tended, which hasn’t been doing too well
of late, will pay a sixpenny (£6250), and
the new Brilliant Freehold also shells out
6d. all round (£3750). Mills United has
300,000 shares, so a sixpenny there costs
£7500. At the end of 1913 it paid Is.
(£15.000), practically all out of its cash
reserve. Most of the other Towers mining
cos. are very poor.

BOTTLED IN FRANCE.
A GOOD REMEDY FOR
SALLOW COMPLEXIONS.
Everyone may have a clear and bright
complexion, free from furrows or wrinkles, if
they will only take a little daily trouble in
the following manner : Buy some Puritol wax
from the chemist or draper, and apply every
night, lap a little cold water on the face ten
times with the hands every morning, allow-
ing it to get thoroughly round the closed
eyes, and pinch the skin very gently across
all wrinkles. Puritol Wax Soap, which
leaves a fine film of wax on the skin, should
be used ; this acts as a protective during the
day. This treatment arouses the dormant
nerves and blood vessels, and undoubtedly
accounts for many of the clear complexions
so much admired by those who do not yet
know the secret. —Advt. [3

WOMAN’S
LETTER
Sydney, December G, 1915.
.My Dear Arini. —
Lady Doris Blackwood returns to Auntie
Helen after a fortnight’s frolics with the
Strickland Kindergarten at Sutton Forest.
A new Help is coming for the State sub-
royalty’s staff, which has moulted in these
fiery times. He is a wounded warrior, and
part of his job here is to convalesce. Young
Geoffrey Hughes, a son of Tom, M.L.C., has
only been on loan as an emergency A.D.C.
In January he goes south to join the Avia-
tion School, intending to be a high-flier at
the Front as soon as his wings are tipped
with the official certificate.
D’Apice, another aide, is slowly wend-
ing his way back to health. It has been a
long, stiff climb.
Chief Justice Cullen presides over the
Old Gold and Silver War Fund. O yes,
O yes! Any old teapots, any old rings,
ancient brooches, battered bangles, ear or
nose rings ! Any old thimbles of your own,
or cigarette-cases you can steal from the
man? Send them to City Mutual Cham-
bers, Hunter-street. The melting-pot will
do the rest.
Rupert Carrington, whose mother was a
I lorsfall, is in the sth Dragoon Guards.
Lvents have moved swiftly for this young
man since the war started. Uncle Charlie
Carrington lost his only son a while ago in
France. So nephew Rupert some day steps
into the barony. The later title. Marquis of
Lincolnshire, snuff's out when the ex-Gover-
nor of N. S. Wales crosses the Ferry. Aus-
tralian Rupert has now offered himself and
his prospects to Sybil, a daughter of Vis-
count Colville.
Hercules Robinson, the 20-year-old son
and heir of Baron Rosmead, has been killed
at the Front. Our Hercules, Governor of N.
S. Wales from 1872 to ’79, was his grand-
dad. The father of the dead boy, also Her-
cules, served in South Africa, and was A.D.C.
to General Hutton, who cussed and com-
manded in N. S. Wales at one time. The
name Hercules runs in the family from Sir
Hercules Langrishe, who was an official at
Dublin Castle in the last decade of the 18th
century, and was the progenitor of the Robin-
sons on the female side.
John Anderson, lately principal Under-
Sec. for the Colonies, has been appointed
Governor of Ceylon. In 1901 he came here
as private secretary to the ducal York, now
George V. They say lie has hopes of living-
in Macquarie-street when the Stricklands
give up housekeeping in these parts.
Strenuous old soldier John Stanley is
gazetted Brigadier-General—a temporary
rank that carries no extra pay—for his ser-
vices as Quartermaster-General. In the
days of peace Colonel Stanley’s head came
under the axe that chopped aged soldiers
out of the Permanent Force without the
ghost of a pension. Now, in war-time, he
is putting in long hours and getting through
as much work as headquarters can give
him—and that is enough to keep half a
dozen men on the hop.
“Dicky” Richards retires into private life,
and the rabbitskin falls to a successor, who
may be Spencer Brunton—or, again, may be
Richard Denis Meagher. Mrs. Richards is
too shy to care about the social fluff that
surrounds the billet, so the gentle municipal
mother of a couple of years past will prob-
ably welcome a quiet spell on the domestic
hearth. But “Dicky” will miss it. He
was just beginning to feel that he was cut
out for a courtier.
As a municipal voter, Woman is frankly
regarded as an imbecile by the touts who
lie in wait to tell her that Blodgers is the
man. “Are you a loyal British subject? Then,
madam, record your vote for Blodgers—the
only candidate pledged to keep the Kaiser’s
hosts at bay.” Blodgers is really a field-
marshal, it seems; but till Kitchener sends
for him he is willing, at a sacrifice, to be a
mere city alderman. Hustled by aggressive
females, badgered by the yelps of male sup-
porters of Blodgers, squeaked at by a mos-
quito army of youngsters waving candidates’
tickets, Woman struggles to the recording
pens. Seizing the pencil, she probably
drives its blunt stub ferociously through the
names her tormentors have yelled loudest in
her ear.
Verbrugghen’s hair is his own property.
Otherwise he would have shaken it all over
the floor at the Symphony orchestral concert
on Saturday. The small tense figure, with
the waving black mane, dominated not only
the players but the audience as well. A
musician (they are always fidgety folks)
Inadvertently dropped his stiek in the body
of the hall. In awestruck silence the com-
pany wondered if Verbrugghen would stop
the performance. But he merely twirled
round and frowned at nobody in particular.
Then a lay Philistine blew his nose like a
trumpet call. It was a dulcet moment, when
the conductor was hushing his fiddlers into a
crooning lullaby. Yerbrugghen’s hair
simply sprang to the attack.
The Town Hall held a massive attendance
for this concert. The programme was of
lighter quality than at the first affair.
Alfred Hill’s Maori Rhapsody Lament was
included in a programme that served up the
William Tell overture with new Verbrug-
ghen sauce. Cesar Franck’s “Le Chasseur
Maudit” was an outstanding bit of thunder
in a long list of average music quickened to
fresh life under the magic baton. The con-
ductor’s desire that the audience like the
orchestra should stand for Chopin’s
“Funeral March” was a big order for
elderly folk on a summer afternoon, and
here and there they sank, abashed, into their
chairs. Still, there were those in the audi-
ence who weren’t too exhausted to applaud
when that tine performance was over. Ap-
plaud the Funeral March? More electric
sparks from the bundle of wires in front!
Another Louise Mack mention of late
Nurse Cavell. The nurse and her fellow
Red Crossers were singing “God Save” when
a wounded German officer joined his voice
to theirs in the hymn and was singing
merrily when Nurse Cavell took notice. “No,
you don’t!” she exclaimed, running over to
him. “You’ll not sing this lying down.”
She propped him up with pillows, and he
continued the musical service more lustily.
After all, perhaps the German knew the air
Quite well, but the words not at all. He
might, devils take him, even have been sing-
ing tbe German anthem, which goes to the
same music, or lack of it. George 1., first
German king of England, brought the noise
with him when he hung up his crown in
London. A composer named John Bull is
said to have written the words.
The League of Honor for Women has a
Central Council in Sydney and a member-
ship of about 1000 in N. S. Wales. The
affair has the benediction of Lady Helen,
and aims at catching and equipping feminine
workers for personal service in war time.
B.v supplying the names and addresses of
every depot and its chief representative and
its special wants and so on the League
hopes to collect stacks of surplus energy
now dribbling away over bridge tables.
The billet of piano professor at the Con-
servatorium trembles in the balance. Rumor,
one hopes, lies with brazen impudence in
declaring that Yerbruaghen has another im-
portation in view. With Laurence Godfrey
Smith away there remained one likely candi-
date —the Russian-born Pintel, educated in
France, and winner of the Premiere I’rix
at the National Conservatorium, Paris. This
school is unique, since money cannot gain a
pupil entrance to it. Merit alone, in open
competition, is the passport. Pintel’s
supporters will at least claim an
explanation if Verbrugghen should pack the
Conservatorium with another imported
nominee.
Cora Lenehan, a daughter of our late
Chief Stargazer, who has been petrolling up
hill and down dale in England under the
wing of a relative, is now making towards
home, by way of Italy and Algiers, with a
long stop in Egypt. By the time she
sights the Nile brother Pat will be due to
arrive from this side.
The bells of St. Clemen’s —I mean St.
Mary’s, were set a-ringing one morning last
week with such spirit that the town thought
at last Peace was sighted off Cape Howe.
But it was only an ordination ceremony
going on, an affair of many hours and of
much interest to those concerned. Inciden-
tally “The Church” scored a hit, ex-curate
Geier, once of Woollahra, being ushered
along another step towards the priesthood.
A new brand of Father Christmas will
make his first appearance at the Royal on
Saturday afternoon, a made-in-Australia
Christmas. His motley will be that
of an elderly teamster, who will receive
parcels from the public, and later on trans-
port them to the children whose fathers
have answered the last roll-call in the Dar-
danelles.
George Titheradge, now very ill, gets a
benefit on Friday afternoon at Her
Majesty’s. Of course you’ll be there.
A daughter of the late G. B. Edwards,
one time a South Sydney M.P., was married
lately in Scotland to Surgeon-Captain AY.
AA r Ingram, of the R.A.M.C. The bride,
Annie Laurie, had her mother and sister to
arrange her plumage.
The Minstrel Boy is off to the war, but
he isn’t taking his wild harp with him this
time—at all events, not the instrument built
like a cathedral that he uses on concert
platforms. Torzillo, Sydney’s premier harp-
ist, has enlisted with the Australian forces,
and is off to the place where the music is
made by shells and bullets and bombs. A
whole orchestra of Sydney musicians has
enlisted with him.
The Allies' Day Committee hopes to
gather in £lOO,OOO before the last copper
comes from the country. Bill and Jim have
the harvest on their hands till February,
but the town helpers are still pegging away.
Burns, Thilp & Co.’s trip to Papua for a
shilling is still available. Tickets can be
bought at Cliallis House.
Young Pat Murray goes up to spend the
Christmas holidays with his father, the
Lieut.-Governor of Papua, and takes his
beloved butterfly net with him. He goes
to Oxford later on.
The January goff carnival at Kosciusko
starts on the 25th, and is this year to benefit
war funds. A few inter-State visitors are
already bumping over the road to the Gov-
ernment hostel for the Christmas fishing;
among others to arrive is dominie Bonsfield,
of Brisbane Grammar School.
“Rosna,” writing from London, tells me:
The convalescent Kangaroo is abroad in London
in his thousands, and any place remotely connected
with Australia is thick with him. The Common-
wealth Offices became so crowded that the military
side has had to absorb a Methodist training- college
in Horseferry-road. By the time you get this we
may even have commandeered Buckingham Palace,
the British Museum and the Mint.
On a large scale the best tiling that has been
done for Jimbilly Kangaroo is the A.N.A.’s “Anzac”
Buffet. This is going to shift with the Common-
wealth’s military belongings to Horseferry-road
where it will occupy what has hitherto been the
Methodies’ gymnasium.
Ada Crossley had several loads of convalescents
from Ilarefield and other haunts of our wounded
out at her place the other day. It was a good
Australian party devoid of starch, and Pte. Kan-
garoo, V.C. (or ought to be), said he’d come again
it’ he might, thank you and please.
St. Brigid’s Orphanage, Ryde, has a
hundred stockings to fill on Christmas Eve.
The war has hit this little community pretty
hard, and the gentle sisters make an even
more plaintive appeal than usual.
Bush fires have been extra-attentive to
A. A. Hordern, of the Pitt-street shop. A
cottage \>n the mountains vanished in smoke,
property at Roseville was damaged, and the
Hordern home at Wahroonga was only let
off after a severe caution.
Hanbury Davies has withdrawn from the
chair at Red Cross Executive meetings.
After two years’ service he wants to sit
back, wherefore he named J. 0. Fairfax as
his successor. The committee was agree-
able.
The Women Painters’ Sketch Club had a
rally in its eyrie in Paling’s last week.
It was an amateurish show, with a little
good stuff scattered through it. Mrs. Hed-
ley Parsons in boats-and-bays studies pro-
vided a strong patch or two. Theo Cowan,
Mabel Barling—the latter still specialises
in smiles on the face of the tiger—Mrs.
Hugh Wright and other members hung their
work in the den. Outside, on the landing,
a Young Person studied music on a rnerci-
fully-dumb keyboard.
An Australian nurse on a hospital ship
sends a line :
Towards the end of July life at Anzac was as near
Hell as- one can get it on earth. We were rushed
back from Egypt for the landing at Suvla Bay
early in August. At Lemnos we saw a great sight.
The night was clear and starlit, no wind, and the
water like polished steel. In single file eight
hospital ships passed out, their green lights and
Red Crosses mirrored in the water. AVe followed
later. Stray bullets pattered on our decks like
heavy raindrops before a storm. One of our orderlies
got a spent one in the leg and was quite proud of
it. At one in the morning we received our
first lot of wounded. It’s wonderful how our men
stand the shock of their wounds. They usually say
nothing as they lie waiting their turn, but look at
the doctor and the sisters with a detached but in-
terested air. It’s typical of Australian and Maori-
land wounded to be chiefly curious about what's hap-
pening to the other fellow. At Cape Hellas, though
tons and tons were waiting for them in Egypt, our
men had no comforts of any kind, and were paying
the Greeks sixpence each for vile cigarettes. I’ve
never done much of it, but I’m afraid I shall have
to offer to help hang a few people some day.
Some of the women in Crown-street Hos-
pital are soldiers’ wives. By way of Christ-
mas gifts the Matron asks for nightgowns.
The Queen Victoria Club, Wynyard-street,
will take the bundles.
The Whiting sisters, tvho often came this
way in the frilly race days, have both be-
come engaged—Dorothy to d’Arcy Osborne,
of N. S. Wales, who is at the Front with a
British regiment ; Aileen to Captain G. B.
Walker, an Englishman on General Nixon’s
staff.
St. John Ambulance Association used to be
a poor, modest little thing that tucked itself
away in halls that- suited its tiny form and
shrinking nature. Since the war it has
grown suddenly to bold, adult size. The
Town Hall housed the annual meeting last
week. Chief Justice Cullen emphasised the
fat roll-call of members stirred by the war
to join the nursing classes. Ten thousand
strong this army is already and still growing-
The Repertory Theatre was packed with
enthusiastic friends and relatives of, the
youthful performers in “What Is It.'” de-
scribed as a musical-comedy revue by Stanley
Kilminster. The Eastern Suburbs evi-
dently bristle with young men and maidens
who have a fancy for the footlights. Their
Musical Society has Joe Carruthers,
M.L.C.. as president. Hubert Russell was
in charge of the music, which had some
tuneful rhythms running hard through its
dry belts. A mesmerist, Sarazo, much like
the old woman who sweeps the cobwebs oft
the moon, was the opening novelty. Then
a very small young man, 11. S. Beeston,
seized a long strip of girl, Ethel Lack, and
to the best of his limited inches whirled
her round in the revue manner. They sang
—-breath permitting—in the mazy intervals.
Then more young men grasped their girls
in the wing, and likewise bounded into
action. An Egyptian ballet, with plump
Iris Ward as chief dancer, was about the
best item. But the Sphinx in the back-
ground had apparently been getting into
trouble, and showed a splodge like quarter
of a lb. of underdone beefsteak across the
Gill Windeyer, a daughter of the Julian
Windeyers, of Waratah, weds a Somerset
man, George Whitley, this week. St. An-
drew’s Cathedral hassocks have been dusted
for the occasion. The groom came this way
by the Mooltan. After three years in Col-
ombo the doves go on to England. At least
that is the present intention. But three
years—what will happen within three years?
The Nurse Cavell memorial recital had a
bleak attendance in St. James’s Hall. A
well-meaning reciter, Marcus St. John, with
a phenomenal memory, got down into
Dickens’s Christmas carols, and gave pages
of Marley’s Ghost. Esther Kahn was
satisfactory at the piano, and there were
other contributors. But everybody shivered
under the same wet blanket.
Bob Wade Ferrell, son of a Tamworth
identity and related to the Wade family here,
married Iris Chaffey, of Neminghn, the other
day. A sheaf of arum lilies was a change
from the shower bouquet which is usually
mentioned as “the gift of the bridegroom.”
Inverell is to be the Ferrell home later.
Grace Watson writes of this Anzac buffet:
In a tearoom measuring- 20 by 10 we have given
7500 free meals to our boys. But Man is not the
merely food-consuming animal we thought him. In
this buffet lie is chiefly filled with the joy of
meeting and talking to Australian women. It seems
so small a thing for us to do, yet the gratitude we
get in return is quite overwhelming. . . .The other
Sunday one came in so weak and ill he had to lean
against the wall. Yet he couldn’t be persuaded to
touch anything till he’d looked us all up and down.
“Thank God just to be amongst Australian women
again!’’ he said.
Rolls of honor are going up in every big
school, and that means a search for the names
.of old boys who have fallen iu deathless
Gallipoli. The Marist Brothers, of Dar-
liughurst, are the latest to ask for help on
this trail.
White stockings and black strap shoes
with the wide white-flounced summer rig-
out are a fairly popular alternative for the
stuffy high boots of October. Unfortunately
deer-like ankles are not built for every
woman’s support, and the white hose seems
to give the solid-footed damsel an extra
inch where she bulges over her shoe-leather.
The Veterans’ Home committee is reported
to have been present to a woman at Bare
Island the other day. The G.-G. and Lady
Helen were the pivots of the occasion ; but
soldier Finn got the ear of the old men. and
cracked wi’ them of battles long since won
and lost.
Good-looking Molly Lyons, only daughter
of Tom Slattery, has changed her name
again. After supporting herself with consid-
erable spirit as leader of a fashionable little
orchestra, she allowed tea-merchant Claud
Gaut to persuade her to say “Yes.” He is a
widower with a son who shortly leaves for
the usual somewhere.
Mosman wedding bells rang out for the
orange-blossom party at which J. 11.
McKnight Cowen and Ruby Jolly were the
chief persons. Limerick lace, pearls and
waxy orange blooms trimmed the bride’s
frock. The honeymooners sampled the new
Brisbane express on their way to their home
on the Tweed River.
There is to be a cafe chantant in the
Wentworth Hotel on Thursday evening in
aid of the IStli Battalion Comforts Fund,
with competitions for Balaclava caps, bou-
doir caps (not for the trenches), knitted
socks and mittens. Mrs. Harry Finn and
Miss Knox are to be referees.
A half-crown fund to meet the out-of-
pocket exes, of artists who give their ser-
vices at camp concerts is the latest notion.
To push it on, a meeting called by Mrs. G.
Earp was held the other day at Queen Vic-
toria Club.
Yours affectionately,
VANDORIAN.

MELBOURNE CHATTER
Melbourne, December 6, 1915.
My Dear Yarrie, —
Lady Helen went out to Camberwell on
Saturday afternoon to “open” a home for
convalescent soldiers. The place was
already in working order when her Ex. got
there; but the social incantations had to be
intoned. Half a dozen sick soldiers were
on view and a whole army of girl helpers
were at hand to lionise and hyaena them.
These girls formed up as a guard-of-honor
(a fluffy, frilly comic-opera-chorus guard)
in a double tile to greet the vice-regal lady.
As soon as that business was over, they
busliranged the visitors with collection-
boxes. Camberwell district has taken over
the place and will support it until the end
ot the war—whenever that may be. The
home is the private residence of the Flin-
ders-lane Doerys, and Mrs. Doery will re-
sume possession when the guns have stopped
for good. There are magnificent lawns,
flower beds and shrubberies, and the pad-
docks slope down the hill to the White
llorse-road, which runs away into the hills.
Y a dyH e I en had Captain Haskett Smith to
A.D.C. her home. The Captain was in khaki,
with a dash of red in it. Sounds like a
cocktail, doesn’t it?
Mr* Leslie Stuart lied Crossed in style
at her Toorak place on Saturday. It was a
te —but not the usual sort. The small
daughters of the house and a few hundred
school friends divided the responsibility and
the honors. Grown-ups merely helped in
menial capacities. Young matrons felt the
weight of their years sufficiently when they
scrambled up _ the domestic plum-trees to
decorate the high branches with flags; but
when it came to helping in the strenuous
trench game (Turks v. Australians) the
matter became serious. A' regiment of
adults manned the refreshment-stalls. Mrs.
Stuart, Mrs. Geoffrey Syme, Mrs. Colonel
Waters, Mrs. McPhail and Miss Wylie were
the company officers. There were ponies
to ride and Belgian dogs drawing little carts.
Miss Martin and Miss Simonsen danced
continuously behind a counter of sweets
while ravenous young customers pranced be-
fore it This corner was very profitable.
Miss Aankivale helped the children with
their jumble stall. Everything on view had
been made by the jumping, shouting guests,
and some of the exhibits were funny. You’d
never have thought there were so manv pen-
wipers in the world. And a staring, pallid
nurse-doll with a monster Red Cross sewn
amoss her heartless bosom with great white
stitches, had to be raffled at last, she was
m such demand. Mrs. Jenner struck the
happy idea of dressing as the Old Woman
\\ ith a Hundred Pockets. She wore a poke
bonnet, a Paisley shawl and a crinoline
skirt made of flounces, with pockets innu-
merable. The children bought packets from
the pockets and seemed tickled to death with
the mysterious little gamble.
Veteran surgeon .Tames Ryan got out of
his armchair the other evening to lecture in
the Melbourne Town Hall for the good of the
Health Society. Ryan is a chevalier of the
Legion d Honneur, and he told something of
what led up to the decoration. He was
attached as a surgeon to a French field am-
bulance during the war of 1870, and the
moving tale of his doings was worth the
ninepence for which the collection-plate
yawned. It is a coincidence, by the way,
that James Ryan’s consulting-rooms, among
tbe timber at the t0 P of Collins-street,
should have been for a long period almost
opposite the house of that other Ryan—.
Colonel Charles “Plevna,” sometime Consul-
General for Turkey, and one of Victoria’s
early representatives at Gallipoli. As for
the Health Society, enriched by the offering
which followed James Ryan’s reminiscences,
it was started a good many years ago, with
the loorak surgeon as one of its vice-presi-
dents, mainly to combat the spread of tuber-
culosis.
All Saints’, St. Kilda, has a fine card up
its white lawii sleeve for its Red Crossings
next Saturday. Lady Helen will sit with
the congregation at the feet of Griffith Tay-
lor while he tells the story of the Western
party in Scott’s last expedition. The pro-
ceeds will help buy materials for the Saints’
summer war-sewing.
The motored classes now turn up in
fifties to drive the returned wounded from
i ort Melbourne. The other morning the
warriors arrived very early, and only the
hrst flight of typists and shop-girls were
abroad to do the handkerchief-waving. Gov-
ernor Stanley was in the procession behind
the wheel of his little grey car. A grey felt
hat was pulled down well over his eyes and
he was inside his oldest grey lounge suit
the one without the waist. Nothing like
old duds for comfort. A batch of nurses
returned with this crowd and got an up-
roarious welcome. There were three grey
sisters in flowing grey veils, and a motor full
ot cheery nurses in white dresses, red capes
and big white panama hats. They looked
rather like a new ballet. One of them
j*.

1 he Rutter Clarkes’ gorgeous place is to he
used tor another and more elaborate carnival
on Saturday. The garden is so large that
plans were printed, and secretary Tainsh
went about with the sheets of Bristol board
while the workers studied it like strategists
looking for the best pitch for Aunt Sally
and other insanities. The Clarke boys have
a den below the billiard-room full of stuffed
animals and weird spider-tliings. The minute
she saw it a Sassiety darling offered her
services as a fortune-teller, and joy-
fully started to plan some witch-robes of cob-
web grey chiffon. At any rate, the fashion-
able dressmakers do well when a sweet crea-
ture with money to spend gets such an in-
spiration.
Mrs. Tallis, who had just arrived, brown
as a berry, from Sydney and the surf, became
responsible for the musical programme. She
promised to bring the "Kick-in” people dur-
ing the afternoon and first-line amateurs for
the evening when the professionals were on
duty at the theatre. Ethel Dane and Tom
Shellford are now planning a coffee-singsong
from a romantic rose-covered side balcony.
Miss D’Ebro motored a bunch of footlight
stars out to the Rutter Clarke gardens to
select their pitches, and the Clarke butler
gloomily watched the rehearsal foolery from
the dining-room window. When you have
buttled solemnly in a house a sudden inrush
of clowns must seem like profanation. A
French village fair will rage, and school-
girls, drawn from the very best flapper finish-
ing factories, will wear French clothes and
sell bazaar truck as fast as they can. Sports
are being organised by the girls and boys of
Melbourne public schools. Jennie Brenan is
coaching small regiments in Morris dances;
and organiser Tommy Cochrane—God bless
him ! —and secretary Lynn Teare are rushing
round, followed by Langdon girls, Joan
\Y eigall and the Bells. Any person who
wants to see them has to run hard. All the
Baillieu womenfolk are helping, also all the
available Sargoods, and the Hennessy dame
is lending her most benevolent smile The
spoil is earmarked for the French Red Cross
and the French Societe Maternelle.

left by the Malwa. Mrs. Law’s husband has been in

Two mme S. Australians—Leonard Darling and
J. L. Sandford—have got commissions in the Eng-
lish Field Artillery. I don’t know why they didn't
seek them in the Australian service. In fact, I
hardly know why I’ve told you that these two got
English commissions, seeing that scores of equally
good young men have got equally good commissions
in equally good Australian forces without any
mention at all.
Mayor Simpson has called public attention to the
unpoliced condition of this religious centre, and pro-
duces statistics to show that every Adelaide con-
stable has 947 potential criminals to keep his eye on.
We must either have more religion or more police-
men, it seems. The citizens’ reply is to say that
they will do without Mayor Simpson. Aid. Isaacs
beat him at the poll on Saturday. Here, you know,
we vote our own mayors in and out, and don’t
leave it to the councillors. They have enough sins
of their own.
Lady Galw'ay left for Victor Harbor on
Thursday afternoon, taking with her Miss d’Erlanger
and Miss Macaulay. She is to eat the salt breezes
for a couple of months. Meanwhile the Governor
remains in town, principally to help with the
approaching recruiting campaign.
“Dick” Butler, our frequent Premier, has just
indulged in his 65th birthday. He has been 25
years in Parliament, so apparently it agrees with
him.
Summer has brought its usual crop of snake-yarns.
Here is the best to date. A farmer at Blanche-
town, who decided to sow a dried swamp for hay,
now finds that he can’t put in his teams to cut the
crop or his stock to eat it, because of the number of
tigei snakes that infest it. He just tears his hair
and leaps on the edge of the 300-acre patch that
is estimated to average two or three tons to the
acre! The whole puzzle will probably have a fire-
stick thrust into it.
Passed out at North Adelaide on Thursday James
Harvey, who left Scotland for Victoria in 1855, and
came to this State nine years later. He was for a
time superintendent of the smelting works at Walla-
roo, and was one of the trustees of the Walter
Hughes estate. Mrs. Harvey died in September, and
both the sons are at the war.
An oversea obituary is that of Mrs. A. W. Ware,
whose husband was mayor here at the time of the
York -visit.
That enthusiastic advocate of temperance, Mr.
Smeaton, found himself in unusual company the
other day when he steered a deputation of licensed
victuallers and hotel-brokers. He got through with
the help of a glass of water.
The annual fete in aid of the Children’s Hospital
was opened by Lady Galway on Saturday, and drew
a satisfactory crowd to the grounds. Her Ex. took
the opportunity to put in a word for the local
charities which are liable to be overlooked these
times.
Mr. Baillie Hamilton, who was private secretary
at Government House a few years ago, is acting as
secretary to the Belgian Field Hospital.
The Laidlaw family, which for many
years has occupied Springbank, one of
rural Heidelberg’s desirable dwelling-places,
is gradually melting away. Old Hubert
Laidlaw and bis wife have been dead for
some years. Mrs. James Harbinson, the
Laidlaw who married Brighton’s doctor-
poet, is gone; and last week Isabella, one
of the remaining spinsters, faded out at the
commencement of her seventh decade. The
poet Harbinson, by the way, is married
again; and he has published no more vol-
umes of verse on vellum. Vellum comes
expensive in these days of dear mutton.
Big Dr. Don Dennett sends a much-cen-
sored coo-ee from the Australian Base to
say that he is hard at work. He can handle
a gun as well as a lancet and shouldn’t feel
strange in his regimentals, for he put in
eight years’ military work in Victoria in
the piping times of peace, when an officer
in uniform was stared at as if he were a
rare bird, and Defence was mostly small
boy.
A Trinity College girl, Mrs. Walter Sum-
mons, is doing Bed Cross nursing in Egypt,
whither she freelanced after her husband
had gone away with the Army Medical
Corps. Major Walter Summons, sometime
of Camberwell, was at latest hard at work
at Heliopolis; and his elder brother, Cap-
tain W. H. Summons, of Kew, has been
wearing a perfectly good motor-car to the
bone in his own particular local job—which
is_ to keep an expert eye on the sanitation
of the Victorian military camps. It is his
own motor-car, too.
Humor (a lady who becomes no more
truthful with advancing years) whispers the
engagement of a middle-aged tenor, who lives
most of his life on the Block, to a girl who
is young, rich and beautiful. What more
has any tenor a right to expect?
Since they took to summering among the
gums and gullies of Warrandyte, an old
mining-camp on the southern Yarra-slopes
beyond Heidelberg, Professor Laver and his
wife have started a series of annual bush
At Homes for past and present students
of the University Conservatorium, and the
next Outbacking is to happen on Saturday
week. There are IS miles of road-travel
to negotiate before one gets to Warrandyte ;
but it is satisfactory for the weary, way-
faring musician to know that there will
be a nice bit of damper and a pannikin of
hot tea awaiting the dusty arrival of the
four-in-hand.
Bachelor Frank Plain, mostly of Collins-
street, is now a lieutenant in King Ed-
ward’s Horse. Ham has been a well-known
figure in Melbourne for many years. Of
late he had been growing a little grey
about the ears and wearing that well-
rounded look which often comes to prosper-
ous 40. The late Cornelius Job, M.L.C.,
of Armadale, was Lieutenant Ham’s father,
and Theophilus was his uncle. How a mere
Frank came into the family I don’t know.
Gordon, the youngest of the Ross-Soden
widow’s sons, recently pulled a perfect
stranger out of the Thames and is to get
a Humane Society award for it. When
the crowd of onlookers heard that the hero
was an Australian they gave him three
cheers extra. The young man is learning
to fly for his country.
The generous Elizabeth Hebden spinster
has founded a liundred-a-year scholarship at
Melbourne Grammar School in memory of
her brother Charles, a rich N.S.W. squatter
who. died early in 1915. Miss Hebden has
nominated the small son of the late General
Bridges as first holder—a kind and thought-
ful act which will be appreciated by all good
Australians.
Lilia Reidy, of Tasmania, has devised an
Australian calendar for 1016. The greeny-
khaki background is decorated with fat
little gum blossoms, and real pendant gum
leaves have sketches of khaki heroes painted
on them.
The crowd had its puzzle at the Amy
Castles third recital. ’Cellist Gladstone
Bell was getting near the end of Popper’s
“Tarantella” when an unknown lady in
street costume glided on to the platform.
Bell was too intent upon worming his way
into the confidence of his instrument to
notice her. but his accompanist, Harold
Whittle, did. Harold gave one apprehensive
glance, then bent over his keyboard and
finished the item. As soon as the crowd
started to applaud the maiden stepped to
the front of the platform and bowed with
gracious condescension. Whittle and Bell
scampered from the scene, and the girl, sit-
ting down at the piano with the air of a
genius about to commit a masterpiece, fell
at once into a commonplace. As much of the
audience as was not dazed and shocked was
doubled up with mirth. But the perform-
ance did not last long, for a humorless
attendant appeared, took the damsel by the
arm and gently led her from the platform.
She did not struggle. She had had her
crowded minute, and was satisfied.
Mrs. Thomson, president of the Malvern
Ladies’ Rifle Club, supplied a trophy for
competition last Saturday. Miss E. C. Hope
Campbell was the winner, and the whole day
was filled with the crackle of musketry.
Mrs. Polock (captainess) won the second
prize, presented by the Club’s coach. G. F.
Williams. This week there will be a com-
petition for a gold medal; also a match
between married and single she-members.
Mrs. Brigadier-Colonel Tivey has become a
member, and Mrs. Foster Wood is shoulder-
ing a gun. Amongst the enthusiasts who are
already in the ranks are Mrs. Edmund
Collins, Mrs. Ernest Keep, Mrs. Appleton
and Mrs. Weston Pett. Are we down-
hearted, girls? Certainly not!

“Lou” : 1 was.wielding tlie pick and banjo
ia a gang on a big channel job once, when the ganger
got his “slip” for giving his “pink eye” (pet) an
easy job. The riding “red light” then unknowingly
picked out another “spur” in the horde, gave him
the book, and rode away. About 10 minutes later
the new serang “chipped” a cove, and that was the
beginning of his end. Hopping- out of the “gutter”
the coot snapped out at him, “Write it out.” As the
flabbergasted ganger produced the book the coot’s
mate straightened up and drawled, “Scratch me off,
too.” Then someone further down the line called
out, “Rub me out while yer at it” ; and his mate
followed suit with, “Give me a leaf, old cock.” Then
another, “I’m snatcliin’ it, too” ; and another, “Make
me up.” “Tally me an’ Jim and Hob,” roared one
of the batter men; and so on until about 23 dun-
gareed demons were “waiting for their time.” When
the slips were all written out the jobless ganger sent
the nipper after the “head,” and when lie rode back
the pennies were spinning and the silver jingling
and changing hands. He asked no questions, but
merely selected another ganger, who called out,
“Blow up,” and the picks went swinging again with
the late ganger on the end of one of them.
# • * * *
“F.H.IL”: Re women riding bullocks (B.
4/11/’l5). Only a couple of years ago it was not an
unusual sight to see two young girls riding on
bullock-back into Niangala (N.S.W.), and not a year
ago a woman and her two daughters travelled by
horseback, unattended by men, from Swamp Oak to
Newcastle, a matter of over 200 miles. They camped
out whenever the feed and weather were favorable,
and —don’t miss this ! —they carried hat-boxes on the
pack-horse. Talking about riding astride by ladies
1 remember, 20 years ago, seeing a Camden (N.S.AV.)
girl ride a bucking black horse to a standstill in her
brother’s saddle, and 12 years back the auctioneer’s
daughter, a girl of 13, could be seen, mounted on her
black pony and riding in a man’s saddle, wheeling
Queensland fats at the Gilgandra (N.S.AV.) yards any
week.
P K 'k : hat class of farriers does
“IY.P.C.” (B. 11/11/T5) associate with that lie lias
to come to the Abo. page for the cure of his prad's
corns? They are due to defective shoeing, and there
are two remedies. (1) Knock the shoes .off and turn
the turkey afield for a six-weeks’ spell. (2) Cut the
corn out and burn a thimbleful of turpentine in the
hole so made, after which replace the shoes, putting
a leather pad underneath them.
* * * *
“Horse Sense”: To cure his prad's corns
lei; “W.P.C.” (B. 11/11/’l5) try paring down the
sole between the bar and the well. Keep on parin :v
til) the horn looks healthy. If it refuses to look
healthy keep on paring. It may even be necessary to
expose the sensitive sole. In that case let “W.P.C.”
see that his knife is sharp, and cut away from the
deepest part of the cavity and not down on to the
sensitive structure. Follow? Pour in tincture of
iodine straight, plug lightly with cotton wool or
oakum, put on a bar shoe, running a leather sole
underneath. I wouldn’t give “W.P.C.” this informa-
tion if I believed he lived within 50 miles of a vet.
or had soft hands.
* * * *
“Spreader”: “W.P.C.” (B. 11/11/T5)
wants to know what will cure corns in the foot of
a horse. Nothing. Acetic acid does a bit of good
at times ; the knife also has a useful effect, tempo-
rarily; but the real dinkum specific hasn’t been
found so far. Half the valuable draught horses that
are worked on the blocks of Sydney have corns, and
there’s a fortune yearning for anyone who can effect
a quick and permanent cure. If the patients are
taken on to blacksoil and worked as farm horses
through a wet winter the corns disappear as long as
that particular class of work is allotted to them, but
shoe them up again in the spring and have a fresh
go at the blocked streets, and 99 per cent, of them
get worse than ever.
* * * »
“James Jam”: “Bandy Andy” (B.
4/11/’l5) wants to know why the lonely bottle beside
the track in Queensland acquires that fine violet
blush while window glass remains colorless; It's a
matter of sui light. The sand which goes into the
furnace and comes out the bottle-oh’s prey contains
iron, which gives the glass a deep green tinge best
seen in the domestic-beer envelope; so when a white
glass is wanted a little manganese is added to the
stew to neutralise the color of the iron. But very
strong light and long exposure to it decomposes the
compound the manganese has formed, and the purple
shade which is characteristic of that substance de-
velops. The windows don’t get so much direct sun-
light, so the change is much slower, but I have seen
the first faint beginnings of it there, too. Radium
does the same trick much quicker than the sun. I've
seen a glass funnel which had something to do with
that elusive substance, and it was quite a deep
amethyst in less than a week.
“Moana”: Frequently there is trouble in
the King Country (M.L.) because some brown man
raids a cemetery and digs up Maori skeletons in
pursuit of Hlcis and other trifles. In fact, one of the
chief industries in the district is digging up deceased.
The other day a local lienare was dragged before the
beak at Rotorua and charged with desecrating four
graves to recover curios. He was fined £2 and
smitten with a mountain of costs totalling
£l5 7s. Gel. “By kolly, Costs him te hot stuff,”
lienare said with a grin as he left the court.

"by not have some of the reserves ring-barked 2
Contrary to general belhi, this is the best way of
getting good timber. 1 have seen land on which this
Plan was followed, and row, after five years, there
are over 2000 saplings of front three to eight inches
in diameter and up to 35ft. in height on an average
to the acre. Hung.and fired, the reserves would
produce thousands of seedlings to every acre, and
being very close together they would grow very
tall. They would require thinning by cutting out
the crooked ones, and the others should be left for
about 15 years. After that the trees should be
thinned again to about 200 to the acre, leaving the
balance for about another 15 years. By this method
the timber would upend itself very high, with long
barrels, and a big proportion of the wood would get
*us at least one good word from Posterity.
* * * *
“C.11.N.”: Simply marvellous feats are
accomplished by the Papuan nigger in his canoe.
Recently I was held up for two days while attempt-
ing to cross Iluon Gulf in a motor-boat, and eventu-
ally, by hugging the coast, I managed to get around,
though I expected to bo capsized any moment. I
was still wondering at my own luck when- across
comes a tiny dugout with outrigger and mat sails '
spread to the gale. One boy of perhaps 12 years was
astride of the outrigger to help keep the balance,
and the dugout fairly flew through the Water. A
thousand of the best would not have tempted me
to try it in such a craft; but it is an everyday
occurrence for Brown Brother.

“Doubter”: Will some Abo.-scribe please
oblige me by getting bitten by a poisonous snake
and trying an antidote outfit that is advertised exten-
sively? It consists of a small tool like an awl, io
make a few punctures around the bite, and about
ioz. of Condv’s crystals, which are supposed to be
well rubbed in. This is guaranteed to be effective,
but I’d like somebody to test it with a jury handy.
“Warre”: The Australian shearer has a
“fiscal” complaint against the cook, as well as u
“l oisonous.” The modern dough-slinger is demand-
ing and getting 6s. per week per man, though the
Arbitration Court allows him but 4s. Thus with a
mess of 30 men the poisoner’s wages, after allowing
slushy his third, come to £6 per week. Add to
this tucker (at least 155.) and fat (which if sheep
are any way decent and the operator a scientific
“roaster” easily brings in 15s, more), and his wage
is £7 10s. nett, wet or fine. This is considerably
more than the shearer can afford in these days of
high prices and short sheds, and he is hinting darkly
that the crimson cook is looking 1 for unamiability.
“Spreader”: “Nick Bottom” and “D.D.”
both know of a boy-champion milk extractor, hut
what sort of cows do they milk? There are cows
and cows, and in any dairy district a herd of 20
could he got together that no man living could
milk and strip dry in one hour 55 minutes. On the
other hand, 20 very easy cows, not heavy milkers
and picked out so that none of them hold their milk
back, would be nothing out of the ordinary for a
real fast boy to milk in 3600 seconds.
. “Ino”: “Nick Bottom” (B . 11/11/T5)
says ho knows a boy who can milk 20 cows in 60
minutes. Very likely lie can. in a sort of way, but
how much milk will lie take from each cow anil
how much will lie leave behind? Some cows give
three or four gallons of milk and some less than a
quart. One milker can milk some cows in little
more than half the time he could milk others giving
the same quantity. Then some extractors will strip
till only a drop can be flipped out at a time; others
leave off as soon as it ceases to come fairly fast.
There is no proof that the boy who drains 20 cows
in an hour is faster than one doing 18 in an hour
and a half. I have seen eight cows that the fastest
milker could not milk clean in an hour.

A WET SUNDAY.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
In clammy cerements swathed and bound
The earth lies stark, with scarce a breath,
Numbed by a ceaseless monotone
As passionless as icy Death.
The clock ticks loudly through the room,
And, ’mid the shadows gathered there,
Chill desolation like a cloud
Hangs heavy in the listless air,
AA'hile sorrow, like, a wandering wind/
Sighs through my heart’s recesses lone,
And to the drumming of the rain .
Proffers a wistful undertone.
The dog lies sleeping, nose to tail.
But twitehingg of his muscles show
How tea singly across his dreams
The ghosts of rabbits come and go;
And ghosts come round me as 1 lie,
Sad pilgrims from the distant years,
To stir upon life’s cheerless hearth
The ashes of old hopes and fears.
The still, grey gloaming, thick witli rain,
The near horizon’s muffled round,
The dark trees drooping motionless,
The teeming sky and sodden ground,
And all the heartache of the day—-
The empty day oppressed with rain,
AA lien ghostlike through the whispering gloom
The past returns to grieve again—
All these have blurred the oft-read page
AVith visions mournful fancy fills,
Unhappy drift of idle dreams
AA'an as the mist that wraps the hills.
At times the wet earth seems to sigh,
At times a bough moves restlessly
As though the smothering memories lie
Too heavily on grass and tree.
And mingling with the drip! drip! drip!
Such sadly cadenced murmurs come—
Echoes of voices long unheard,
Phrases from lips for ever dumb.
And wounding with remembrance keen
l-’ace after face before me swims,
Till, in vain effort to assuage
The misery of idle limbs,
I toss away the book, and move
Irresolutely through the room,
Absently stir the smouldering logs,
And stare unseeing through the gloom.
Yes, these grey days are Memory’s own,
AVhen o’er and o’er she sifts the years,
Her eyelids heavy with regret,
Her eyes abrim with useless tears.
VICTORIA.
SYD. C.

liy I luke”: The Maori has a commis-
sai-ißt dodge that I can recommend from practical
experience. Shoot, snare or bomb a pigeon, kaka or
tni. Clean and pluck him, then make some stones
red-hot, and ?tow them in the bird’s interior. Wrap
him in fern leaves or flax blades, sprinkle plentifully
with water, cover with your macintosh or coat, and
tie on top of your swag. By the time you reach
your next resting-place a warm meal will be ready
for you, cooked in first-chop style. Tangata calls
this recipe tunu-haere, which means “cook-as-you
go.”

I>areoo ’: The little black ant is the pest
of the bushman’s camp, climbing in myriads over the
tucker table. The sugar-tin is a seething mass, the
lioney-pot crawling, and the condensed milk a horror,
filling bushy’s cup to the brim with bitterness and
blasphemy. I supply a remedy, which is absolutely
efficient. Get four strands of hair from a horse’s
tail, wiap them round the table-legs and secure with
cotton. That’s the terminus for black-ant opera-
tions in that direction.

, L.E.”: Fergus McTartan was a most
y squatter, but he met a strange proposition when
he gave a S’Army man a job. Once he watched
lobias Cymbals ploughing. It was a scorching dav,
and Toby gradually stopped the plough and fell on
his knees in a cool place. Fergus came along, and
Toby prayed with great fervor. “An’ what may ve
be doin’ doon there?” he queried. “Praying,” re-
plied the ex-sinner. “Satan attacked me. I’m often
attacked like this.” The tyre of McTartan’s patience
blew out. “No funny bezniss here! I engaged ve
to ploo fram sax i’ the morning till sax at nicht.”
1 he" his piety asserted itself. “But, me guid mon,
ye kin pray a’ nicht, an’ gae doon on yer marrow
bones, an’ hae a real guid fecht wi’ Satan, an’ the
Lord be wi’ ye in yer grrreat afflection.” Tobias
rolled up his tambourine that night and headed along
the hard and narrow track.

luldyson” : It all happened away back in
ISTB. There had been startling gold discoveries over
the border, and the Gridiron Lead was drawing men
from all the Victorian fields. Threepenny went with
the tide. There were several reasons why Three-
penny should go. One was in the instinct of a
digger which possessed
“Traybit”; another was
Mrs. Threepenny’s studied
insolence. They had been
married only a year or so,,
but already all distant
hills were green and re-
freshing to Threepenny in
contrast with his imme-
diate ills. He went to
Gridiron Lead, and worked
there for two years, doing
fairly well. Billy Three-
penny had neglected to
tell Mrs. Threepenny where
he was going, possibly to
avoid the necessity of ex-
plaining when he intended
returning. It had been
his intention never to . re-
turn ; but, having money
and feeling good, and ab-
sence having made the
heart more hopeful, he
eventually packed and
headed for Tollbar once
more. “After all,” he
told Kyley, '“the blanky
house is mine.” As he
came over Till’s Bridge
into the township with
Kyley a house was coming
out. It was a four-
room e d, weatherboard
house on a big jinker
drawn by 14 horses. As
they looked a woman put her head out
of the front window, and a man stepped
from the front door, and stood talking
to the driver. “Come on,” said Three”
penny, and he pulled his mate away off
the track. They walked through Toilbar.
“But where’s your house?” said Kyley.
Threepenny sighed. “That was my house
we passed on the road,” he said “Go
oral” gasped Kyi ey. “Yes,” said Threepenny,
an that was my missus poked her ’ead outer the
winder. The man what come from the door was Ben
vee an old flame of the missus’s.” “Geewillikins!”
said Ivyley. “Yes,” said Threepenny, “we blessed
near interrupted the helopement!”

“Flinderino”: How many know that flying
foxes are edible? Two Northern Territory team 5 -
sters were conveying stores from Port Darwin to a
Roper River station when flood-waters hemmed them
m for 18 days. Their meat soon gave out, and for
a fortnight they lived on flying-fox cutlets and
stew. They agreed that the tucker was good
though the smell was outrageous. Also it was con-
tagious. When they eventually arrived at the sta-
tion nobody, not even the blacks, would come within
smelling distance of them. Dead whales and defunct
camels are as violets compared with weather-beaten
teamsters exuding essence of flying fox.

“Moana” : I wish to enter a mushroom for
tha world’s championship. It came up unexpectedly
at Poxton (M.L.) the other day, pushed aside the
cow-shed, obscured the landscape and caused a dis-
turbance generally. When it had finished expanding
it was 4ft. 7in. round the edge and tipped the scales
at 131 b.

“Barcoo”: I can’t swear to a cure for
fistula in a horse, but next time “Merrigal” (B.
21/10/’ 15) and others see signs of the trouble in
front of the wither let them clip the hair short and
rub turpentine in hard on both sides of the neck as
long as the patient will stand the attention. Shortly
afterwards foment with water close-up boiling. One
week’s spell and all signs of the coming fistula dis-
appear.

“Lou”: Hard luck? Well, how’s this?
The Arbuthnot family, who owned the biggest saw-
mills . on the Murray, recently lost a steamer on
the river by fire. They built another and that was
burned on the Wakool (N.S.W.). Last month then-
mills at Koondrook were cleaned right out by the
demon. Everything but some sawn timber went up
in smoke, and there was no insurance, apparently
owing to some oversight in regard to a policy.
* * * *
‘Myall”: There’s a good deal of jaw about
the abilities of the jumbuck barber, but mighty
little credit is given to the average “blue-tongue”
or picker-up for the work performed by him. The
lad who can pick up 700 fleeces in a day is no
slouch, and if he also has to do the “brooming” he
has no time left to discuss such vital questions as
the probability of the Huns invading Greenland or
the advisability of building a strategic railway from
Bourke to The Balonne. I know one youth of 15
who picked up 851 fleeces in a day, besides sweep-
ing the board, picking the locks, and attending to
the innumerable calls for tar. If any Abostralian
can trot along his better I will give the boy a job
and guarantee him the full wage of 37§d., irrespec-
tive of age.
* * * *
“Fait Lux” : Mount Magnet (W.A.) pos-
sesses two curiosities in cockatoos. One always places
a tobacco tin over his head before retiring. Take
away his tin hat and he refuses to go to roost, nor
will he allow anyone else to sleep until it is re-
turned. The other bird is a black brother, and has
been living a domesticated life for years. Anyone
else know of a black cockatoo living in captivity?
“D.F.”: A white leghorn hen, appropri-
ately owned bv one Drake, who lives at Little
Swanport (Tas.), has laid an egg which weighs
over a third of a lb. —sJozs. This is claimed as a
record in hen-fruit; and if anybody can disprove it
let him kiss the Book and hand over the evidence.
* * * *
“Hay”: I should like to put in a word for
the quandong as an ornamental tree, worthy of the
consideration of any suburban botanist. Whether
covered with a crop of its peach-like fruit or just
its glossy green leaves, this graceful native, with
its dark red-brown slender stem and graceful
branches, is one of the most beautiful of our many
beautiful bush dwellers. Its acid-tasting fruit makes
a very good jam, and, being easily stoned, may
also be dried and. stored away for winter use.
There are two kinds of native peach, a dark-red
variety and one much paler in color. The latter
is very sour, and not such a valuable adjunct to the
menu as the red kind. The curiously-crinkled
nuts used to be bored to make bead necklaces for
the bush girls, and the old-time bushman when
he didn’t affect a piece of mutton-shank ivory used
to carve an elaborate ring from a quandong nut
through which to pass the ends of his bright silk
neckerchief. This sort of work required a special
kit of carving tools, and it took a month of Sundays
to complete a single ornament.
* * * *
“H.8.W.”: The oddest piece of grafting
I have seen is in Maitland (N.S.W.) cemetery. Most
people have seen peaches growing on plum trees,
etc., but has anyone come across a bluegum sprout
on a camphor-laurel tree? Apparently a branch of
the higher bluegum was broken off by the wind, and
in its fall caught in a fork of the laurel, where it
has been green for years.
“Warrego”: “Barcoo’s” bush woman (B.
1/11/ IS), who rode 90 miles straight out to take
her sick child to a doctor, has a mate. A quarter of
a century ago there lived in a bend of the Warrego,
25 miles from Charleville (Q.), a selector, his wife
and their two sons, aged three years and one year
respectively. One day when the father was away
rounding up horses the elder boy jabbed one of the
blades of a pair of scissors into an eye. His mother
didn’t waste time. She just removed the weapon and
then rode, carrying both children, into Charleville.
Her action saved the child’s eye. I know, because
she was my mother and a white scar on my brother’s
right iris concludes the evidence.
SAVING BY FAITH.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
A crowd had gathered on the pier
To see the boys in khaki go,
And when the parting tick was near
A moving truck upset the show,
And overboard went Israel Mo.
The man was fat and built to float;
He might have done quite well, they say,
Had he not opened wide his throat
To yell for help, and so made way
For half the waters of the bay.
Strode forth a soldier tall and fine,
Threw down his garments one by one,
And plunged into the rippling brine.
Two minutes and the work was done—
I. Mo nas drying in the sun.
AY hen drained completely Mo arose,
The soldier hero he dismissed:
You vant rewarding, I suppose.
1 vos kvite safe, sir, 1 insist—-
I am a Christian Scientist!”
VICTORIA.

Lipton’s
Tea.
By Special Appointment to Ilis Majesty the
King, Purveyors to the late Queen Victoria
and King Edward the Seventh, the King and
Queen of Italy, the King and Queen of Spain,
and to every Viceroy of India for the past
25 years.
THERE can be no better recom-
mendation to the virtues of Lip-
ton’s Tea than the fact that in
India, which is the greatest Tea-pro-
ducing country in the world, Lipton’s
Tea has reigned supreme in the Vice-
Regal Court for nearly an ordinary
working lifetime.
Lipton’s colossal organisation has
been described as “The business on
which the sun never sets.” and Tup-
ton’s Tea as “The Pride of Five Con-
tinents.”
A Medical Opinion of
Lipton’s Tea.
Read what Dr. N. E. Yorke-Davies, the
Famous Nerve Specialist says regarding
Lipton’s Tea: —•
“It has also other advantages besides the
excellence of its quality. It comes from a
British Dependency, and is prepared under
British auspices. It does not pass through
the hands of natives, but it is manipulated
after it leaves the gardens entirely by

“Lee oh” : Exit to somewhere overseas the
red-funnel' coaster Ilaupiri, one-time the
Richmond, for some months past laid up
in Wellington (ALL.) Harbor. The Rich-
mond was at one time about the best-known
steamer in the South Seas. She and the
Herman liner Lubeck, sailing out of Sydney,
pretty well shared the Samoa trade between
them. Donald and Edenborough. of Auck-
land, ran her on the big round, Tonga-
Samoa-Tahiti-Rarotonga, and she panted into
the Waitemata every six or seven weeks
smelling of the isles and jammed to the deck
with freight. Square canvas was carried
on the foremast ip those days, for there
wasn’t much help then for a disabled single-
screw ship in the long tropic stretches. The
old ship’s furnaces were of the accommodat-
ing kind; they burned timber, copra, any-
thing. Once she ran out of coal returning
from Rarotonga and had to put into Sunday
Island, where the crew cut enough firewood
to carry her on to Auckland. Once she
was searched from how to stern by the
Squareheads at Apia for arms and ammuni-
tion, in the days when old Tamasese and
Ills German backers were top dogs in Upolu.
and was policed by a man-o’-war launch
steaming round her from dark till daylight.
O. A. Edenborough, one of the owners, a fine
old all-round adventurer, who had been a
Navy midshipman, a soldier in Maoriland,
free-lance officer in the Texas Rangers, and
South Sea treasure-hunter, used to sail with
her every trip. The Dutchmen hated him
with the hate of the flames of Tophet. but
the French camarades at Papeete loved him
as a, brother.
* * * *
“Q.D.” : There is said to be a pot of wealth
concealed in a cave somewhere near Hamp-
ton, in the Blue Mountains (X.S.W.). The
legend is that when bushranger Lowry and
party stuck up the Mudgee coach and made
a big haul of banknotes, they took the loot
to a spot a few miles from Oberon. and
divided it up at a house which figured
as headquarters. They also, the story runs,
celebrated the occasion joyously in the rum
of the period. A large portion of the notes
they decided to plant as a stand-by. Mem-
bers of the gang, scouts, etc., were watching,
and two of the more commercially-minded
rooted them out again the same night and
replanted them. These two were in turn
watched, and another two gentlemen once
more exhumed and reinterred them ; but they
were again resurrected by two more pirates,
who took the butter-cask containing them
about 25 miles, and planted it in a cave
out Hampton way. What with the efforts
of the police, and the extreme rage of the
different deserving but duped planters, the
successful pirates decided to let matters
stand for several months. Unfortunately,
in the meantime, one got seven years and the
other five years in connection with another
indiscretion. One reformed in gaol, and
swore never to handle the notes again. The
other died or cleared out or something.
Consequently thousands of pounds’ worth of
notes in a butter keg are said to be waiting
in that cave for a finder. The cask was
buried in dry limestone dust, so it might be
quite sound still. But what a fearsome
thing it would be to find the treasure and
discover that the bank that issued the notes
had gone bung.
“D.F.” : That venerable ark, the Kangaroo,
which paddles to and fro across the Derwent
(Tas.), is a standing advt. for the durability
of Australian timber. She is GO this year,
and is tin' oldest steamer built south of the
Line still in commission. Maoriland’s
Stormbird, which is a year older, was built
in Scotland. The Kangaroo’s main timbers
are nearly as strong and sound as when
they were put in. They were knocked
together at Ilobart, and are mostly bluegum.
“XXX”: Justice Rich stated an obvious
truth in his report when he said :
In tliis war between nations the time, is fully ripe
for organising tlie services of all classes of the com-
munity. Professional men have already been mobil-
ised. Business men might well be employed in mat-
ters where their training and experience would be
of the greatest advantage to the military authorities,
e.ff., in connection wiith ordnance and on finance
committees.
These instances were germane to the report;
but further illustrations are obtainable in
matters of transport. Experienced shipping
men would save tens of thousands of pounds
and handle the transports with the greatest
facility. As it is, some young Brass But-
tons from Whitehall messes the service and
keeps freights idling in unknown ports. Rein-
forcements are thus hung up and cargo is not
shipped, which means a direct loss in mili-
tary pay and an indirect loss to the producers
whose goods are eating up interest while
waiting to reach a market.
The “poor farmer” who remains so after
a season or two evidently doesn’t understand
his business. Two of the—er—profession at
Clunes (Vic.), where Peacock comes from,
lately sent some potatoes to Melbourne and
had the misfortune to have them overhauled
by interfering agricultural inspectors. There
were 121 bags altogether. The tops con-
tained large, -floury tubers, the centres
medium-sized samples and the bottoms
chocolate loam, choice but a trifle indigest-
ible. The consignment yielded lGewt. of
earth, and as spuds were then bringing £l2
a ton the honorarium’s fine of £3, with £1 .
costs, will leave the honest agrarians a
nice credit balance if they can get past the
interfering officers next time.
