.®ad Australian : Politeness without sin-
centy is one of the chief characteristics of
the South American, right down to the com-
monest peon, and even the handulo will col-
te* h IS war indemnity with the greatest
suavity of manner, while Ins sawn-ott cant-
Inna, directed at his victim s fifth rib, empha-
sises the divine right of the conqueror. The
bandulo, when not actively engaged in levy-
mg forced contributions, is usually an
arricro or cargador, who attends strictly to
his ncgocio, but always with an observant
eye to possibilities of illegitimate business.
My outfit is pretty well known on the passes
of the Andes, and as my dealings have in-
variably been circumspect where the rights
of arrieros or cargadores were concerned, 1
have always been on the free list, so to
speak. But a short time ago, when I had
reached a point where the mule track ran
along on the side of an almost perpendicular
spur, overhanging a fast-flowing river, 1
struck trouble. It was just about dusk and
my arriero was ahead pushing the pack mules
along, when some gentry concealed on the
upper side of the trail opened fire and two
mules went down, one blocking the road, the
other going over the cliff into the river
Miguel, the arriero , was oil. his mule and
under cover in one act. 1 acted just as
promptly, and reached for lus long .44.
Nothing further happened for about an hour,
and it being black dark by that time, we
made back to the nearest camping ground,
About G a.m. the following morning three
cargadores with whom I was well acquainted
arrived, and after the usual polite saluta-
turns the spokesman, “El Gallito” (the
Little Rooster), put it like this: “Distin-
guido Senor Australiano. We have done
that last night down in the Quebrada del
Tigre which renders it imperative that we
get across the boundary without further
delay; but before we leave the territory we
wish to apologise most humbly for any incop.-
venienee which you may have suffered
through a most lamentable error on our part,
an error which ive fortunately discovered in
time, and tp-tsay that we are leaving you the
two best cargo mules we possess, in order
that you may in a measure recoup your loss,
a loss which we truly deplore. We
would further say that we thank God
that your respected person has not been
subject to any inadvertent violene
part, and, assuring you of our i eaye ,
tinguished consideration, we take o ttj b
commending you to the care of the - three
Cod and His Holy Saints. lhen . IM) lit*'
lifted their sombreros in the uj°- _ _
manner, raked their mules with tuei
inch rowels, and lit out for the nex 1 1 ,

“Devilfish”: Nearly every ketch and fisli-
ing-boat on the Tasmanian coast has a
motor-engine nowadays, and it has always
been the correct thing to get the engines from
ihe other side of the world. But now one
A. B. Buyers, of Hobart, has turned out
front his engineering shop a motor-engine
locally made from top to bottom and pro-
poses to keep on doing it if the public will
use a mere Australian article. All Buyers
wants now is more of them,

“Esmeralda”: “Jaques” (B. 11/11/T5)
asks why every sailing vessel reaching South
America from Newcastle has from 10 to 100
tons of coal short. The explanation is
that owing to the shallowness of most S.
American harbors the coal is lightered ashore,
With a few lumps dropped from each lighter
an astonishing amount of mineral can be
lost. It is reckoned that enough coal lines
the bottom of Callao, ITuanchaco and other
ports to bunker Britain’s fleet for a year.
ALL FOR 3d.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
At a well-known suburban Anglican church,
attended by well-to-do middle-class people, I re-
cently helped the secretary to count the collection.
There were 42 threepenny-bits.
— Letter-writer in Melbourne daily.
The thrummer is a useful coin;
’Twill buy a lot of things,
From (say) a fairly “high” cigar,
Or something cool served in a bar,
Up to a pair of wings!
For 3d. sterling anyone
A picture-show may see,
Or buy the lengthiest of “wets,”
A tram ride, or some cigarettes,
Or—immortality !
“The thrummer is a useful coin”—-
Pear brethren, this my text;
For in this world ’twill help you on,
And will, when you from here have gone,
Admit you to the next!
VICTORIA.
HARRISON O.

POLITICAL
POINTS
More war-time economy in the use of
loan money ! The Tasmanian Government
proposes to build a railway 131 miles long
from Branxholm to Moorina, and has got
the biggest part of the survey done for the
sum of £2816 17s. lOd. Luckily the coun-
try is, on the whole, fairly easy, or the job
might have been really expensive.
Even such a good old Conservative print
as the Hobart Mercury is patting the tiger
on the head and calling it Fido :
Wore our medical men Government servants, and
the whole subject of the nation's health the national
care, as will before many years be the ease in civil-
ised countries, no pains would be spared in instruct-
ing the ignorant amongst us as to the terrible pun-
ishments that follow filthiness and the neglect of
sanitation and hygiene in towns.
\\ ith the zeal of a new convert it out-
does the local Labor party, which is anxious
to nationalise the hospitals and do away
with the collection-box method of gathering
the coin to run them, but has so far fought
shy of the idea of making all the doctors
State servants.
W hile Defence-Minister Pearce is getting
together and training his new 50,000. is
he also getting the 5000 or 10,000 machine-
guns with which they should be supplied to
put them on an equality with the Hun and
the Turk? If so, where is he getting them
made? They can be made in Australia, but
not if the Defence Department allows
trained mechanics to enlist for trench fight-
ing. Some weeks ago a Bendigo engineer of
first-class attainments enlisted. He hasn’t
gone to the Front yet, neither is he employed
on machine-gun making or artillery repairs.
In fact, he is beating the drum in a regi-
mental band.
Labor’s silver jubilee gave Holman,
Flowers and a few others an opportunity to
recall the early days of the N.S.W. Labor
movement, when the orators spoke from
candle-boxes, wore out leather to save
tram-fares and collected coppers to pay
postage. Hughes, on the day of his election
to Parliament, went hungry because he
couldn’t buy a meal—he hadn’t then got
down to apples and nuts, but could enjoy a
steak with anyone, as he can again now.
Things are different these days. N. S. Wales
spends £4OOO in motor-cars that Ministers’
wives may do their shopping in comfort; and
Neilsen gets a few guineas a day to keep at
a distance; and a feed to Carmichael in
London costs £335: and then there are
third-rail test picnics which cost a couple
of thousands, and a score of other little
matters. The martyrs are making up for
the privations they suffered in the bleak
days all right. -At least, some of them are.

1 need not remind you of the enormous financial

So desperate is the position in one part of the coun-
-11 ,v lllal Government is now borrowing more than
ever it did before, in spite of increased revenue from
taxatio "’ an increased harvest and a population
diminished by the departure of men for service in
tlle ann ;' Die financial strength of England, great
though it was, is being strained to the uttermost,
and if we only stand fast it will collapse under the
double strain of war and the voracious demands of
Germany’s good friends Holman and Jack Scaddan.
Brother Huns, the future is full of promise. Let
ns practise rigid economy until the hour when Aus-
tralia lias got the last shilling from our enemy,
Then the British Empire will be rent asunder, for
with the stoppage of borrowed cash Australia would
he faced with the terrible alternative of working for
a living. Ilochl

N, S. Wales Government Gazette lias
a list of persons in the State who hold ex-
plosives. Against 10 German-looking names
are. set no less than 700,000 cartridges, and
against six others 6251 b. of gunpowder. All
these people may be loyal Australians, and it
is not likely that any of them at this time
of day will try any sort of serious fireworks
display ; but in the light of the thoroughness
of the big German conspiracy throughout
the world it seems remarkable that names
that are used in Hamburg and Frankfurt
should be associated with so much sudden
death.
t In a half-hearted sort of speech Premier
Earle introduced the Commonwealth Powers
(War) Bill in the Tas. House of Assembly
and. then left, it in soak while lie talked
again of pubs and took a trip to Melbourne.
The Bill’s chance of passing is so sick, any-
way, that no one is worrying much about
it. The Liberals are solid against it and
had the nerve (after all the talk about the
“crime ’ of dividing the people at this crisis)
to say .that the questions should have been
submitted to the electors. Independent
A hitsitt has the casting-vote and he does
noc like the Bill either. But even if he let
if through the House of Dodery would kick
it out with all the emphasis its gouty foot
will allow.
For an hour oi' so the other morning pro-
ceedings were conducted in the Ma State’s
Leg. Assembly with a dignity which recalled
the spacious days of long ago. Boston, hav-
ing solemnly pronounced John Haynes “a
dirty scoundrel,” and been informed by Jack
Fitzpatrick that he was himself a “boor,”
declared that if Fitzpatrick wanted a smack
on the nose it was only necessary to call him
a few more names. Whereupon Fitzpat.
made a start and said Boston was a.n “igno-
rant country lout,” and Boston rushed over
to reach the nose he wanted to smite. Then
three or four representatives threw them-
selves upon him, and tli e air was rent with
loud shouts and yells and assorted howls.
A little later on Stuart-Robertson intimated
that the Chairman of Committee was a
blockhead, and there was more uproar, and
somebody was subsequently suspended, and
somebody else was dragged out by the Ser-
geant-at-Arms and deposited in the ice-chest,
and a dog rushed into the Chamber and
barked loudly at the Speaker, and Jabez
Wright snored like the last trump on a back
bench, and chaos ran the show. After which
the Assembly took a deep breath, and pulling
itself together, inside five minutes passed
appropriations for £2,762,943 19s. 9d. of
loan, and fled to catch a tram.
The mangled remains of the Electoral Bill,
which was to try to make Tasmania’s Hare-
Clark system of proportional representation
a bit proportional and provide an object-
lesson for the world in this particular line,
came before the House of Dodery the other
day.. After 29 minutes’ discussion they were
ordered a funeral without a division. The
Bill had been so hacked to pieces in the
Assembly and reduced to a series of shreds
and patches dealing with by-elections, keep-
ing State seats warm for members who tried
for the Federal Parliament and failed, and a
few other minor matters that it was not
much odds, anyway.
Horse-doctor Gilruth’s latest report on
the Northern Territory refers to the small
promise of agricultural settlement, and at the
same time declares the climate to be one of
the healthiest in the world for white people.
On this Sydney Telegraph says: “The
opportunity for constructive statesmanship
is still there. What is wanted is the states-
man.” Yes, and what the constructive
statesman ought to construct is the direct
railway from Oodnadatta to Pine Creek.
Failing this, it doesn’t much matter whether
a horse-doctor or a poultry expert is at the
load of affairs in the Territory itself.
Here’s a story that wants looking into :
One of the things the German is raising the wind
with is hops, and he seems to be getting a breeze
from Australia. For some time past cheap parcels
of (ostensibly) Yankee hops have been coming in,
hut there is a sort of German flavor about them.
In 1913 Uncle Sam got only 4700 bales of bops from
Germany, but for the first six months of the war —-
from August, 1914, to January, 1915 —he got in
38,000 bales. This may help to explain how it is
that, with a surplus of production over consumption
of 82,000 bales, U.S.A. has been able during the
past year to export 123,000 bales. If the hops
are sent to America from Germany, and then re-
exported to Australia, the people who handle them
get a rebate of the duty paid when they enter the
1 .S.A., so they have only the Australian duty to
consider. The Tasmanian hopgrowers are on the war-
path, and want Minister Tudor to prevent any German-
American or American-German dumping. But there
seems to be an idea that it is wrong to touch the
tariff during the war.

THE RETREAT.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
There tire only meagre details of Servia’s dogged
retreat. — Cable.
In the gold-and-turquoise glory of a typical Spring
day',
Where the bine Pacific rollers break in silver round
the bay,
We take up our daily paper-, and we open out the
sheet,
Just to read the meagre details of Servia’s retreat.
They make interesting reading, with our morning
tea and toast,
Though at times our gaze will wander up the
promontoried coast
Where the dazzling beaches nestle at the purple
headlands’ feet—
After all, they’re meagre details of Servia’s retreat!
Yet they somehow hold attention ; and the sated eye
returns
Prom the glorious, tranquil seascape to the big,
black type that burns
Like some harsh, corrosive fluid which on heart and
brain will eat,
Etching in the meagre details of Servia’s retreat.
A lightning flash of fancy, and our gaze is far-
away.
Here the sky is gre.y and sullen, and the landscape
grim and grey;
Cold and turbid vims yon river, thickly falls the
cutting sleet,
And a piercing wind and bitter follows Servia’s
retreat.
Through the mist-wrapped, hidden valleys, reached
by gorges far below.
These forbidding, naked rock-peaks, chequered here
and there with snow,
Where the lonely, wind-borne eagle swoops respon-
sive to a bleat,
There’s an army, there’s a people, there’s a nation
in retreat 1
Old men, women, little children, broken soldiers
starved and faint—
God! if any artist secs them what a picture he will
paint 1
They are filthy, ragged, shoeless; they have neither
bread nor meat,
And disease and famine revel in Servia’s retreat!
Like the conies of the Scriptures these be feeble folk
and few ;
But gigantic and relentless are the foemen who
pursue,
Making waste the land with slaughter and foul deeds
none may repeat—•
Oh, we dare mot mention details of Servia’s retreat!
From the San and from the Danube, from Morava’s
fruitful fields
They are marching, weak and weary, but their spirit
never yields!
Still they front the savage Bulgar; still the Hun’s
huge grasp they cheat;
Still the Austrian dead bear witness to Servia’s
retreat!
Fire and sword and rape and outrage and stark
cruelty they know—
A hundred hopeless rearguard fights have brought
their numbers low.
’Tie the wreckage of a Nation drifting southward
through the sleet,
But the world shall ring for ever with the tale of
its retreat!
Not the arrogance of Austria nor the Teuton’s mailed
fist
Could cow that little people marching hidden in the
mist ;
Not the faithless, valorous Bulgar nor the Turk, no
more effete,
Though in arms together banded, could break Ser-
via’s retreat!
For the spirit of Kralevich and Tsar Lazar is not
dead,
And Kosovo’s plain still reddens where the Sultan
Murad bled ;
And the love of land and freedom burns with all its
ancient heat
In the brave and dauntless remnant of Servia in
retreat!
TT.S.W.
E. N. MAcCULLOCH.

A HINT TO THE
SISTER
WONDERING WHAT BEST TO SEND
TO HER BROTHER AT THE FRONT.
To the thousands of sisters who have
brothers at the Front, and whose thoughts
are constantly with them, and what can
best be sent them to make their lot easier or
safeguard their health, it may not be amiss
to suggest sending a bottle of Dr. Morse’s
Indian Root Rills.
A bottle of pills may seem an amusing
or unusual thing to suggest, but, seriously,
could anything of equal size or cost be sent
that will be so necessary or useful? It is a
well-known fact that the men require such a
remedy. The irregular life causes the system
to get out of order, and it is this getting out
of order that is responsible for such an
enormous loss from sickness. The shot and
shell of the enemy are only part of the
dangers the soldiers face. To be an effec-
tive soldier, good health is far and away
the most important single item.
Dr. Morse’s Indian Root Pills are too well
known to the public to waste space telling
what they will do. The little amber bottle
in which they are packed will secure them
from all kinds of weather conditions, and
they can be carried by the men on their
persons at all times without inconvenience.
Ten thousand bottles of Dr. Morse’s
Indian Root Pills were offered and accepted
by the military authorities during the early
months of the war. From letters received
from Egypt and other advices the gift of
these pills was highly appreciated, because
they met the everyday requirement and kept
them in perfect condition. See that a bottle
of Dr. Morse’s Indian Root Pills is in-
cluded in any parcel being sent to a brother
at the Front. — Advt.

NOTIONS
Sydney Royal Agricultural Society pur-
chased some expensive information last week when it
learned that it hasn’t a censorship over the turn-
stiles. Some two years ago a citizen had a dust-up
with the officials over a question of pony exhibits.
Ihe Council wiped him and his ponies off its books
and warned the argufying party off the 11. A.
Ground. Eighteen months later he sought admission,
and was forcibly refused. Thereupon he sued the
Council for £lOOO damages. A jury awarded him
one farthing, and the Judge invited each side to pay
its own costs. It was explained that a society or a
club has not the right to refuse admission to its
grounds for ever to any person, provided that he con-
ducts himself respectably. The general belief is
that anyone solemnly “warned off the turf” by the
A.J.C. or the V.R.C. is virtually outlawed and ex-
communicated, and trespasses on any registered race-
course at his peril, but according to the latest
Verdict this view is an unwarranted assumption of
authority, the which is loaded with possibilities.

Fritz Holland and “Red” Watson, he of
the thick neck and iron jaw, provided Saturday’s
disturbance at Sydney Stadium, and went the whole
distance. The methods of these men are as dis-
similar as their physical appearance. Holland,
little and limber, is a boxer of rare skill; Watson,
chunky and slow-moving, has no boxing skill, but
carries a fearsome smite and the pugnacity of a
When Holland wins he gets there by
science; AVatson arrives with a sudden welt on the
jaw.
This time he couldn’t deliver the welt. Instead he
took many welts himself; but all the while he
fought gamely back for an opening and threw hay-
makers about with untiring energy. AVat.son’s
capacity for assimilating punishment is extraor-
dinary. Time after time. Holland bashed him on
the side of the jaw with a measured right, but
without causing the recipient of the polite attention
any more discomfort than could be shaken off by a
toss of the head. Once Holland got him to the
ropes and punished him with at least a dozen wal-
lops in succession, left and right straight to the
face. AVatson took the bombardment without flinch-
ing, and finally jumped forward like a wounded bull-
dog and gave Holland a rousing half-minute. At
the end of the journey there was only one in it
so far as scoring was concerned, for Holland had
held the upper hand all through.
Incidentally, the dust-up caused many of the
crowd to wonder how often a man may hit low
Without being turned down. On three occasions
at least AA’atson got far below the mark. His
trick of holding the top-rope with one hand and
aiming a back-handed swinger with the other also
looked mighty close to the edge of things. In fact,
AA r atson is full of tricks and surprises. He has to
be to get through. But he isT also one of the
most determined scrappers seen in a Svdney ring.

Tommy Uren bored into Fred Kay at
Melbourne Stadium on Saturday night, with energy
and hard bones. As fast as the fleet-footed Kay
sprinted St. Thomas followed, dealing out the most
un-Christian punishment. AVhenever Kay got a chance
at long-range fighting his left shot into Uren’s larder
with a view to repeating the recent Kay-Dyer per-
formance; but the young man from Sydney had alto-
gether different ideas, and, closing in at every oppor-
tunity, jerked upper-cuts to Kay’s head that "dropped
him in the third and blooded him every round from
the seventh to the end. Kay. was so hopelessly in
the negative at the twelfth encounter that the
referee declared peace, whereupon Uren took posses-
sion of the welterweight title.
* * * *
“Montalex”: I see that the Avould-be re-
vivers of the ancient Irish wolf-dog breed have at
last turned out a 36-incher. Doesn’t sound much,
but just mark it on a wall, and you’ll see what it
means. Even a 30-inch dog is a giant. Of course,
the new wolf-hound is a compound, the basis being
a cross of deerhound and ulmar (Great Dane) and
the difficulty of, fixing the type will, as usual, lie
in the ever-present tendency to revert to one or other
of the constituent breeds. That was the trouble
with the earlier bull-terrier. AVhen he wasn’t a
shambling, apple-headed monstrosity, he was gener-
ally almost (indistinguishable from either the bull in
the one direction or the white English terrier in the
other. But careful and long-continued selection and
infusion have absolutely fixed the type as we know
it, combining the bulldog’s breadth of skull and
power of grip with the long, “punishing” jaw and
immense rapidity of action that make the well-bred
bull-terrier such a formidable antagonist.

The south-easter on Saturday last favored
the limit boats in the - Svdney ■ Flying Squadron’s
Handicap, and they cut up the money. Four
hundred pounds has now been divided by the 1 two
sailing clubs, and the whole fleet has participated.
Tom Tait, the outside-marker of the lot, was the
only boat unable to earn a cent up to Saturday, but
when he managed to flop his catamaran over the
line into sixth place he got his finger into the pie
and lifted £2. Scot leads the field with £5O.
There is to be no Henley-on-the-Yarra
this season only a carnival on the lines - of a
Henley regatta, with canoe-racing and life-saving
demonstrations as special attractions. The affair
will be head-quartered in the Henley Reserve, as
usual, and the only difference to the naked eye will
be that most of the competing parsmen will be
married men with large family-responsibilities or
elderly bachelors with bald heads and sideboards, all
the other rowing men by that time—February next
—having gone off to the war. David Hennessy, now
beg inning l to be the Bored Mayor, to judge from his
appearance, is the prime mover so far; but the
remnants of the various rowing-clubs are also taking
the matter in hand in a serious manner, the resulting
profits being designed to build huts for social pur-
poses at Mudros, Lemnos and elsewhere in the Aegean
Sea and thereabouts. By the way, it will be in-
teresting to see what happens this year to the
annual-free-luncheon privilege of the Henley life-
members—a nice point to occupy the attention of
the committee during a couple of warm merry
hours.
‘•T.B.S.”: A word about the Manly surfer,
George Robey, and how he gained his D.C.M. (B.
20/8/ lfi). The late Lt. Addy and 1 were working
ammunition and water at the spot on the beach
where Robey was engaged. It was Lt. Addy who
recommended Robey for his fine behavior on that
memorable day, and 1 had the pleasure of signing
the document as a witness to the facts. When we
first landed Robey was instrumental in throwing up
an embankment of sand, etc., as a cover for wounded
who were coming in from the left flank. Mean-
time we heard that our second in command had been
wounded. Robey immediately left the beach, climbed
the hills, eventually found the Major and brought
nun to a place of safety. When he returned a boat-
load of men was just scrambling* through the water
on to the beach, and it was noticed that one man
remained in the boat sitting bolt upright. He hail
been shot through the forehead just above the eye-
brows. The Turks could easily see him, and directed
their fire at him. Robey immediately went to his
assistance, and was able to push him from the seat
on to the bottom of the boat, where he was fairly
safe. The boat was only a few feet from the shore
nimly embedded in the sand and rock, and a dead
mark During all this time the beach was con-
stantly under a cross-fire —shrapnel from Gabe Tepe
tort and machine and rifle fire from the foot-hills
on the left—but it didn’t prevent Robey battling
hard to save a machine gun which had fallen into
the sea from a boat. I’m writing this propped up
to allow sporting people and others to learn what
this gallant son of Australia really did for his
honorable distinction.

Wounded at Gallipoli, Private Austin
Smith, of the 12th Light Horse. Born at Box Hill,
near Windsor (N.S.W.), he when quite young earned
the reputation of being one of tin l most finished
amateur cross-country riders in the State. Possessed
ot a cool head and a fine pair of hands, he steered
many animals for that good sport., G. A. Terry of
Box Hill. In 1906 lie won the Hunt Club Steeple-
chase at Eastwood, where the late Edward Terry
reigned supreme, and landed his mount a winner in
a similar race at Moorefield when pitted against pro-
fessionals.

6-furlongs’ : W. A. Long, M.L.C., just
deceased, was owner of Grand Flaneur, whose name
is handed down by old ’uns to more supple genera-
tions as the unbeaten and unbeatable champion of
the Australian turf. Jt was Long’s pet theory that
the present-day racehorse is miles behind the prads
of earlier days, and that Grand Flaneur was in a
class by himself. Yet this champion only won £BOOO
m stakes, which is explained partly by the fact that
in his day stakes were small, and partly by the other
tact that he started only nine times. Of the manv great
horses since his day the names of Carbine, Trafalgar
and Poseidon occur as instances of nags with records
equal to, if not better than, Grand Flaneur. True, they
were all beaten in turn, but so would Long’s famous
horse have been had he contested as often as they did.
“Merrigal”: The just-dead “Bill” Long
never practised his profession of barrister, though in
tie early legal days of his friend Ted Lee, of
Bathurst, he occasionally accompanied him on c-ir-
cult for companionship only. He was a wealthy
man in those times, but speculations and helping
mends left him comparatively poor at the finish.
Mamre ’ Smith, once owner of Eurimbla station,
Molong, sent for him a few days before his death
and expressed a desire to leave him everything; hut
Bill remarked that he was sufficiently gilded, and
suggested Billy Sparke instead, who, having no
objection, duly received the gift, and remembered
Long in his turn. The old man, who never married,
was a brother-in-law of the silver-tongued Dailey
and that keen lawyer “Jimmy” Martin, once Chief
Justice.
* * * *
The late It. Barr Smith was a great judge
of a horse or a dog. His brother-in-law, Thomas
Elder, founded the long-famous Morphettyille stud,
between Adelaide and the sea, and the family kept
it going till recent years. Mr. Smith was said never
to have backed a horse except his own, or to have
had more than a pound on. II is colors, yellow,
black cap, have not been seen for a long time. He
won the Adelaide Cup with Banter in 1879, and in
the ’9o's Mostyn twice won the Goodwood for him
aud the City Handicap once. On the last-named
occasion (1890), the “double” was in the family,
his son, Tom Barr Smith, winning the Birthday Clip
with Destiny.

“Rag”: It was, of course, only to be ex-
pected that I key Mo would discover a means to
pioceed with his profitable business in Svdney, despite
the comprehensive definitions of what is a place
within the meaning of the Gaming Act. He now
has motor-ears running to time-table in several
suburbs on certain days. If you happened to he
within eyeshot of such a locality, say, five minutes
before one of these cars was due there, you would
see a knot of from two to 20 rmnters impatiently
awaiting the arrival of Ike. When he did arrive
you would notice that, instead of stopping, the car
moved dead slowly alongside the kerb and tiiat the
men who had been waiting approached it in turn,
inquired as to the price of such and such a nag in
some race or other, handed some boodle to the per-
sons in the car, and received a betting voucher in
return. It is obvious that hundreds of stations can
he called at during a day, and a big and regular
clientele is being gradually got into the net. If the
ear is moving, no matter how slowly, the rookies
reckon that it cannot be “a place.”

“Y.Z.”: Mossy Isaacs used to tell this
little bit of autobiography. Mossy was the one
eminent bookmaker of Australia about whose nation-
ality there was no possible, probable shadow of
doubt. His nose stands for the sign nasal of all
Israel. “I vos on a pizneth call at the houze of a
cliend of mein,” said Mossy, “und presendlv a
leedle girl come in, and haf a goot, long look at
me, und then she say: ‘Farder, farder, does Mister
Isaacs know he’s just like a Jew?’ ”

The once great Sandow was very annoyed
when the last mail left England. Some babbler
who didn t know his facts had set afloat a yarn that
the strong man had been shot in the Tower or some-
where else handy as a German spy, and that Mrs.
Sandow had been sentenced to a term of imprison-
ment on a similar charge. Of course there was
nothing m the yarn, Sandow being a good
Russian, born of Russian parents, although beyond
the frontiers of the Bear. He was looking for
his detractors with a hammerlock in one hand and
a compendium of jiu-jitsu in the other at latest
information. His team mate was an uncompromis-
ing lawyer with a barrowload of blue paper.

Maoriland’s footballers are rapidly thin-
ning out on their way to the Great Scrum. Amongst
the latest to go are Glenn, of Wanganui, who was a
member of the great All-Black team that set
Britain agape in 1905. He’s in France with the
Artillery. Another great player, Pat Harris, who
was one of the best of the hard-doers that made
things merry in Australia in 1897, is off with a
Southern M.L. bunch. Young Gilray, Maoriland
i epresentative and Scottish international, is also on
the wing. He is booked for England to join the
army there. Too many Australians and Maoiilanders
doing that. Whaffor ?

“Nyren”: While on the subject of groat
batsmen a word for .John Small, the elder, who
died in liis 90th year (b. April 19, 1737 ; d. r)ec. 31,
1826). He was the greatest batsman in his day,
and played in first-class matches until over 70 years
of age. Fifty years of first-class cricket! Tie was a
shoemaker, and over his.door he hung this sign:
John Small make bat and ball,
Pitch a wicket, play at cricket
With any man in England.
It was through him that the two-stick wicket gave
place to the three-stick. In a match beween five of
Hambledon and five of England one “Lumpy”
Stevens bowled the ball three times between Small’s
uprights without removing the bail. This seemed
100 tough, and the third stump came to light.
Another Noah of the pitch was “Silver Billy” Bel cl ham,
who played first-class cricket for over 40 years, and
was “the greatest cricketer of all time.” lie
married twice, and was the father of 39 children,
tlie majority of whom died young. Beldham lived
to-be 98. Tom Walker was an A. C. Bannerman,
but, unlike the latter, very slow on his legs.
“F. 8.” : How’s this for a coincidence? In
a cricket match played at Stafford (Eng.), in 1881,
between the First XI of King Edward Vl.’s Grammar
School and a team from (lie nearby township of
Galley, a master named Garlick played for the
school, while two of the Gailey players were named
Onions and Leek respectively. Despite the odds
against the combination, the score-book read: “Gar-
lick, c. Onions, b. Leek, 31.”

“Pick”: The mechanism of the auto-
pistol, like that of the automatic sporting gun and
magazine rifle, is necessarily of such accuracy that,
kept clean arid lightly oiled with a suitable non-
clog lubricant, if the weapon throws one shell it will
throw all that fit neatly. This, however, is where
the weapon frequently fails. The extent to which
cartridges of a given calibre differ is a marvel, and
so well is this known among riflemen that a cart-
ridge-tester is found in every shooter’s kit. Either
loose-fitting or tight ammunition may result in
jamming. Otherwise, for rapidity, accuracy,' dis-
tance and velocity the auto pistol has outed the
revolver just as tlie present-day rifle has silenced the
old muzzle-loading Enfield.
“Sardonyx” : Reference was recently made
to Jem Mace’s historic fights with Bob Brettle, in
the first of which Mace suffered the only defeat in his
long career. 1 knew the “Old-Un” well when he
and Jack Thompson kept the V.R.C. Hotel in Mel-
bourne, 40 years ago, and I remember asking him
about this very matter. Jem grinned at the recol-
lection. “Ay, he got away wi’ me right enough,
the first time, because 1 niver got prawperty goin’.
But the foony part of it was that he said I had
no poonch. He i'oond out all about that the second
time!” This in allusion to the terrific hit with
which Mace literally drove Brettle’s nose into his
face, smashing the nasal bones and nearly destroying
the sight of both eyes. Well might Mace say—
I’ve heard him often —that a “pro ’’ who let-go full
force on a novice should be charged with using a
deadly weapon.

“Jackar”: Here is a IV. G. Grace fact
which, as far as I know, has not been recalled.
“W;G.” was a desperately bad “sport,” and could
be very objectionable over a losing point. In a
match at Bendigo, Oscroft skied one of Glen’s under-
hand slows to leg. Giant Midwinter was fielding
there and, promptly recognising that if he stood
upright inside the chain fence he could not reach the
ball, he twined one of his legs on a post, and swing-
ing backwards made a most wonderful catch.
Oscroft started to walk towards the pavilion, but
Grace rushed out and ordered him back, loudlv
deolaring that it was no catch, :ts both ball and
fielder were out of bounds when the ball lodged in
the fielder’s hands. Controversy on the point waxed
fast and furious for over a quarter of an hour, when
both umpires gave the verdict “Out” on the argu-
ment that as “Mid” had one foot on the ground
inside the chains and the other 4feas dangling inside
of it, it could not be correctly said that the fielder
was out of bounds. The decision displeased Grace
very much, and he sulked over it to the ill-mannered
extent of refusing to have luncheon with the com-
mittee.

s
Not looking for trouble, but to possess that quiet confidence to
look after yourself when trouble does come your way.
You admire the pluck of the man who takes a beating gamely
from an experienced Boxer. But you don’t admire his awkwardness,
or the way he leaves himself open, or how he punches the air;
and wears a blackened eye that advertises his downfall for days
afterwards.
I Teach Boxing by Post
in such a manner that you will rapidly develop your natural
ability.
The Instruction is simple and explicit—based on
my own personal experience and connection with the
world’s champions—both amateur and professional.
HUNDREDS of pupils throughout Australasia and
N.Z. are learning to be “shags” through means of
my postal course. Here is evidence: —
“I had a spar with a big chap last week,
and m.v hand seemed to work like a machine :
ami he said after that he could not keep my
left out of his face.”
There are no loopholes for any misunderstanding.
The lessons have been so carefully prepared that
anyone could understand them.

‘I simply can’t account for this
trouble with my teeth—and I’ve
been so particular to keep them
immaculately clean always.” This
is the complaint of thousands.
It’s just this way, Madam,
louve spent your time watching
the highwaymen robbers of tooth-
health—Decay Germs—and for-
got all about their sly, insidious
accomplice, ‘‘Acid Mouth.” Acids
are constantly forming in tlm
mouth. They are constantly
“working.” But they work
“incognito.”
And here lies the danger.
These mouth-acids, unmolested,
unsuspected, gradually dissolve,
and break down the enamel. The
tooth then becomes easy prey to
decay germs.
Look out for “acid mouth,”
that filcher and falcon of healthy
tooth life. Pebeco Tooth Paste
users have a good chance to keep
their teeth for life.

“Jobber'’: Blew into the Kursaal, Cairo’s
music-hall, last week, to renew an aeqiialnt-
anee with Leed and Lemar, billed as "Ex-
eentrik Australians,” who put on an excel-
lent turn. Each artist at the show is
announced by the card system, as at Sydney
i’iv., and finally there is one on which is
printed “Bonsoir.” Said I to mv pal, “I
wonder if this pot is an Australian?” Quoth
Bill: “Sounds more like a flamin’ Dago to
me.”
Hugh McCrae, after some personal ex-
perience :
I flu nYon 0. s note on the Melbourne Repertory
(lb 18/11/‘ir>) interests me. The fact that the
players provide “the most convincing performances
to be seen locally” is no doubt due in part to the
“excellent easting" of the play by Grogan McMahon,
lint it is due to something 1 more. What appears to
“Harrison O.” to be merely an “engaging 1 artless-
ness” in “the supporting company” is, as a. matter
of fact, the result of McMahon's remarkable ability
in training the limited raw material at his dis-
posal. The excellent performances of the “sunport-
ing company” are attained simply because each and
every member of it is not only well chosen by
McMahon, but is also drilled and rehearsed and
bullied and bashed and implored and execrated by
the gentle Grogan morning 1 , noon and niglit, collec-
tively and individually, until the amateurs have
acquired, not “artlcssncss,” but the art that con-
ceals art.
Harrison O. criticises adversely- McMahon's own
personal performances. 1 happen to have inside
knowledge of McMahon's work, and, to me, it is a.
marvel how he manages to appear on his stage at
all, let alone give the many fine performances to
his credit. After eight or nine weeks or so assidu-
ously spent in perfecting- the “artlessness” of his
amateurs, and after (on the day of production, mind
you) directing and helping in all the scene-shift-
ing, carpet-laying, lighting, advertising 1 , nail-drivirg
and God-knows-what-ehe, and finally “making-up”
and dressing each and every member of the cast,
and giving them a final drink to 1 ensure further
“artlessness,” Grtgan pulls up his curtain and
totters on to his stage the most dog-weary and ill-
prepared actor in his company. Of personal re-
hearsal in his parts he gets none, as it is properly
understood. How he manages to find time even to
memorise his parts, let alone do them justice, is
more than this writer can divine.
If “Harrison O.” had a more intimate knowledge
of the process of acquiring the Repertory actor’s
“artlessness” and of the difficulties of McMahon’s
position, lie might not have written his thoughtless
criticism of one who not only is perhaps the most
Pulsatile and intellectual actor Australia has turned
out. hut is also one who, in his Repertory work in
Melbourne, has laid the community under a deep
obligation. After witnessing his performances of
■John Tanner, in “Man and Superman.” Ferrand in
Ihe Pigeon, l Iric Brendel in “Rosmershohn,”
Carve in “Tlie Great Adventure,” Sir Charles
Worgan in “What the Public Wants,” and Mr.
Burgess in ‘.‘Candida,” 1 confess to knowing no
actor wnose work is more natural and convincing.
“Bab ': This trouble about the alleged
11ufinish methods of the Sydney Wilhelm
Hop in controlling the crowd of Allies Day
seems to me to be due to J.G.W., Ltd. For
last year the Firm Ims been importing
U.S. crook dramas, in each of which there is
a brutal and bullying police inspector. I-lis
name is usually Lincoln Plumer. One or
all of three things has happened. (1) The
Sydney public has got so accustomed to this
idea of the police that it has come to regard
Johannes Hop as a collective Lincoln
Plumer. (2) Mr. Black, surfeited with im-
ported Lincoln Plumerisms, naturally re-
gards the local Lincoln Plumers as angels.
(•>) The local John Hop, seeing the admira-
tion that audiences display for the Lincoln
Iluiner methods, has decided to take him as a
model and is trying to live up to his stand-
ard. The obvious remedy is to deport Lin-
coln Plumer and put an embargo on the im-
port of L.S. double-cross melodrama. Any-
how, Lincoln Plumer’s habit of announcing
the arrival of- twins is getting on Australia’s
nerves.
“Melb.’ : Yesterday I went over the site
of tlio old Cremorne Gardens, Richmond, by
the Yarra. A little township has grown
there, with cottages and streets, but no patch
of garden. A blasted sort of place. Rip
\ an Winkle showed me where the lake had
been, with its panorama of the Siege of
Sebastopol, and a nightly din of bombs and
fireworks. In the midst of this Garden of
Eden was the Pagoda, with a dancing plat-
form. It resembled a gigantic bridecake.
In the eastern part of the gardens stood the
I antheon Theatre; in the west was the
circus, including a menagerie. What won-
ders the elephant and camel were! By the
lake, westward, was the bar. where Spiers
and Pond began. The Cremorne Christmas
pantomime one year was “Jack the Giant
Killer.” The little girls were known as
but became so well known outside
that Coppin made a transformation scene,
and the fairies all grew up in one night
from an average of perhaps 14 years to 30.
MY GLOSSARY.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The play contains a great deal of American slang
....but a glossary of terms is provided in the
programme. —Sydney show notice.
The Yankee plays I see, dear heart,
I love for their profundity ;
I study when I sit apart
My glossary, my glossary.
The “neutral” language who derides —-
Its slang, its twang, its fluency ?
The thoughtful management provides
My glossary, my glossary.
I know not what the actors say,
Their talk is Double Dutch to me;
I solve the riddle, though, next day—
My glossary, my glossary !
For “English” pure and undefiled
Some people yearn. Rank pedantry !
Bless those, however, who compiled
My glossary, my glossary 1
And thus these Yankee plays, dear heart,
Offer rs education free 1
1 study when 1 sit apart
My glossary, niv glossary.

THE same German firm has hitherto supplied thousands of
German pianos to Australians during many yeais.
Yet German pianos and player-pianos are still actively
advertised m Australia by the Anglo-German Agents, so that once
again your enemy will regain his footing m Austialia, once moie to
establish his spy system, and to use Australian money to piepaie
his ultimate revenge for his present disappointment !
If you are thinking of buying a piano, will you come and inspect
for yourself the Beale Pianos —made in Australia, yom own countiy,
by your own countrymen ?
If you buy a Beale Piano you
will be actively assisting in provid-
ing a livelihood for nearly 500
Australian families, many of whom
have sent sons, brothers and fathers
to fight the Germans and their
Allies.
Just come and see the Beale
Pianos you will be delighted with
their qualities in every respect.
Mr. Octavius C. Beale,
founder and present con=
trolling Director of this busi=
ness, was born at Mount
Mellick, Queen’s County.
Ireland, of an old Irish
family. The Directors and
Managers throughout the
business are all descendants
of British families for many
generations. There are no
Germans employed in the
business,and no supplies are
obtained from Germany.

taken of communications insufficiently stamped.

liability is undertaken re contributions volun-
tarily submitted; and no MS. will be returned
unless stamps accompany.

CORRESPONDENTS.

wooden.... Marian J.: The knock-off whistle is blow-
ing. ... Charles H. M.: No good. Have another
brain-spasm... .J. 8.: A very gentlemanly lion, but
his paws are out of drawing. .. .H.W.: Why a group
of dwarfs?. .. .Grant D.: You lack skill and the gag
is a hybrid. .. .K.A.S.: It’s the same old delusion
that any blazing amateur can write or draw without
training, but only the specialist can mend a gaspipc.

bad. . . .W.E.J.: Too dull and obvious and faint....
J.L. (Tas.) : Same complaint exactly....?.: “Effec-

“Yarriambiak”: In his classification of
occupations for military and industrial pur-
poses statistician Knibbs has placed “Mem-
ber of Legislative Assembly” and “Member
of Parliament” in class 71, but “buzzer” is
in class 22. This seems to be a duplication.
Also Knibbs appears to wilfully seek queer
company for himself, for “Government
Statist” is in class 71, which also includes
such doubtful characters as justices of the
peace, arcade beadles and State Governors.
Judges and magistrates and inmates of penal
establishments are likewise included in “71,”
as well as magicians, clairvoyantes and
numerous other humbugs. But it looks
over the odds to place a useful person like
an ale-bottler next door to an alderman.
Inoffensive bassoon playerk are placed
cheek-by-jowl with Chinese bankers, and
barmaids are not far away. Bone gatherers
are forced into the company of bookmakers.
Shrimp sellers and jockeys, horse-trainers
and jobbers, sheep’s-trotter dealers,
choristers, colporteurs, niglitmen, organ
blowers, ostrich farmers, palaeontologists,
kapoc teasers, hucksters and Greek profes-
sors, refractionists and wheel-chair drawers,
catgut makers and brewery emptiers (this
must be a large group), fat collectors, bone
polishers, masters-in-equity, manicurists, pot-
boys and medal dealers (strange how the
Australian Patriots’ League bobs up at
every turn), manure makers and rent col-
lectors are all in this splendidly diversified
group. And as if there were not enough
every blessed importer from A to Z in the
whole .index is (brown, in with the rest.
CITY VERSES AND NARRATIVES ETC.
The Dog Carts Visit the Queen vie Market.
Excitement prevails in the market place
When dog cart goes around,
O er the Chinamen’s cabbages and peas
In their exit mongrels bound;
Noisily yelp the captured one’s
And potatoes are hurled about
Recklessly thrown by roughish men
To scareth the seeked for Out;
Eagerlv on the trail of dog’s
Two grim men went their way
Hardened to protests and jeer’s
Of the people on market Day.

Do good, quick, clean Ironing.
You can iron in any room in the house—or on the porch—with a G-E
Electric Iron. Attach it to any light socket, turn the switch and the G-E
Iron is ready in a moment.
Aside from these conveniences, think of the time, money and steps a
G-E Iron saves. Think of the lessened wear and tear on the family linen.
With a G=E Electric Iron you get “ Even Heat.”
EVENHEAT"
No Spot too Hot
No spot too hot; it is no hotter at the point than at the
heel—at the edges than in the centre. Ibis, safeguards
against scorches—makes the ironing go quicker an
easier; saves time, which means saving electneitj ,
fact
An Average Family Ironing costs
only Threepence for Electricity.
Specially-arranged air chambers in the G-E Iron hold
the heat down directly to the ironing surface. Using
the heat this way also permits you to do consuleiable
ironing with the electricity turned off.
The G-E trademark on this iron stands for quality—it assures you a
lifetime of satisfactory service.
Banish hot=weather Discomfort.
Turn the switch and command the faintest zephyr, or a sweeping
breeze—as you please—and it shall be yours—m home office, bedroom,
ballroom or' workshop. A simple device places the speed entirely within
your control.
Twenty vears of laboratory study and factory application are concen-
trated in the smooth-running, noiseless mechanism of the G-E Electric nan.
Its Oscillating Motion,
silently stirring the air—like the breeze flow of nature—is only one of the
many practical features of this fan.
Its sturdiness of construction, the perfect integrity of its materials and
parts, its delicacy of balance throughout, which prevents vibration and weai,
all assure you a lifetime of satisfactory service.
As to Economy, sufficient to say that the G-E Fan, depending on the
size, can be run
Seven or Eight Hours for One Penny
—less than the cost of burning a single ordinary incandescent light.

Look to Your Teeth.
There are no teeth like those furnished by
Nature. For service, comfort, and good
health, artificial teeth cannot be com-
pared with a natural set.
Sensible people will accordingly try to pre-
serve their teeth as long as possible by
keeping them clean. Cold water and soap
and water are useful, but are somewhat ;
ineffectual. Rough powders, such as cuttle-
fish, crabseyes, crude chalks and charcoal
are too severe on .-tije enamel.
EUDENTA TOOTH PASTE is scientifi-
cally prepared to clean the teeth, sweeten
the breath, harden the gums and disinfect
the mouth (thus preventing decay).
EUDENTA is therefore, regarded as the
perfection of dental luxuries.'’ 1/- tube every-
where. Post free 1/3. Free sample posted.

IN BANANA LAND
It took five policemen all their time to
keep Brisbane’s old ladies from rushing the
State butchery to death the first week it
opened. Well, something of the kind was
wanted to counteract the bad effect of the
State’s infliction on the public of the dear-
est and worst bread in Australia for three
months past.
Ivobbi : Is it any wonder the cactus
curse cant be killed? At Lake’s Creek the
other day a gang in digging out some of it
found one root 36ft. long. It had a whole
host of feeding roots attached to it, and its
corporation where it was most like a Legis-
Councillor was one inch in diameter.
I 11 blow out a match on this for anybody
who doubts the measurements.
Roma-street markets saw a novel strike
the other day. A North Coast farmer, in
compliment to an agent who had secured
top prices for his potatoes, sent him a box
of cigars, which the agent distributed among
his buyers. But when the next line came
along for sale the buyers declined- to bid
until fresh cigars were sent round. The
strike ended, of course, in smoke.
The Bananaland Government, which is
sending round a lecturess to preach thrift
and economy to the common people,
announces that it intends to practise these
virtues by spending £2,707,000 of loan
money this year. At the end of November
the Treasurer was £264,000 worse off than
at the same date last year, a deterioration
entirely due to a rise of £325.000 in expen-
diture Thrift! Rcpnomy 5
A member of the 0.0.5. goes ovey an old
explanation once more:—
1 notice in B. 18/11/’l5 a paragraph attaching
the administration of the Queensland Charity Oygani-
sgtion Society, the apparent ground being the great
disproportion between the amount expended in
salaries and commissions and that devoted to the
actual relief of distress. The Charity Organisation
Society is not, and at no time lias ever pretended to
be, an association for the distribution of charitable
relief. Whatever work it may do in this direction
is of an incidental nature only; its real functions,
set out and explained from year to year, arc those
of an investigating and advisory Board. It is, as
may be imagined, the uncompromising enemy of pro-
fessional mendicancy, but at the same time it has
proved itself, over and over again, a real friend to
tho'a in genuine difficulty or distress. The few
hundred pounds which appear in the balance-sheet
represent a very small proportion of the amount dis-
bui-cd in and around Brisbane each year, either
through the agency or on the advice of the C. 0.5.,
and the value of its services to the community at
larg-' may he judged by the estimation in which
it is held not only by those directly connected with
charitable work, but among business and profes-
sional men. Lastly, the society acts as a connecting
link between the different benevolent institutions of
the city. AVith one or two exceptions, all these
ins!itutions have representatives upon its committee,
which meets at regular intervals to discuss not onlv
the direct operations of the society itself, but any
malters of general interest. I can assure the public
that the officials earn every penny of the salaries
they receive. , : .- v

St. George, St. Andrew and St. David are
again at loggerheads with St. Patrick on
account of the ould Irish gintleman having
induced the Government to make his day a
statutory holiday while neglecting the other
fellows’, Treasurer Theodore alleges he did
it because 100,000 people petitioned him to
so lionoi* St. Patrick, and says lie would do
tlie same for the other saints if their
votaries pushed them into equal popularity.

Queensland's latest Loan Estimates notify
that it is proposed to spend £7OOO on a
Mental Diseases’ Ward at the Brisbane Hos-
pital, while other , portions of Cohen’s cash
aio to he spent on extending and improving
other asylums. All permanent works, of
course, and all reproductive !
‘Barcoo’ : “Weemurra” in a recent Bul-
letin got tangled concerning £470 put aside
for water-diviners. He evidently imagines
this is to be expended on human water-supply
discoverers*,, but he will find the money is
for the 1 maintenance and manipulation of two
mechanical contraptions introduced and im-
ported by the late Government. On first-
hand information they are as inaccurate as
their two-legged confreres.
Labor unions are doing their best to show
that Labor Governments are a mistake.
After of comparative peace, since last
election there has been a long series of
strikes in rapid succession. Some day there
will be a big one, and the police who are
now organised into a union will have the
opportunity of trying which is the stronger
—loyalty to the country or loyalty to union-
ism.
Governor Goold-Adams : “W<? have got to
put our backs to the wall and go forward.”
Yes. and with our flag nailed to the mast
we will also put our blind eye to the tele-
phone. and, putting down our foot, cry, “Up
Guards, and at ’em!”
“liangan”: The majority of Australian
politicians are suffering from shock at the
action of Carmichael, M.L.A. (N.S.W.), in
declining a fat job to go to the Front. Of
the 64") politicians necessary to run this
country only nine have taken on the bigger
job. The other (>:>(> remain behind and All
in their time urging others to go. In Bris-
bane the other day a youthful politician was
breathlessly exhorting all fit men to obey the
call when a rude man in khaki abruptly in-
quired why he didn't go. “My man ”
solemnly said the astounded tongue-fighter,
' / am doing far more important work in
this city.” Another, similarly placed, ex-
plained that he had conscientious reasons
Yet, men with hide like that would he dead-
safe at the Front!
. The Ryan Government lias now brought
a Gas Bill. Hitherto most of the cos
have charged ,6s. Bd. per 1000 ft. It is now
proposed to check their hilarity bv fivino-
the prices at 4s. 6d. in South Brisbane and
4s. in Brisbane, with a referee to assess
rales in all other centres. The Bill looks
crude, and far below the standard of either
I'-nglish or N. S. Wales legislation on the
subject.
“Fitzroy” : Rockhampton earns the biscuit
tor cussedness. Some years ago it wanted a
port badly. It made such a row that the
then Government, at a huge cost, gave it
Broadmount. But it didn’t want Broad-
mount ; it wanted Port Alma. Another
generous Government gave it Port Alma.
I hen it didn’t want Port Alma; it wanted
the river deepened. As nobody would listen
to this it started out for something else. It
round it wanted a railway into the Alton
Downs farming district. It wanted this rail-
way more than it had ever wanted anvthing.
Ike Ryan Government, not knowing' Rock-
hampton, decided to oblige, and some weeks
age the work was started. Before the line
had pushed its nose clear of the city
Rockhampton discovered a new route for it,
and Minister Adamson stopped the work to
listen. Meanwhile the local shire council
took a vote and ascertained that the railway
wasn t wanted at all. Then Adamson got
annoyed and cancelled the job. But he
wasn’t half as annoyed as the big timber
company that had bought land and started
to put up a mill on the proposed route. Rock-
hampton is now looking round to see what .it
wants next. _ It thinks it will be a water
supply. As it has half a dozen schemes in
mind it expects to thoroughly enjoy itself.
The Warships of William.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Oh, where are the joys of the Past
(The poet sang once in his lay),
I guess they’re afloat in the vast
Where man never anchor could weigh ;
But, friend, we’ve a problem to-day
More baffling and puzzlesome still—
In mercy enlighten us, pray:
Oh, where are the warships of Bill ?
No pennon or funnel or mast
Peeps up with intent to betray;
No thunders arise on the blast,
Or die on the verge of the bay ;
We’d heard of sea-panthers in grey
Who’d swoop like a hawk on “the kill,”
But who can locate their array?
Oh, where are the warships of Bill?
Von Tirpitz, O chief of the cast,
Now where are the craft of thy sway?
Art thou but a phantom at last
To frighten our babes at their play?
Fat Neptune who dodges the spray,
Who prinks of good lager his fill,
When— when does he mix in the fray?
Oh, where are the warships of Bill?
Envoi.
Your submarine diving is gay.
And gives our old ladies a thrill ;
But battle’s our business to-day—
Oh, where are the warships of Bill?
QUEENSLAND.
F. BORN.
QUEENSLAND GOSSIP.
Brisbane, December 4, 1915.
My Dear Tiiargominoaii,—
We’re trying this recruiting scheme of
marching men over long distances at a great
waste of time and hoofs. “The Dungarees”
arrived on Tuesday after walking from
M arwick. Hospitality on the way was per-
haps the main feature of the business. One
of the recruits is a navvy, who was toiling
on the railroad. lie dropped his pick, and
without waiting for his back pay fell into
line.
The cadets of area GB stood to attention
this afternoon, while a photo, of the late
Capt. Moran, of the 15th Battalion, was
unveiled. It will hang in the drill-shed
for the present. Capt. Carter (who was in
action at the same time as Capt. Moran)
and Lt. Luxmore spoke in praise of the
dead soldier, and Capt. Bousfield unveiled
the memorial.
r i he Deputy-P.M.G. unveiled an honor
hoard in the (J.1’.0. on Friday. There are
187 names on it already, and when others
who have recently enlisted are added there
will be over 200.
Some Brisbane girls took fruit, elc.. to
the Military Hospital the other day, and
hearing that there was one chap who had
lost a leg, got him something extra. But
he wasn’t having that. “Pm not an in-
valid,” he said firmly. “We’ll give this to>
the poor blokes who can’t get out of bed”—-
and so proceeded to conduct the party round
the wards, looking for “the poor blokes,’ 1
himself on crutches.
Nurse Clare Mclntosh, of Townsville
was married to-day to Capt. Cecil Collins’
who has been looking after the business of'
the Empire at Rabaul. Capt. Collins goes:
t > Egypt soon. The missus intends to go.
part of the way with him and continue her'
old job of nursing in one of the hospitals.
A fete in aid of the Wounded Soldiers”
and Belgian funds is happening to-day at
Mowbray Park. What on earth will jvv
poor women do when at last peace is de-
clared, and there are no fetes to be>
arranged and no knitting to he done?
Speaking of knitting. If any good Aus-
tralian would manufacture sound vulcanite
knitting-needles in great haste, he would
amass a fortune. At present those we are
able to obtain come from England. They
cost one shilling a pair, and break on no
provocation whatever.
Mrs. W. H. Carvosso gave an At Home
at the I.W.C.A. rooms on Wednesday in
honoi ol Airs. Tom Price, widow of the
late Premier of S. Aus. and one of the
recently-appointed J’s.P. for that State.
The new Technical College buildings are
completed. Very fine looking, they stand
m old Government House grounds and are
bounded on one side by the river and on
the other by the Botanical Gardens. The
equipment is excellent. Particularly good
work is being done by the School of Domestic
Economy, where a class of blind, deaf and
dumb scholars receive instruction in cook-
ing ; also about 200 boys from the A.A.M.C.
have gone through special courses in the ■
art of preparing hospital and trench tucker..
“The _ Cingalee” has drawn good houses
every night this week, whereat the Amateur-
Operatic Society is much elated and local!
charities are enriched. The Governor and!
Lady Goold-Adams adorned the vice-regall
box on Thursday night.
Yours affectionately,

Bananaland’s birth and death figures are
again most disheartening— to Little Austra-
lians. The figures for 1914 show a birtli-
rate of 29.5 per 1000, and a death-rate of
•DL. these compare with the latest avail-
able for other countries in this absurd way :
Births per 1000. Deaths per 1000.

The infantile death-rate of' 03.87 is also one
of the lowest in the world, and altogether it
is about time statistics were abolished if the
white-man-can’t-thrive-in-the-Nortli theory is
to have half a chance.

Gentlemen,
Wo are requested by the Com-
missioners of the St. John Ambulance
Association at Havre and elsewhere
to supply, for use in the Base
Hospitals, 1,000 2/9 bottle 3 of
Phosferine*
They inform us that your remedy
is in constant requisition l?y the
Field Hospitals, and from the reports
they receive, it is held in high
esteem as a Tonic possessing valuable
recuperative qualities.
I shall be obliged by your imme-
diate attention, as it is necessary
to get the Phosferine forwarded to
France as quickly as possible.
(Lady) Helen S. Sloggett.

If any illustration appearing in r
Bulletin takes your fancy, write to tin
Manager, '/ho will quote you pri e for it.
Captain Gerald Loughran, one of the Vic-
torian surgeons who went to Gallipoli in the
early days of the lighting, and has since been
mentioned as a frequent performer in the
V.C. class, is a product of the medical school
at Melbourne Uni. In the early stages of
his studies he forsook the frogs and took a
year or two off to go to the Boer war as a
private. Presently he came back, a cor-
poral, and finished his course. Afterwards
lie went to Murtoa, in the Wimmera district,
where the frozen lambs and the Deutsclier
names come from.
Richard E. Oaten, one of the clerical
representatives on the just-constituted N. S.
Wales Clerks’ Wages Board, is the most
prominent member of the Clerks’ Union —its
president and its delegate to the Labor
Council, lie was secertary of the Federa-
tion of Labor —the body formed to bring
about a sort of amalgamation of all the
unions, but now superseded by the One Big
Union idea. lie makes his crust as an
accountant and secretary of the Athletic
League, for which he also does most of the
handicapping. Lewis 1 lerinan, one of the
relied-upon organisers of the Labor party at
election times, is the Clerks’ Union’s present
secretary, and the other employees’ rep. on
the Board.
Lieut.-Col. “Jack” Hutchinson, in com-
mand of the 21st Battalion of Australian
Infantry which was aboard the Southland
when she was torpedoed, is a younger brother
of “Bill” Hutchinson, the new Minister for
Lands in Peacock’s rehashed Vic. Cabinet.
The Colonel is a partner in the Hutchinson
family store at Warracknabeal. He was
one of a little bunch that started a local
militia at Warrack, and he retained his in-
terest in defence matters until the chance
came to do something with battle-smoke in
it.
Charles Dalton, an affable young man not
very long from England, who followed
Charles Godby as vicar of Malvern when the
older man passed on to spend his declining
years in the deanery garden at East Mel-
bourne, is to fill |he comfortable shoes of the
late Canon Cason as rural dean of Malvern.
Australia offers many opportunities to the
right sort of ambitious student of divinity
and parlor-manners, providing he be (.1)
English, (2) accustomed to deal discreetly
with Liberal dowager-parishioners, (3) will-
ing to accept a good salary.
Adelaide University lately had four
new endowments established at one Senate
meeting. One, given by the veteran Edwin
Smith, is in memory of his grandson, Lieut.
Eric Talbot Smith, killed in Gallipoli. This
is to frank a student through any degree
course at the University, and is in the gift
of the Commercial Travellers and Ware-
housemen’s Association. So is a smaller
one named after their late secretary, Archi-
bald Mackie, and covering the Commerce
course. The other two are both to com-
memorate John L. Young, the schoolmaster
of the last generation who had the training
of C. C. Kingston, Register Finlayson, and
many other afterwards-prominent men.
The numerous Snowball family, of Mel-
bourne, of which Oswald, M.L.A. for Brigh-
ton, is- the best-advertised member to-day, is
now mostly too long in the tooth for service
in Turkey. Still there are some eligibles.
The late Dr. Snowball, the “children’s doc-
tor,” who made the family name respected
throughout the State, left four boys. Three
of them (all the unmarried ones) are under
arms. By the way, Joseph R. Snowball, of
the Licensed Victuallers, a brother of the
late doctor, lately made public a suggestion
for reducing drunkenness in the Australian
army. Briefly, it was that all respectable
publicans should refuse to serve sozzled
soldiers. Joseph R. is a cousin of Oswald,
who proposes to reduce drunkenness among
soldiers by continuing to keep the wet can-
teen out of camp, thereby compelling the men
to get their dipperful at any old pub. Appar-
ently neither Snowball believes that the Aus-
tralian soldier should act as his own publi-
can, though there is a large pile of evidence
in favor of that scheme.
“Moana” : Re stewards still accepting tips
on Maoriland steamers (B. 21/10/’l5), in
spite of the Arbitration Court’s prohibition,
made at their own request. Accepting isn’t
(he word. They are demanding loose change
these days. The other night at Wellington
a party of Expeditionaries going on final
leave before departing to fight for the
hungry stewards, climbed on to the Maori
armed with their tickets, and were promptly
told that they’d have to get off, as the ship
was full. Then someone whispered that for
“two bob a head” he’d find berths some-
where. The men in khaki handed out the
cash and got the beds.
“Eryander” : If the Allies get to Constan-
tinople they’ll only be adding another chap-
ter to its record as the most chronically-
besieged city on the planet. Huns, Arabs,
Persians, Russians, Hungarians, Venetians,
Bulgarians and Turks —all had a try for it by
turns before the Ottomans finally wrested it
from the last Constantine. When the pre-
sent writer was in the ancient and dog-
infested city the population was made up of
about half a million genuine Turks and
650,000 Greeks, Armenians, Syrians, Arabs,
Jews, Italians, French and English. Practi-
cally no Germans; for the Turk, despite the
accursed friendship forced upon him now, has
a holy hatred of the Teuton.
Though it has produced Willie Watt,
.M.H.R., and Peacock of the Chortle, much
may be forgiven the Australian Natives’
•Association for its iine war record. By way
of proof that it does more than talk, the
Association has published a list showing that
3040 of its members had joined the colors up
to November 20, and that three of them—
William Dunstan, Ballarat East; W. J.
Symons, Brunswick ; and Alexander Burton,
Euroa —had won the Victoria Cross. Appar-
ently A.N.A. really stands for Abdul’s
Numerous Antidote.
Harry Lawson, Victoria’s newest Attor-
ney-General, passed his exams, in 1911.
Donald Mackinnon, whom Lawson displaced,
was by a coincidence one of the law-
examiners who pencilled him for a pass.
Lawson represents Castleinaine and Maldon
in the Assembly and has been doing it for
sixteen years.

The “HYDRO”
After being Closed for Renovations during the Winter Season
Re-opened on December 1.
w
EDLOW BATH HYDRO, a Palace on a precipice at an altitude of
3500 feet, is but a stone’s throw from Med low Bath Railway-station, and
commands extensive views of the magnificent Kanimbla Valley. Ihe
“Hydro’s” appointments include a Casino, in which are provided frequent Entertain-
ments, a Library, an Art Gallery, Billiard and Smoking Rooms, Cloister Lounges, and
numerous semi-private alcoves which are all luxurious comfort personified. Outdoor
amusements are provided by means of Bowling Green, Tennis Court, Croquet Lawn,
Golf Links, Cricket Ground and Rifle Range. The “Hydro” is Electrically lit and
is provided with a Hot-water Installation. The cuisine is unquestionable
and the attendants are courteous. Private Garage available for visitors.
Tariff from 16s. per day, or £47/6 per week.
Ita!a Cars leave the Hydro daily for the Jenolan Caves, or for Excursions to other places
of interest, and private cars may be arranged for on application at the Hotel or at the
City Booking Office: 42 Castlereagh Street, Sydney.

Government Notices.
Department of the Navy,
Melbourne, 2nd December, 1915.
Appointment of Draughtsmen anti
Engineers to Proceed to England to
Undergo Course of Instruction in
Submarine Building.
APPLICATIONS are invited from per-
sons qualified and desirous of proceed-
ing to England to undergo a course of
instruction in Submarine Building, for a
period not exceeding two years.
2. The following Draughtsmen and En-
gineers are required, and the qualifications
for appointment are as shown: —
Posiiion.
1 Ship Draughtsman.
1 Engineer Draughtsman
1 Electrical . Draughts-
man.
1 Ship Fitter.
1 Engine Fitter.
1 Electrical Fitter.
Qualifications.
Good knowledge of me-
chanics required.
Special knowledge of
Diesel and similar
prime movers.
Must have had a sound
training.
Good knowledge of his
trade and a special
aptitude for mechan-
isms.
Good knowledge of ma-
chinery and practical
knowledge of engine-
room practice.
Good knowledge of elec-
trical work.
3. Applicants must be Australian born or
permanently domiciled in Australia, and
should not have passed their 30th birthday
on 15th January, 1916.
4. Salary (minimum) £250 per annum.
A separation allowance of £1 per week will
be paid to families of married officers
selected.
5. Selected candidates will be required to
enter into an undertaking to serve the Com-
monwealth Government for a period of at
least five years after their return to Aus-
tralia.
6. Applications, accompanied by copies of
testimonials and evidence as to age, should
be forwarded to the Naval Secretary, De-
partment of the Navy, Melbourne, so as to
reach him not later than 15th January,
1916.
GEO. L. MACANDIE,
Naval Secretary.

How Headaches Start.
Headaches may be started by a hundred
and one derangements of the health, but
the actual cause is often impure blood. The
nerves of the head are the most sensitive
of the entire nervous system. Like all the
nerves of the body they are dependent upon
pure blood for their health. They are affected
by any derangement of the system that
throws impurities into the blood. Sufferers
from headaches will find that their health
is run down or that their digestion is out
of order or that there is some other cause
why the blood is impure. It is only natural
then that the nerves should complain and
that the head should ache and throb.
The most dangerous practice that sufferers
from headache can fall into is the taking
of drugs that merely deaden the pain. As
time goes on the patient finds that she has
to increase the dose to get the same effect;
and later many become drug slaves.
The correct treatment aims at eliminat-
ing the cause of headaches. Such a blood-
making tonic as Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills
can be recommended to every headache suf-
ferer. In building up the blood, they not
only strengthen and tone up the system, but
nourish and free the nerves from pain. The
pills are free from all harmful or habit-
forming drugs which are so common in
headache remedies. Your nearest dealer in
medicine can supply you.
j Free —A useful booklet, “Diseases of the
Blood,” will be sent on application to Dr.
Williams’ Medicine Co., Sydney.—Anvx.
How to Avoid Sick Headache
Many people suffer at regular intervals
from sick headache. Generally they can tell
hours in advance when an attack is coming
on. The trouble is caused by accumulation
of poison in the digestive organs, and when
a certain point is reached this poison pro-
duces nausea and the attack follows.
If sufferers would take a dose of Pink-
ettes, the new laxative, at intervals, these
distressing attacks may be avoided. I’ink-
ettes are dainty litle. sugar-coated ovals
tlmt arouse no feeling of repulsion, and they
act so gently that they are the ideal laxa-
tive to use in correcting a tendency to sick
headache. They have no unpleasant effect
to make them objectionable to the extra-
sensitive sick-headache patient. Old-
fashioned. harsh purgatives will not do at
all in such cases. If you would like to try
them send for a free sample to I he Dr.
Williams’ Medicine Co., Sydney, mentioning
that you read of them in Tiie Bulletin.
Sold everywhere, Is. per bottle. — Advt.

The Inky Way.
From an officer of the 2nd Field Artilleiy
Brigade :—• , , v „
As showing the .imperturbability of the Austra la
soldier, this might interest you. I recently crossed
from Alexandria on the ship that picked up a giea
many men from the water after the torpedoing o
the Southland, which was carrying a full load ot
Australian Infantry on their way to the Front. We
were one of the first ships to arrive at the scene.
The; sea was full of boats and rafts, many of them
very much overcrowded and almost sinking. One
raft in particular was in a very bad way, being
overturned, and far too many men were hanging to it
and sitting on the bottom. It was fortunately very
calm, but still the water was washing over the men.
As we neared them I was amazed to see one chap
reading Tub Bulletin ; he was sitting on Ins
haunches, elbows on knees, and enjoying it
thoroughly, in spite of the fact that they had been
two hours in the water and all were pretty ex-
hausted. I discovered later that he had been reading
it aloud to his pals to cheer them up. They needed
it, poor beggars! The paper was in his pocket
when he went overboard, and the ready-witted chap
made fine use of it.

“Copy Boy”: What is the record long-
partnership on the Australian inky way .’ 1
nominate that of Johnny Watson and A. b.
Laurie, owners of the Bobdek Watch, pub-
lished at Mount Gambier (S.A.). Laurie
set the Watch going in ISlil and two years
later he was joined by M atson. Altei •>•»
years the partnership is still prosperous and
healthy. Laurie is now 73, while Watson
who was the first Mayor of Mount (xanibiei,
but otherwise is of unblemished character—is
a bright boy of 81. # #
The first number of the British Empire
Review (Australasian edition) has been
published. One line: “In these stirring
times our thoughts constantly turn to our
sovereign.” Things don’t look too good.
* * * *
John Sandes ends the argument:—
Allow me to remark with a blush that I was the
writer of the lines which attempted, very inade-
quately, to depict the appearance of the late P. K.
McOaiighan as Lafayette at a fancy ball given at
Government House, Melbourne, by the John Maddens
in 1897, and which formed the subject of an action
for libel against the Argus, the aggrieved party
claiming damages on the ground that he had been
held up to contempt and ridicule. The case was
decided by a jury of 12, presided over by Justice
Hodges; Isaacs and Duffy for the plaintiff, Purves
and Coldham for the defendants. I was in court
ready to swear that the verses were, a bona fide repoit
of the plaintiff’s appearance and that there was no
malice. But I was not called. I had been on the
most friendly terms with plaintiff, and only a
few days before the fatal fancy ball he insisted on
introducing me over a cup of coffee in the V ienna
Cafe to David Syme, remarking genially, “This
young fellow is in the same line of business as
yourself, Syme. He writes for the paper up the
street.” The libel action was chiefly interesting
from a legal aspect in bringing out the essential
distinction between a well-known character and a
public man. I gathered that in law, no matter how
well known a man may be in a community by reason
of his strongly-marked personality or quaintness of
attire, he is entitled to be free from all comment
in the press. If the plaintiff had been even a mem-
ber of the City Council he might have been depicted
in verse with impunity, so long as there was no
malice. A useful point that for pressmen. It was
during the hearing of the case that I first realised
what a highly-trained legal mind . can do in the
matter of interpreting any document. Barrister
Isaacs read two of the verses —supposed to be the
report of the constable on duty outside the ballroom.
In them this: —
An’ Ceres fled when he loomed up near,
An’ Hebe declined to stay,
And pretty Minerva, wid helm and spear,
Grew faint when he came her way.
Learned counsel proceeded to draw the blackest
deductions as to the sinister insinuations which in
ins opinion the writer sought to convey to the public
concerning the moral character of the plaintiff, as
evidenced by the suggestion that the ladies all fled
in terror at his approach. Makes one think of the
dark meanings imported into “chops and tomato
sauce” in “Bardell versus Pickwick.” The jury
evidently thought that there was something in the
point put forward by counsel’s interpretative acumen,
for they gave plaintiff a verdict —and £5 out of
the £5OOO that he asked for. Dear me, it all
happened 18 years ago! Purves and Coldham and
McCaughan are dead, and even Isaacs and Duffy have
ascended on high.

Ross's Magazine, a little monthly, has
just put its fist round the corner. “Bob”
Ross, its founder, is mostly list, and has
used it in more fighting-papers in Australia
than any other man of his age. He lias
been in the ring in Queensland, Broken Hill,
Maoriland and Melbourne, and at present
does the slogging for the Socialist. Like
most of the cult he is a mild-mannered
fiend, who honestly regards the capitalist as
the only really fierce and bloodthirsty citizen.

“Lou” : Years ago the old Charters Towers
Telegraph ran a matrimonial column, and
every week a batch -of short letters, from
either sex, stating requirements and so forth
made the rag a thing of joy. You could
arrange a “meet” somewhere, get the lady’s
or gent.’s address, or be intro’ed at the office.
The letters were printed as written, and out
in the camp we used to muster round the
cook-house at night and argue about the
goeds on offer. We married them all off
each week except one. His letter we
tacked on the bread-box lid, and here it is :
I’m a tin scratcher, an’ a pretty decent sort of a
cove as coves go. I’ve got a nice little pyramid o
tin waitin’ ’ore, an’ I wanter be put on the tracks
of a good sort of an upstandin’ twist an’ twirl. One
who doan mind goin’ for a karasene tin of mother
an’ daughter an’ boiling the knock me silly while
I banjo the dirt. Face looks doan matter much —■
no questions asked by neither. —(Signed) Metel’ead.
As fur as we could learn, “Metel’ead” was
never suited.
THE “IN MEMORIAM” COLUMN
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
In childhood, ere my soul divined
The shade of the approaching hearse,
I dreamed my deeds might be enshrined
In deathless verse.
A sub. whose eyes have lost their fire,
Memorial verse that staggereth
I daily scan, and most desire
A verseless death.
VICTORIA.
