THE RAPID HEALER
BEFORE THE MIRROR.
By “JEANNETTE.”
“Jeannette,” the well-known writer on Beauty Culture and author of “The Book of Beauty,”
will be pleased to help and advise any reader on matters of the toilet. Queries should be acconi-
panied by a stamped addressed envelope, and directed to “Jeannette,” c/o “Before the Mirror,
43 Gray’s Inn Read, London, W.C., England.
These are undoubtedly days of exceptional nervous strain for one and all of
us. Anxiety, with its beauty-destroying influence, is casting its shadow over tne
lives of manv of us women, and while, perhaps, a few—the more stoical among
us—can avoid worrying over past or prospective events, we may all, with a little
care and forethought, succeed in preventing the result of worry from being notice-
able in our appearance. To these fortunate few this little chat will, however, be
as interesting and instructive as to those who stand in real need of help in toilet
matters, and may be the means of bringing to their notice some hitherto unknown
method of retaining, or regaining, that beauty of face and figure which is the bnth-
right of every woman. Some of the ingredients mentioned below are at present
not generally known to the public, but any good chemist will usually be found to
have a small quantity in stock.
Freckles and How to Cure Them.—lt is always
those with the finest and fairest skins who
freckle most easily, but this knowledge is poor
comfort when the face is disfigured with these
ugly little blemishes. To prevent the forma-
tion of freckles, a wash should be applied to
the face which will form a shield from the
rays of the sun. A simple and inexpensive
one can he made up at home by dissolving an
ounce of cleminite in four tablespoonfuls of
hot water. Shake the bottle, and when cold
apply to the face and allow to dry on the
skin. If this is done every day, renewing the
application whenever the face is washed, and a
little mercolised wax rubbed into the skin
every night, freckles will not make their
appearance, no matter how hot the sun. To
remove freckles which have already formed,
apply the wax every night, rub it well into
the skin of the face and neck and leave it on
till the morning. Then wash off, using Pilenta
soap and warm water.
The Hair —To Increase the Growth. —To arrest
an undue falling of the hair and increase the
growth, scalp massage and the application of
a good tonic is absolutely necessary. Part the
hair in the centre, and starting at the forehead
massage for at least ten minutes. Then apply
a tonic. A simple and inexpensive one can be
made up at home by mixing one ounce of
boranium (which can be bought at the chem-
ist’s) with a quarter of a pint of bay rum.
Add the boranium to the bay rum, allow to
stand for half an hour, then strain and add
sufficient water to make half a pint. Dab
amongst the roots of the hair with a soft
sponge.
Blackheads, Enlarged Pores, etc.—Pdacklieads
are unusually troublesome things during the
summer months to those bothered in that way.
The extra warmth makes the skin “give,” and
it looks more greasy than usual, and conse-
quently the blackheads are more noticeable.
To remove them and close up the pores of the
skin dissolve a stymol tablet in a glass of hot
water, allow the effervescence to subside, then
dip a soft sponge into the liquid and bathe
the face. After a few minutes, dry the face
with a soft towel, and the blackheads will be
instantly removed.
To Reduce a Double Chin.—To reduce a double
chin, give hard, firm massage every night with
mercolised was;, stroking the flesh firmly back,
wards from the chin towards the lower portion
of the neck. Do this every night, and in the
morning bathe with cold water and rub into
the skin a little parsidium jelly, fins treat-
ment will strengthen the flabby tissues and
restore the contour of the face. Occasionally
a double chin is due to stooping over books 01
work, but generally it is the result of putting
on weight. When the latter is the case a tew
clynol berries, eaten regularly every day will
greatly assist in reducing the superfluous
flesli. A glass of hot water should be sipped
after each meal.
Superfluous Hair.—Dark-complexioned women
are far more frequently troubled with a
growth of superfluous hair than those with
fair skins, and at the first sign of these objec-
tionable hairs steps should be taken to remove
them, or tliev will develop into a strong
growth which will lake a very long time to
destroy. A little powdered pheminol applied
to this growth will remove the unsightly blem-
ish, destroving it permanently if the hairs be
but few. Two or more applications may be
necessary if the growth be unusually strong,
but about one ounce of pheminol should be
sufficient for the most stubborn case. Phemi-
nol can be obtained at the chemist’s, and a
little powdered alum should be obtained at the
same time for dusting the skin before applying
the pheminol.
Grey or Fading Hair. —To restore the color to
grey or fading hair add one ounce of concen-
trate of tammalite to four ounces of bay rum.
Apply to the roots with a soft sponge every
night until the desired result is obtained, then
use the lotion two or three times a week. After
three or four days a decided improvement will
be noticed, the hair turning gradually to its
original color. An iron tonic will be found
most beneficial when the greyness is caused by
general debility.
Beautiful Hair—How to Shampoo.—Whether
the hair is luxuriant and glossy, or thin and
lanky depends very largely upon the care
bestowed upon it. To keep the hair in good
condition it should be shampooed once in
every two or three weeks. Before shampooing,
massage a little pure olive oil or almond oil
into the scalp, then mix one teaspoonful of
stallax granules in a cup of hot water, fill a
basin with warm water, into which the ends
of the hair can fall, and shampoo the stallax
mixture into the scalp in the usual way.
ItinsQ, and dry by fanning with a palm fan, or
in the open air. if the weather permits. Should
the hair be naturally very greasy, the oil
massage should lie omitted. [3
The Elopement.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
it /TY heart is mostly conger cel.
/\/§ And all my world is wet;
± tJL But while it wriggles 1 can feel
That stars arise and set —-
Perchance my lady at the wheel
Has dimples still to let.
The dandelions of my hope
Around me drooping lay.
As stairs made slithersome with soap
My path seemed swift and grey,
Therefore 1 could not well elope
With Madeline that day.
I sprawled upon the grass and moaned.
I heard the dull clocks chime.
The slim and withered purse I owned
Weighed on my soul like crime,
For that elopement w..s postponed
The twenty-second time.
The thin, delicious girl whose nice
Young heart I boldly won.
Whose lips were like a curving slice
Of beetroot in the sun,
Was ready twenty times and twice
With me to cut and run.
Her parent with the stern, large boot,
A tallow merchant strong,
Was guessless of the game afoot
Or how the hunt went wrong;
His soul was rank with lard and loot
As mine was sweet with song.
Elopement cannot be financed
As once it used to be,
When Lochinvar’s tall charger pranced
His lady home to tea ;
All love affairs are no”' ’•omarepd
In terms of £ s. d.
One toot upon the motor horn,
One stirring taxi ride,
And. spite of life and death and scorn,
The girl had been my bride —-
A young star of the evening born
Laughed till he shook inside.
I said : “Yeung star, is this the time
To make a mock of me?
As cashless as a cow you climb
Out of the dark, salt sea,
While poverty like gulfs of slime
Is ladled over me. - ’
The small star said : “You must excuse
My mirth on such a night.
It is a shame that you should lose
An hour so sweet for flight.
Pray take my car, or, if you choose,
I’ll drive you to delight.”
Swift as a German shell he sped
Across the seascape wide,
And circling neatly overhead
Completed a long glide
By landing steady as a bed
Three inches from my side.
“Jump in.” His voice was low and sweet,
Like starshine on the sea.
llis hair was gold, his waist was neat,
His lips were red and free;
Amazement made my wild heart beat
To find that he was she.
Uprising like a whirring bird,
We climbed against the sky.
A laughter in her white throat stirred —-
“Now, whither shall we fly?”
I gave the right address. She heard,
Leaned back and seemed to sigh.
I saw her look. I understood.
My reason all went blind.
I murmured : “Would you be so good
Some other road to find?
Pray do not think me mad or rude,
I’ve simply changed my mind.”
The bright star lady smiled like spring,
She set the car a-whirl,
I felt it flutter, soar and swing
Round in a corkscrew curl,
And then I simply had to fling
My arms around the girl.
My Madeline was mad as cats,
I felt it as I fled,
Mad as a dame with six new hats
Who has to stay in bed
Because a cold that gnaws like rats
Is sporting in her head.
The pleasant lady of the star
Was kind as kind could be;
We sailed the dimpled darkness far
Beyond the nearer sea,
Till at the moon’s bright fringe the car
Was but a kiss and me.
I cared not where our wheels might turn
Or where our wings might float;
What leagues of darkness we might spurn
I did not mark or note;
I felt a soft mouth breathe and burn
Above a clear, white throat.
I said: “Dear girl, I do not know
How far you mean to drive.
But I should like to take it slow,
Lest morning should arrive.”
She said : “One kiss before we go,
The- hour has come to dive !”
She pulled a lever as she spoke,
A white light filled the sky,
It was the pearly dawn that broke,
And we were up so high
It took the. breath out of a bloke
Who was not used to fly.
We fell a hundred miles or so
In one tremendous drop.
I saw a vast grey ocean flow
With wind-whipped waves on top,
And then the whole ethereal show
Went one eternal flop.
Flop! And the girl and car were gone.
Half-drowned. I climbed the sea.
I reached the air. the bright sun shone,
And it was plain to me
The boots and clothes that I had on
Were heavy as could be.
I needs must swim a thousand miles
To come to any shore;
I thought about the star girl s wiles,
And those red lips she wore ;
And then odd waves in various styles
Tossed me about some more.
The depth of water where I drowned
Was quite five hundred feet.
There were no rocks or islands round
On which to take a seat,
But some small fishes came and found
That I was good to eat.
My Madeline was mad as cats,
I knew it all the while,
I knew it when those hungry sprats
Came round and tried to smile,
And when the lobster brought her brats
To chew me up in style.
My heart is mostly conger eel,
And all my world is wet,
But while it wriggles I can feel
That stars arise and set —
Perchance, my lady at the wheel
Has dimples still to let.

CURSE O' MOSES.
The Last Shaft.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I DON’T feel lonely now as I sit writ-
ing by night in the “front room” of
the old home by Log Paddock. I used
to feel very, very lonely here as a boy,
thirty-odd years ago, when they were all
alive and around me. The Bush seemed
haunted then by a Future, which is dead.
I've got in touch with the world since that
time. Besides, two old digger-mates, Bill
Payne and Harry McDermott, who have
batched in the old house for years, are smok-
ing their pipes in reflective silence, one on
each side of the big kitchen fireplace, after
a hard day’s battling with the water m a
wet shaft (thinly disguised as a contract
well) that they are trying to bottom at the
other end of Log Paddock. (This end has
been “this end” from time immemorial.)
Moreover, “Young Jack” McDermott —
he’s about thirty-three—-will be home soon
from an Irish-German wedding down at
Muller’s farm, with a ridiculous and grossly-
exaggerated account of the whole business,
and a warmly-colored and higlily-improper
description of the young couple’s immediate
future. They have to be all night in the
train in wintry weather. Also, he’ll bring a
bottle of good Weinsberg port-wine for me
from a vineyard across “the crick.”
* * *
.Tack has come home with a mate of Ger-
man descent; and I can hear him taking
his father to task about the way the Old Man
brought him up. .Tack has the same affection
and regard for his father as Beniio had for
his “old ’uns” ; but Jack has a different way
of showing it. However, he gets little change
from the Old Man.
* * *
Near midnight. The rain has cleared
away, and it is bright moonlight. Log Pad-
dock, opposite—which isn’t a log paddock,
and hasn’t been one since the writer’s child-
hood—looks strangely open. I had forgotten
to notice that the bush has been cleared
away (for the second time since convict
days), and only a few shade trees left for
the immortal Cow. For this, from being
first a farming and vine-growing, then a
mining, and, next, a wheat district, has
become mostly cow-cocky. This will help
you to understand some things.
The log fence —I remember parts of it —-
was composed of two long logs laid side by
side on the ground, with chocks across, and
other two logs on top of them, and so on.
There isn’t a log of it left now, and hasn’t
been for many a long year. They all went
for timbering shafts, for post-and-rail fences
and firewood. Then there was a stout two-rail
fence—l saw it put up. Now that is gone,
and Log Paddock is surrounded by the eter-
nal wire-netting, with two barbed wires on
top. For Bunny had triumphed here. But
barbed wire was a blessed relief from the
eternal, bullocking “billeting.” Every curse
brings an antidotal blessing. And poor
people make a living out of the rabbits.
At least, they are locally and tolerantly
referred to as “the poor people.” Some of
the “poor” people average £4 a week, and
over. My philanthropic friend, whose heart
bleeds for the rabbiter, averages thirty
shillings ; and he averages it under protest.
Talking of fences. I’m always expecting
a fence where there’s none now. Round the
old house is a very old two-rail, and along
the road frontage a barbed wire with an
old post here and there mortised for .two
rails; sometimes several of these posts in a
row—as in front of the house. They seem,
in the moonlight, like passing ghosts that
pause momentarily and stare. I didn’t know
why they did it to me until it struck me
they were the remains of a fence that I put
xip," as a boy. more than thirty years ago.
But good timber had long been scarce even
then, and the post and rails had belonged
to another fence.
I’d like to have a portrait of that boy as
he was then. Those old grey posts haunt me
in their old grey way with thoughts and
fancies of another boy whom I saw recently
in Sydney. An old grey aunt said this
young fellow was the dead spit of me as I
was at his age. She thought she’d gone back
thirty years—or I’d come on. He is my
son Jim.
(Never mind. We’re coming* to the Last
Shaft in Log Paddock soon.)
Dawn breaking above Golden Gully. Then,
slowly, the sunrise over old Mount Bucka-
roo purples all along the Mudgee Hills. The
old German vineyards are flushed above the
slopes beyond the dark-green slieoaks across
Pipeclay Creek. But they are greatly
changed. They have long got away from the
old, cast-iron, immigrant-German idea that
handicapped two generations—the immigrant
German generation and the first Australian-
Germans; the fixed idea, as an idea can only
be fixed in Teuton minds, that vines would
grow nowhere but on a sunny slope. Nowa-
days the best are grown on the levels above,
and some even on the creek flats. The
(Continued on next page.)

slopes, from which the old, old vines were
uprooted, are bare and “scalded,” and will
probably grow nothing for years. Very
modern houses and cellars have taken the
places of the old, German, snake-infested
ones. They say that snakes have grown
scarce in the district. Perhaps Laughing
Jack could explain, since lie’s been protected.
But snakes come out of some of those cellars
yet. I know, because I’ve been bitten by one.
There’s the enlarged portrait of a young
man on the wall, amongst old prints that
used to belong to us. A young man with a
full face, roguish eyes and a dark moustache
rather pointed and turned up at the corners.
I knew him well. He was full of whimsical
humor and practical jokes of a harmless
sort. He first appeared, in my boyish mem-
ory, at the hack door while the Chieftain-
ess was entertaining a German lady-friend
of the old school and a severe-looking school-
mistress—childless, and of another old
school. He was then sinking a shaft with a
party, of whom my father was one, on the
original two-acre freehold, just behind the
house. His name was Harry McDermott.
He married and came with his young
wife to live in the old house the day we
were leaving.
On my last visit, twenty-odd years ago, he
was little changed. (On that occasion, by
the way, I took his two boys up to Con
O’Donnell’s to buy lollies. One of the boys
was Jack.) Harry was expecting to strike
it rich in a shaft in Log Paddock. He said
at the dinner-table that if I, in my travels
abroad, should happen to come across a
party of three —a jolly, good-looking
fellow like himself; a sour, discontented,
jealous-looking old woman; and a pretty,
soulful, golden-haired, happy young woman
—-I’d know at once that it was him and his
wife and the children’s governess, and that
he’d struck his golden hole at last and was
making a grand tour of the world.
Out in the kitchen an old man is getting
an early breakfast ready at the big, open
fireplace. At least, he has the appearance
of an old man. Ilis hair —what is left of
it—is as white as his beard. He is bent,
and, when he walks, liis legs, seen from the
rear, are shrunken and “gone.” His eye-
lids have dropped and his eyes are half-shut
most of the time. He seldom smiles, and
only gives a grunt of a laugh now and
again; but in one of the few brighter
moments he opens his eyes, and a ghost of
the old whimsical manner comes back. For
his name is Harry McDermott. (The Last
Shaft is very near now.)
He lost his wife ten years ago, ard he
and his mate have batched in the old house
nearly ever since. Ilis children have grown
up and drifted off. All except “Young
Jack.” Everything in the old home is
almost as the wife and mother left it—save,
of course, in the matter of window curtains
and little woman’s things like chat. The
front bedroom is never used. He sleeps on a
bunk in the kitchen, and his mate and Jack
in the “boys’ room” between. (I used to be
one of “the boys.”) The big kitchen,
dresser is as it was, always kept clean and
tidy, as only old diggers amongst landsmen
keep things. Of the astonishingly complete
crockery outfit scarce a dish has been broken.
> Harry McDermott and his mate, Billy
Payne, live by well-sinking and prospect-
ing, and getting a bit of gold now and then.
But the shallows and the dry ground have
long been worked out; for nearly all Log
Paddock, when you get down a little, is a
subterranean lake. They are sinking a well
for a cow-cocky amongst the ancient work-
ings at the upper end of Log Paddock —
with an eye to a “bit o’ gold” at the
“bottom,” if they can reach it.
People say they shall never bottom that
shaft—or well, if you like it better. But
they sink in the mullock, and they bail, and
timber, and sink in the mullock, and bail,
and timber, battling bravely down. It is
sawn slabs now instead of split, with cleats
at the corners instead of pegs. It looks as
neat as if the shaft were bricked. But they
can’t keep the water out. There was a little
water “follerin’ ’em down” last week.
There’s Gft. now. It’s no use bailing on
Sunday, as of old ; for the same depth will
be there on Monday morning.
Young Jack is going to “knock off for a
week” (he’s fencing) and give them a hand
to help bottom that shaft. But he knows the
case is hopeless. It would need a steam-
pump. and all the gold they are likely to get
wouldn’t pay for the engine-oil. Oh, the
lifelong faith of the gold-digger!
Billy starts off first in the morning, while
Harry cuts the dinner and puts it in the
tucker-bag. Then Harry starts, with the
dog after him. Going up the road, there is
always from a quarter to half a mile between
the mates. And in like manner shall they
come home in the evening. Only then the dog-
will be in the lead. They were known to
walk together once—when there was trouble
at home. I wonder if they’ll walk home
together if they strike gold? They’re about
the only two old professional diggers left in
the district.
And they’ll stick to that shaft, and dig
out the mullock, and put in the slabs, and
light the water as sailors fight it in the hold
of a stricken ship. For they are fighting
for something which is dearer to an old
digger than his life—they are fighting for
Independence in their old age! No old
digger looks forward to living with a
married son or daughter in the city—or with
a daughter-in-law or son-in-law —when he is
“worked out.”
And I’ll always have a vision of an old
grey digger, at (he bottom of a wet shaft,
working by the dim light of the candle in its
socket, stuck in the side —-working at the
slabs in his spectacles! Harry McDermott’s
eyes have failed him.
Now. I suppose you’ve already forgotten
the jolly young digger—and the wife—and
the governess?
* * * *
So we old writers go timbering up the
walls of our ruined lives, and sinking
through the mullock of the Present, and
bailing out, and delving in the drift and the
wash for the gold of human nature and the
best part of the Past. And we very seldom
find them.
HENRY LAWSON.
The Troupe in Cowland.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
COWLAND at last!
Before leaving Brisbane, Benson,
our comedian, had dilated upon the
treat in store.
“My boy,” he enthused, “you'll have the
tour of your life. The scenery along the
rivers is—is” He paused, searching for
a superlative. “Well, to drift up those
rivers”
We did drift. The hyacinth grew to a
height of three feet. It was “so thick Mick
Grogan’s cow walked aerost one day.”
Though a city company with a widely-
known success allegedly of the bush, we
failed to allay the natural suspicion with
which “the troupe” is regarded.
“Yairs. _ They was rooms reserved, but
some blokes come in from Outback,” Bung in
shirt sleeves casually remarked. So the
hungry troupe invaded Pine Grove boarding-
house. Two anaemic sausages reposing on a
plethora of plate failed to appease the mum-
mers’ craving. A demand for reinforcements
brought the response that, “We on’y got two
pounds, and they run ter two each.”
A gentleman who invaded the ladies’.
dressing-room that night, while they were
on the stage, used his perception to such
advantage that £2O changed hands. After
a long search we unearthed the hibernating
John Hop in a back parlor. He reckoned it
a of a joke.
The caretaker’s conviction that we
“wouldn’t git much of a ’earing ter-night on
account of ther darnce larst week” was
upheld.
After the play a large voice, forcing a
passage through a tangled array of whisker,
informed us that the owner possessed 150
cows, while a horny index finger sketched
imaginary Ayrshires on my lapel.
"I suppose you have a milking machine?”
I asked.
“Well, yer see,” he drawled, “it’d cost
two hundred and thirty quid altogether.”
“How do you get on for labor?”
“Orlright. I got eight kids!”
Rumors of a Russian victory having fil-
tered through to Milky Plains, we interviewed
the local paper for confirmation. The editor,
still charged with the electric atmosphere
of the Council Chambers, woke with a start
at the troupe’s intrusion. He dismissed us
with languid courtesy, said that he knew
nothing whatsoever, and advised us to try
the pub, which apparently imported rumors
with the beer. After the fourth, we found
them to be true in every respect.
Our reception, so far, was scant in cour-
tesy and abundant in disfavor. The expla-
nation of Cowland apathy for a city
dramatic success came with the dawn at
Jersey Hollow.
“Hey, Bill, get up!”
“Whasser time?” Bill cloudily inquired.
“Arf parst five.”
“D’.ver go to the play larst night?”
“Yairs.”
“ ’Ow was it?”
Jim spat meditatively. “Too exag’erated.
Course, some of it makes yer laugh. But it
slings off at the cookies.”
There was menace in Jim’s voice. He
spat again. “City blokes ’d like it. There’s
a flash cow in it wot acts the son. I’d like
to ’ave ’im out at Brindle Creek clearin’
forty acres. .. .It’s too exag’erated.”
Bill sat up in bed, and his eyes fell on
the paddock opposite. “Frorst this mornin’,”
he drawled.
They went to the slavery of the bails still
cursing the “exag’eration” of the stage-
cocky’s son.
At the next town the caretaker’s pre-
diction that we’d “have a pore ’ouse on
account o’ the church bazaar larst month”
was fulfilled.
Sunday we had condensed milk. Bill Jim
enlightened us. “Yer see, we takes ther
cream, an’ ther pigs gets ther skim milk.”
He considered this merely justice. No
smoked actor could sell at Is. 6d. a lb.
The method of applauding was new to
us. At the end of each act a peculiar
driving yell—a cross between the mopoke’s
moop and the crow’s quark—expressed the
local sentiment. The caretaker volunteered
the information that “they was encores.”
Our luck varied with the importance of
the cow and the accompanying deadness of
the popular intelligence.
At Ayrshire Flat the caretaker’s pro-
phecy that “they wouldn’t turn up much ter-
night on account av Jim Dorgan gettin’
buried larst week” came to pass.
One night while regretfully watching the
frothy subsidence of his third, the comedian
had a bright idea.:: “We’ll write an Austra-
lian play on Maeterlinck lines,” he said.
He admired the amber effect against the
acetylene. “They’ve had the ‘Blue Bird’ in
the city. We’ll write a play for these parts
called ‘The Brindle Cow’!” .
An electric silence struck the bar. Several
cow-Kaisers paused with beer aloft.
“What’ll your hero be?” I asked.
“Manager of the butter factory, of
course.”
“And the villain?”
“Oh,” with emphasis, “the villain’s the
school inspector!”
The soft churning of the screw swilled
tlie water to a milky wake. The ever widen-
ing ripples swayed the tall bulrushes....
the shadows deepened in the tangled under-
growth. . . . the lowing of the distant herd
.... the pleasant hum of the butter-factory
engine gently breaking the silence that comes
with the evening. Ahead, the glory of a
western sky reflected in the river’s mirrored
surface. A moving bank of dun, fleeting
across a field of jersey yellow, crashing
against the strawberry rays that burst the
sullen shadows with Ayrshire red. .. .Every-
where peace. Sydney to-morrow! Thank
God!
RICHARD BELLAIRS.
